INDIA
KERALA AGRICUTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        Agriculture in Kerala, as in most other States in India, is the
mainstay of its economy, accounting for about 58% of State income. Tree
crops mainly coconut, cashew and rubber and pepper cover more than 70% of
the area under cultivation. Kerala is the largest producer of these crops
in India. Yields are much below potential and are declining due to lack of
attention to their development. The large number of smallholdings is a
significant feature of the agrarian structure in the State, and a continued
decline in productivity would adversely affect the lives of most rural
families.
ii.       This project would have as its main objective the improvement in
productivity of major foreign exchange earning tree crops and pepper, with
emphasis on improving the economic status of the smallholder farmer. It
would cover new planting and rehabilitation of coconut with systematic inter-
cropping of food and other crops; pepper rehabilitation; cashew development;
establishment of seed gardens; and processing of smallholder rubber. Sup-
port to smallholders for agricultural development is offered mainly in the
form of long-term institutional credit and special extension services. Other
support services included are research, technical assistance and training.
iii.      Some 75,000 families in six out of the eleven Districts in Kerala
would participate. The project is expected to increase output on 45,000 ha
of smallholdings - 5,000 ha new coconut planting, 30,000 ha coconut reha-
bilitation and 10,000 ha pepper. About 8,500 ha of the coconut areas would
be irrigated from ground water permitting more intensive intercropping with
fodder grass and cocoa. At full production incremental output would be about
200 million coconuts (18,000 tons coconut oil equivalent), 6,000 tons pepper,
15,000 tons milk, 2,000 tons cocoa, 6,000 tons cashew and 37,000 tons of other
food crops. Rubber processing facilities, with ten small crumb rubber
factories, would not increase quantity but improve the quality of rubber
production by upgrading about 25,000 tons of low grade rubber produced by
50,000 smallholders into higher value technically specified rubber.
iv.       Seven year project costs would total about US$69 million (M)
equivalent. The foreign exchange component would be about 15% or US$10 M.
The proposed IDA Credit of US$30 M would cover 44% of total project costs
(or about 48% excluding duties and taxes estimated at US$7 M). Of the remain-
ing 56% (US$39 M), 10% would be financed by Central and Kerala State govern-
ments and 39% by the Agricultural Refinance and Development Corporation
(ARDC), lending banks and other participating agencies; and 7% would come
from smallholders' own resources.



- ii -
v.        The proposed IDA Credit includes US$20.7 M intended for long-term
lending for smallholder development (US$17.3 14), cashew development (US$1.0 M)
and crumb rubber processing (US$2.4 M). This would be made available to
project beneficiaries through the Cooperative and Commercial banking systems.
The granting of loans to beneficiaries would be planned and supervised by the
ARDC which would also undertake related refinancing. Smallholders and other
participants would contribute between 5% and 15% of their investments, bor-
rowing the remainder from the lending banks. Loans would be for up to fifteen
years, including a three to seven year grace period. Interest would be at
11%, the rate now applied to most agricultural schemes sponsored through
government banks.
vi.       Equipment for the nine new crumb rubber factories (US$2.2 M) would
be procured by international competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance with IDA
Guidelines. Equipment for the Palai crumb rubber factory, farm equipment,
vehicles, laboratory and office equipment (US$3.1 M) would also be awarded
under ICB except when contracts could not be bulked at US$100,000 or more,
when they would be awarded in accordance with local procurement procedures
in which competition is assured by adequate representation of local suppliers
and local agents of foreign suppliers. Local manufacturers would receive a
margin of preference of 15% on equipment or the prevailing customs duty if
lower. Engineering and technical advisory services (US$0.5 M) would be en-
gaged in accordance with IDA guidelines for selection of consultants. As is
usual in the case of agricultural credit operations, ICB procedures would
not be suitable for procurement financed by loans to smallholder farmers
(US$34.9 M) comprising farm development inputs; about 75,000 farmers would be
involved, individual amounts would be small and the operation would be spread
over seven years. On-farm development for cashew and seed gardens (US$1.1 M)
would be carried out by State agencies on force account. Contracts for civil
works (US$4.8 M), because of their small size and scattered locations, would
be let on the basis of competitive bidding, advertised locally and in accord-
ance with local procedures which are satisfactory. The balance of project
costs US$5.0 M (excluding contingencies) would consist of staff and related
expenditures not involving procurement.
vii.      Coconut and pepper area development by smallholders and establish-
ment of seed gardens would be controlled by a Special Agricultural Development
Unit established under the overall control of the Kerala Agricultural Produc-
tion Commissioner. This Unit would also be responsible for project extension
services. Cashew development would be implemented by the Kerala State Planta-
tions Corporation; crumb rubber processing by Cooperative Rubber Marketing
Societies under the supervision of the Indian Rubber Board, and agricultural
research by the Kerala Agricultural University and the Central Plantation
Crops Research Institute. A Project Coordination Committee, with represent-
ation from all participating agencies and governments,has been established
under the chairmanship of the Agricultural Production Commissioner. An Eva-
luation Unit reporting to the Agricultural Production Commissioner, would
also be set up to evaluate project performance and benefits.



- iii -
viii.     The project would permanently improve the income of some 75,000
rural households, the majority of whom would belong to the poorest segment
of the population. Annual income of a family rehabilitating 0.5 ha coconut
without irrigation would increase from the present Rs 1,500 (US$160) by al-
most 100%; a family with 1 ha coconut, under irrigation and intensive inter-
cropping, would enjoy an income increase of about 300%, from about Rs 3,000
to Rs 10,000 on completion of debt service in fourteen years. The financial
rates of return to farmers are estimated to range from 18% to 49%. The rate
of return to the economy based on projected world market price, is estimated
to be 30%. With a 10% decrease in the project benefits the rate of return
would be 26%, and a 10% increase in costs would give a return of 27%.
ix.       Risks inherent in this complex project were considered and con-
servative assumptions made in regard to project phasing, costs, yields and
commodity prices. Risks not covered by such conservative assumptions are
judged to be acceptable in view of the benefits which would accrue to a large
number of comparatively small farmers and to the economy as a whole.
X.        The project is suitable for an IDA Credit of US$30 M to Government
of India.






INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The project was prepared by the Government of Kerala (GOK) with the
assistance of an FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program mission which visited India in
the Spring of 1975. Assistance was also provided by the World Bank Resident
Mission staff in New Delhi and Bank headquarters staff. This report is based
on the findings of an IDA appraisal mission which visited India in May/June
1976, consisting of Mr S. Thillairajah, Miss I.C. Girardot (IDA), Messrs J.G.
Ohler, K.S. Krishnan and A. A. Campbell (Consultants).
II. BACKGROUND
General
2.01      Several features distinguish agriculture in Kerala from agriculture
in other parts of India. Firstly it represents an even greater share of GNP
in Kerala than in the rest of India, 58% against the national average of 45%.
Secondly the high density of population (at 550 persons/sq km it is three
times the national average) results in much smaller holding sizes than in
other States. Almost two-thirds of the holdings are less than 0.5 ha. Farm
holdings below 1 ha account for 82% by number but only 34% by area. Con-
versely holdings of 10 ha and above represent only 0.2% by number but 13% by
area. The result of the smaller holding size is a State per capita income
rather below the national average. A third distinguishing feature is the high
intensity of land use. Some 60% of the State area is under cultivation
compared with 45% in India as a whole and a great proportion of this is
either under permanent tree crops or is double cropped. Fourthly a much wider
variety of crops is grown in Kerala than in other parts of India, many of them
only found in Kerala or closely adjoining areas of neighboring States.
2.02      The distribution of crops within the State falls into well defined
zones with distinct characteristics. Three natural regions that run north-
south are recognizable.   The highland is mainly under tree crops such as tea,
coffee, cardamom and rubber. The lowland, where not under paddy, the most
important crop there, is under coconut, and the midland is under a combination
of a large number of perennial and annual crops, often intermixed with one
another - arecanut, coconut, cashew, rubber, rice, tapioca, ginger, banana and
vegetables.
A. Main Crops
2.03      Coconut is a traditional crop of Kerala.  In 1975-76 the area of
coconut was about 750,000 ha representing 25% of the total cropped area in the



-2-
State and about two-thirds of the total coconut area in India. Though basic-
ally a commercial crop, coconut is also grown as a garden crop by smallholders.
Apart from providing nuts for domestic consumption as food, Kerala produces
some 300,000 tons or 90% of the country's milling copra. 1/ Only 50% of this
is milled in the State, which nevertheless has more than 1,100 oil milling
establishments. The coir 2/ industry provides direct employment to more than
600,000 people, mostly women. The crop also produces 300,000 tons of coconut
shell used mainly as charcoal fuel.
2.04      Most smallholders follow traditional methods of cultivating co-
conut. For instance only about 10% of farmers apply fertilizer. The result
is that yields are low and declining. Currently they average 4,000 nuts/ha
against 6,000 nuts/ha in 1969/70 and over 10,000 nuts/ha from properly cul-
tivated trees. A 100% yield increase has been obtained from existing gardens
by adopting good cultural practices (fertilizer usage, spraying and intercul-
tivation). By introducing irrigation a further 50% increase has been obtained.
A contributory cause of declining yields is the spread of root wilt and leaf
rot diseases which now affect about one-third of the area under coconut. The
Kerala Department of Agriculture (KDOA) does free spraying for root wilt in
two major protective belts along the northern and southern borders of the
diseased areas. Spraying for the control of coconut leaf diseases is also
done by KDOA on 50,000 ha annually; it is planned to expand the program to
100,000 ha. A spraying charge is collected from the farmers. In view of the
importance of coconut cultivation to the national economy and its significance
for the farming community of Kerala, GOK is implementing several programs to
raise yields, since land suitable for new planting is limited. "Intensive
Package Programs" (see 2.12) are being implemented by KDOA covering 93,500 ha
- 187 units of 500 ha in various parts of the State. Special assistance is
provided by GOI for this program.
2.05      Pepper, also an export crop, is grown mostly in the submontane
tracts of the Western Ghats in South India; 98% or 25,000 tons, comes from
120,000 ha in Kerala. It is mainly grown by small farmers often in
mixed cultivation with arecanut, coffee and cardamom. Average yields are
low at 250 kg/ha compared with 1,000kg obtained from plantations in Indonesia.
This marked difference is attributed to the use of unimproved varieties,
senile vines, diseases (slow and quick wilt) and inadequate cultivation
practices. KDOA is multiplying pepper cuttings of superior varieties for
distribution to farmers at cost.
2.06      Cashew is one of India's major foreign exchange earners. The
country itself produces about 140,000 tons annually and imports a similar
amount for processing prior to export. The import of raw nuts, however,
has been declining, from 193,000 tons in 1972 to 116,000 tons in 1975 because
India's main suppliers, Mozambique and Tanzania, are developing their own
processing facilities. It will be necessary to expand Indian cashew produc-
tion substantially if export markets are not to be lost. Kerala alone pro-
duces about 118,000 tons of raw cashew annually, from about 105,000 ha, or
1/   Dried coconut kernel.
2/   Fiber taken from coconut husks.



some 84% of national production. Cashew yields in Kerala at 1.1 ton/ha,
although about double the yield in other parts of India, can be increased
substantially with improved management. Kerala therefore provides the best
prospect for increasing national production. Kerala is also the center of
the cashew processing industry. With 264 factories it has 92% of India's
processing capacity and provides employment to 125,000 workers. It accounts
for a similar proportion of the country's US$110 M exports of processed
cashew.
2.07      Rubber plantings in India increased by about 50% during the twelve
years ended 1975-76 to almost 225,000 ha, more than 90% of which is in Kerala.
About 50,000 ha of this is immature. Some 70,000 ha of the mature rubber has
reached the end of its economic life. Under the Fifth Five Year Plan, uneco-
nomic areas are to be replanted at 5,000 ha annually under the ongoing Rubber
Replanting Subsidy Scheme managed by the Indian Rubber Board (IRB). Though
rubber yields have improved from 365 kg/ha in 1961 to 760 kg/ha in 1975 there
is considerable room for improvement. 1/
B. Other Crops
2.08      Garden Crops:  these are mostly food crops for consumption and
for sale in the local markets. They include tapioca, 2/ ginger, fruits and
vegetables. Tapioca, second in importance to rice as a staple food, is
grown on about 318,000 ha in Kerala. The poorer sections of the population
depend largely on tapioca for food. Varieties cultivated take from six to
twelve months to mature, yielding an average of 18 tons/ha. Ginger is also a
valuable crop grown extensively in Kerala, producing annually over 26,000 tons
on about 12,000 ha. Fruits occupy some 180,000 ha making Kerala the largest
horticultural State in India.  Vegetables are grown in almost all parts of the
State. Pulses and groundnuts are grown in rotation with other crops in the
drier areas.
2.09      Cocoa is a relatively new crop to India.  Total area is estimated to
be 6,000 ha, most of it planted since 1970. The Central Plantation Crops Re-
search Institute (CPCRI), situated at Kasaragod in the north of Kerala (see
map), has carried out a considerable amount of experimentation on cocoa
intercropping - varieties, spacing, shade and husbandry required (2.13).
Results from experiments in other countries are borne out by experimental and
field results in South India - that cocoa grown under mature coconut at a
stand of 175 trees/ha can yield 700 kg/ha of dry beans at full maturity in
the ninth and tenth years. The soil and climatic conditions are also well
suited for cocoa cultivation.
1/   Malaysian yields are more than 1000 kg/ha.
2/   Also called cassava or manioc.



-4-
C. Dairy Farming
2.10      Increased dairy production is an important factor in promoting
social and economic betterment. Systematic development of dairying in Kerala
began in 1952 with the launching of the All-India Key Village Scheme. This
was followed by the Intensive Cattle Development Project. It was not until
the Third Five Year Plan, however, that a concerted and comprehensive attempt
was made. The Indo-Swiss Project (a joint venture of GOI, GOK and the Swiss
Government) was set up in 1964 at Mattupatty with two specific aims: creation
of a new breed of cattle adapted to local conditions; and promotion of fodder
production. This project has helped to produce Brown-Swiss Cross cattle
suitable for economic milk production. Kerala is reported to have a breeding
herd of 1.3 million cows and 150,000 buffaloes. Cattle in the plains are
invariably stall-fed; as fodder is scarce it is supplemented by concentrates
which constitute the principal source of nutrients. Current annual milk
production in Kerala is 440,000 tons, yielding a per capita daily availability
of 52 grams compared with a recommended minimum requirement of 210 grams.
Dairying offers immense scope for development in Kerala as a means of subsi-
diary employment to the rural poor and as a source of much needed nutrition.
D. Institutions
General
2.11      Agriculture in India is a State responsibility, GOI providing policy
guidelines through the Five Year Plans, and funds as necessary through budget
allocations. GOI follows two approaches to assist States in the planning and
development of major perennial crops: for cardamom, coffee, rubber and tea,
through Commodity Boards which are quasi-government institutions with limited
autonomy under GOI Ministry of Commerce; and for other crops including areca-
nut, cashew, cocoa, coconut and spices, through Directorates which function
under GOI Ministry of Agriculture. The responsibilities of Commodity Boards
are wide and include planning, research, extension, marketing, and labor condi-
tions. The Directorates, however, serve to examine policy and prepare, in
consultation with State Governments, proposals for the Five Year Plans. The
State institutions directly concerned with the agricultural development are
the Department of Agriculture (KDOA) and the Department of Animal Husbandry.
Extension Services
2.12      KDOA and the Department of Animal Husbandry are responsible for
extension services relating to all crops (except those under Commodity Boards)
and livestock. In carrying out extension activities, these Departments now
follow an intensive area approach. Originally three extension workers, one
each specialized in agriculture, animal husbandry or cooperatives, used to be
assigned to a block to service about 30,000 farmers. In addition to being
required to serve too many farmers, these workers were entrusted with many
administrative tasks. There were also numerous unfilled vacancies. Hence
achievements were small. It soon became apparent that a new approach was



-5-
needed and the popularly named "Intensive Package Programs", were introduced.
These were designed to concentrate on a limited area and on major crops,
and involve applying modern techniques of farming, use of better seed, sti-
mulating the flow of credit and providing technical assistance. When Special
Package Programs are approved by GOI, GOK receives funds from the Central
government and engages the necessary staff. There is an adequate pool of
qualified graduates in Kerala with agricultural background and training
(Annex 1, paras 23, 24 and 25).
Research
2.13      Responsibility for agricultural research was transferred in 1972,
from the State Departments to the Agricultural University of Kerala (AUK).
All State Research Stations including those for coconut, pepper, cashew and
horticulture are now responsible to AUK. AUK carries out applied research,
and research findings are disseminated mainly through KDOA's extension staff.
Basic research related to plantation crops especially coconut is conducted by
CPCRI. The latter works on associations of crops with coconuts, particularly
intercropping with cocoa. CPCRI also organizes training for state extension
staff. KDOA is represented on the Boards of CPCRI and AUK. Research on
livestock development is being carried out at the Indo-Swiss Project.
Credit Institutions
2.14      The apex institution for short and medium-term credit is the
Kerala State Cooperative Bank (SCB). It works through eleven District
Central Cooperative Banks and 1,731 Primary Credit Societies at the District
and village levels respectively. Long-term credit in the cooperative sector
is provided by the Kerala Cooperative Central Land Mortgage Bank (KCLMB)
through thirty Primary Land Mortgage Banks (PLMB). The overdue position of
PLMB in the project area is satisfactory (Annex 12, para 2). Commercial Banks
(CB) are comparatively new to agricultural credit and their lending has been
mostly for short-term crop credit and medium-term investment credit.
Marketing Institutions
2.15      Marketing channels for most commodities are well established in
Kerala. Traditional channels are the village fairs, village merchants,
brokers and agents, wholesalers, processors, exporters and industry. Also in
recent years, cooperative institutions and government sponsored Corporations
and Boards have entered this field. In Kerala there are more than 2,000
market fairs, thousands of established private traders, and about 100 Co-
operative Primary Marketing Societies handling marketing at the village
level. The State Trading Corporation of India, Kerala State Cooperative
Marketing Federation, Kerala Rubber Marketing Federation (KRMF), and the
Kerala Livestock Development and Milk Marketing Board are some of the larger
government sponsored institutions actively involved.



- 6 -
III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Description
3.01      The project would comprise:
(i) new planting of high yielding coconut - 5,000 ha
in Cannanore, Kozhikode and Malappuram Districts;
(ii) rehabilitation of coconut, including replanting of
senile and unproductive trees - 30,000 ha in areas
free of root wilt disease in Cannanore, Kozhikode,
Malappuram and Trivandrum Districts;
(iii) provision of minor irrigation facilities in project
coconut areas - 1,000 ha in new planting areas, and
7,500 ha in rehabilitation areas;
(iv) intercropping in coconut areas - 26,500 ha of various
garden crops without irrigation; about 3,000 ha of
cocoa, about 3,000 ha of fodder for dairy cattle and
about 2,500 ha of other crops with irrigation;
(v) rehabilitation, including replanting of pepper - 10,000
ha in Cannanore, Idukki and Kottayam Districts;
(vi) rehabilitation of a State-owned cashew plantation of
2,280 ha and new plantings of 1,470 ha in Cannanore
District;
(vii) establishment of about 435 ha of seed gardens for coconut,
cashew, cocoa and spices;
(viii)  establishment of ten crumb rubber factories (nine new and
one expansion), each of 10 ton/day capacity;
(ix) strengthening of research by CPCRI, AUK and IRB, and
training and technical assistance; and
(x) provision of improved extension services and investment
credit facilities for project participants.
3.02      Activities described in para 3.01 (i) through (v) would be
carried out by small farmers who would be provided with credit and extension
and other support services. Credit would cover costs of land preparation;
planting, and replanting and rehabilitation; maintenance of plantings till
maturity; irrigation facilities; and dairy stock. The program would be
administered through the Special Agricultural Development Unit (SADU)
described in paras 4.02 and 4.03. Ground water surveys carried out by GOK



indicate that a minimum of 20% of areas identified for coconut new plantings
and 25% of the areas planned for coconut rehabilitation would be suitable for
intensive development under irrigation.
3.03      It is estimated that it would take up to three years from the time
of initiating activities in any single extension package unit before all
interested farmers started developing their holdings. Thus it has been
assumed that 25% of participating farmers would respond in the first year of
commencing project activity in their unit, 50% in the following year and the
remaining 25% in the third year.
3.04      Coconut Development.  Land available for coconut new planting is
limited and estimated at a maximum of 40,000 ha of which 5,000 ha would
be planted under the project. The plantings would be phased over a period of
five years (Annex 2, para 10). The recommended stand of 175 trees per ha
would provide sufficient space for intercropping. Coconut rehabilitation
would aim at improved maintenance, removing all unproductive trees and re-
planting as necessary. On an average, about 20 trees per ha would be re-
planted. The tree stand would be maintained at about 175/ha to permit
intercropping on a more systematic and intensified basis.
3.05      Coconut seedlings used for new planting and replanting would be
from selected local varieties raised in KDOA's 18 nurseries. Good quality
seed nuts are procured mainly from private farmers having good mother palms.
KDOA can supply over one million seedlings annually and would be able to
meet project requirements estimated at 1.6 million over 7 years and a maxi-
mum of 600,000 in any one year. An assurance was obtained from GOK that
the project would be supplied with sufficient planting material of good
quality. Cultivation practices would generally be in accordance with the
package of practices recommended by KDOA.1/ Systematic intercropping would be
encouraged in order to maximize farmer incomes. Intercropping under rehabili-
tated coconut, except in irrigated holdings where cocoa and fodder grass would
be grown, would be restricted to crops that can be harvested before the dry
season to minimize competition for moisture eg. tapioca, pulses and vegetables.
3.06      About 8,500 ha would be provided with irrigation through ground
water exploitation. Provision is made for new wells, deepening of existing
wells, filter points in sandy soils, and for the supply of pumpsets.
1/   KDOA, through its extension service units, recommends cultivation
practices for each of the major crops grown in the State. These recom-
mendations generally follow the "Package of Practices" published period-
ically by CPCRI for the respective crops. In the case of coconut,
controlled intercropping is recommended which generates an additional
source of income and also benefits the major crop by helping to control
weeds.



-8-
Pumps of 1.5 hp would be installed on holdings of between 0.5 ha and 1 ha.
Farmers with larger holdings would be given loans for 3 hp pumps. The pump-
sets would be powered by electricity which is available in all coconut areas
identified for project development. An assurance was obtained from GOK
that the Kerala State Electricity Board (KSEB) would provide connections for
the pumpsets as and when these are installed. Funds required by KSEB for
completing such connections are included in project costs 1/.
3.07      Cocoa is grown as intercrop mainly in coconut and arecanut gardens
and also as a pure crop in partially cleared forest land. For optimum re-
sults cocoa requires light shade and well distributed rainfall. The dry
seasons in Kerala are long and economic cocoa production is possible only
under irrigation. Cocoa interplanting would therefore be limited to re-
habilitated coconut areas which would be provided with irrigation under the
project. Compact areas would be located for cocoa development to permit
intensive supervision and use of plant protection measures. KDOA nurseries
would provide Amelanado planting material. Planting intensity would be
about 300/ha.
3.08      Dairy Farming.  Trials carried out under the joint auspices of the
Indo-Swiss Project and CPCRI have shown that dairy farming under coconut is
very profitable. The facts show that 1 ha of coconut with irrigation can
produce a minimum of 37.5 tons of green fodder annually from interplanted
grasses. Ten tons of green fodder per annum is adequate to maintain a cow
also receiving about 1.5 tons of concentrate feed. Thus 1 ha of coconut
planted with grass would be able to support 3 cows. Project cattle would be
mainly Brown-Swiss Cross, supplied by the Indo-Swiss Project (para 2.10),
although in the initial stages, due to a shortage of Brown-Swiss Cross,
upgraded local cattle would also be used. Existing artificial insemination
and veterinary and animal husbandry extension services would be strengthened
under the project.
3.09      As most farmers would wish to grow food crops on about 20% of
their holdings, about 6,000 ha would be available for cocoa and dairying
on the 7,500 ha of coconut areas to be rehabilitated with irrigation.
This would be split equally between fodder and cocoa. Phasing of coconut
rehabilitation and intercropping is shown in Annex 2, Table 1.
1/   KSEB is a suitable recipient of IDA funds insofar as it is a beneficiary
of existing Power Credits. In accordance with assurance given in the
Third Power Transmission Credit (377-IN), GOK has agreed to provide an
annual subsidy to KSEB to cover any losses incurred in its rural elec-
trification operations.



-9-
3.10      Pepper Rehabilitation.  It is proposed to rehabilitate 10,000 ha
of pepper areas in Cannanore, Kottayam and Idukki Districts. On average,
50% of the existing vines in these areas are senile and uneconomic. Further-
more, these older varieties are susceptible to diseases. Rehabilitation
activities would include replanting 50% of the vines with high yielding and
disease resistant types and the introduction of improved cultural practices
as recommended by KDOA. Plantings would be made from rooted cuttings of
selected hybrid varieties produced in KDOA farms.
3.11      Cashew Development.  Project financed cashew development activities
would be restricted to the Kerala State Plantations Corporation (KSPC). The
object of this project component in addition to its purely productive aspects
would be to demonstrate the potential of cashew cultivation when using
modern varieties and techniques. The cashew component consists of three
elements:
(i)  rehabilitation of 2,280 ha of cashew plantation now owned
and operated by KDOA, but which would be taken over by
KSPC. About 10% of the trees would be replaced;
(ii)  new planting of 1,470 ha of cashew, on land to be
made available by the Forest Department. These new
plantings would be made and managed using the most
efficient production techniques;
(iii)  establishing a pilot plant in which to test the
techniques and economics of processing cashew apple.
Current production of this bi-product of cashew
production is estimated at about one million tons
most of which is wasted. The apple can be used as
a basis for beverages and preserves.
Assurances were obtained from GOK that KDOA's cashew plantation of 2,280 ha in
Cannanore District would be transferred to KSPC and that about 1,500 ha of
land suitable for cashew would be made available to KSPC for etablishing a new
plantation. Transfer of these lands would be a condition of disbursement for
this component.
3.12      Seed Gardens.  Although KDOA has established several coconut,
cashew, and pepper nurseries to produce quality seedlings for farmers, its
basic seed supply (including vegetative material for cuttings and buddings)
is inadequate and obtained from research stations, government farms and
private commercial plantings. To remedy this situation, the project would
establish seed gardens; there would be 300 ha for coconut (the major crop),
50 ha for cashew, 35 ha for pepper and about 50 ha for other spices and
cocoa. The coconut garden would be planted so as to produce high yielding
hybrid material of a cross between tall and dwarf trees. The cashew garden
would be planted with superior, high yielding, cultivars available in India.
For pepper, concentration would be on varieties which are highly resistant
to wilt disease. Assurances were obtained from GOK that it would make suit-
able land available for seed gardens. Transfer of such land would be a
condition of disbursement for this component.



- 10 -
3.13      Research, Training and Technical Assistance.  Research by CPCRI and
AUK is meager in relation to the importance of tree crops in the Indian
economy. Research efforts need to be intensified. Provision for research
made under this project would supplement activities already planned under the
Fifth Five Year Plan and includes laboratory buildings, equipment and supplies,
vehicles, research personnel and overseas training. Services of experts, to
be attached to CPCRI, would be secured for short periods totalling 18 man-
months. Overseas training would include that of five crumb rubber factory
managers of the Cooperative Rubber Marketing Societies (CRMS) and five senior
executives of IRB who would be associated with project implementation. Engin-
eering services, estimated at 27 man-months, would be obtained from specialists
in crumb rubber factory construction. Additionally, 12 man-months services of
a crumb rubber processing specialist would be engaged for periodic visits to
assist IRB in implementation work. Assurances were obtained from GOI that
arrangements for overseas training would be finalized in consultation with
IDA and that technical assistance specialists provided would be employed in
consultation with and with qualifications, and on terms and conditions accept-
able to IDA. Employment of consultants for engineering services would be a
condition of disbursement for the Rubber Processing component. Details of
research, and technical assistance are in Annexes 6 and 7.
3.14      Extension Services.  Intensive technical support would be essential
for successful smallholder operations and would be provided through strength-
ened extension services. The extension approach employed would be similar to
that followed by KDOA in its Intensive Package Programs for coconut and paddy
(Annex 1, paras 12 and 24). The staffing intensity at field level would,
however, be doubled in the project areas. Two Agricultural Demonstrators led
by one Junior Agricultural Officer would form a team and cover a package unit
of 500 ha or about 1,200 farm families - the staffing intensity at field level
being one extension worker for every 400 families during the initial three to
four years of development in any one extension package unit and to 800 families
thereafter until development is completed (2 or 3 additional years in one unit).
One Veterinary Surgeon and 3 Livestock Assistants per 500 ha of fodder grass
(supporting about 1,800 cows) would be posted in addition to the agricultural
officers (Annex 10, para 5). Provision has also been made for training, ve-
hicles for staff, low cost buildings in the project areas for administration
offices, and spraying equipment for plant protection campaigns. Following
GOK's current practice, motorcycles would be sold to staff on credit. The
Intensive Package Program approach is similar in its agricultural extension
aspects to the Training and Visits method recently introduced in other Indian
States. However, in view of its limited and scattered geographical coverage,
the project is not a suitable vehicle for reorganizing the Statewide exten-
sion service along Training and Visits lines.
3.15      Rubber Processing.  Total rubber plantings have increased substan-
tially in the past fifteen years 1/ and natural rubber production in India
1/   Encouraged by the new Planting Loan Scheme (1962), the Replanting
Subsidy Scheme (1957), and the special extension services, all
administered by IRB.



- 11 -
is expected to increase from 165,000 tons in 1975 to 205,000 tons in 1985
and 250,000 tons in 1990. About 90% of the increase would come from Kerala
where 60% of total production is from smallholders. Existing processing
facilities for smallholder rubber are inadequate. The product, mostly ribbed
smoked sheet (RSS), is of poor quality and fetches relatively low prices and
more than 20% of smallholders' rubber production is sold to remillers as
scrap grades at even lower prices. Under the project nine new crumb rubber
factories would be established and the plant now operated by the Palai CRMS
would be expanded.
3.16      The new factories would be sited so as to minimize collection costs
of rubber. Kottayam, as the District most densely planted with rubber in
Kerala, would have two of the new factories. Quilon District would have two
and one each would be located in Alleppey, Cannanore, Kozhikode, Idukki and
Palghat Districts. Exact locations would be based on special surveys now
being carried out by IRB to evaluate availability of scrap and latex from
smallholders, and the ability of the different CRMS to operate the crumb rub-
ber factories on a commercial basis. The Agricultural Refinance and Develop-
ment Corporation (ARDC) would finally appraise all proposals for crumb rubber
factories. Each factory would be designed to operate three shifts with a
total daily output of 10 tons. The Palai factory would be expanded to the
same production level. None of the CRMS has experience in managing major
industrial ventures. A first and essential step in the program is to arrange
for the training overseas of the potential factory managers, and the IRB
officers who would undertake project coordination and supervision. Addi-
tional support for factory construction and operation would be given through
technical assistance consultants to be employed by IRB (3.13).
3.17      All factories would be designed to produce Grade B crumb rubber
using 70% to 80% scrap grades blended with RSS/field latex. 1/ Each factory
would be equipped with testing laboratories so that all crumb rubber pro-
duced would be marketed as technically specified rubber. Construction of two
new factories and the expansion of Palai factory would commence in project
year two. Three more would be commenced in the following year. The remaining
four would be constructed in 1981 for commissioning in 1982. An assurance
was obtained from GOI that the locations and the timing of construction of
the nine new crumb rubber factories would be finalized in consultation with
IDA.
B. Cost Estimates
3.18      Total project costs including duties and taxes over a seven-year
development period are estimated to be Rs 619 M (US$69 M equivalent) of which
the foreign exchange would be about Rs 90 M (US$10 M equivalent). The follow-
ing is a summary of project costs; details are given in Annex 8.
1/   Grade B crumb rubber, consumed in largest quantities, is an in-between
quality, Grade A being the highest quality made mostly out of premium
grades, and Grade C the lowest quality made mostly out of scrap rubber.

3.19      Cost estimates are based on findings during appraisal updated to
September 1976. Physical contingencies have been added at 10% on rubber
processing factories, buildings, equipment, seed gardens and cashew devel-
opment because cost estimates for these are not based on detailed engineering
drawings and factory and farm locations are yet to be finalized. Physical
contingencies are not needed for on-farm development by smallholders, but 5%
has been added to costs of extension services, research, training and techni-
cal assistance components. Price contingencies totalling 30% of base costs
(excluding physical contingencies) are also included, representing price in-
creases compounded annually on all cost-components except wages and salaries.
These are based on Bank guidelines for inflation rates in India and countries

US$7 M. The remaining costs of Rs 339 M (US$39 M) would be met by farmers,
ARDC and participating banks, GOK, GOI, CRMS, IRB, KSEB and KSPC. The

- 14 -
3.21      The IDA Credit of US$30 M to GOI would be on standard terms.  GOI
would make available to ARDC under a Subsidiary Loan Agreement, US$20.7 M to
refinance loans by lending banks to farmers and KSEB for smallholder develop-
ment, KSPC for Cashew Development, and CRMS for rubber processing facilities.
US$2.0 M, would be passed on to GOK for allocation as appropriate to the
various agencies (IRB, CPCRI, AUK, SADU) concerned with the project. The
remaining US$7.3 M of the Credit would be left unallocated to meet physical
and price contingencies. GOI and GOK would provide directly US$7.0 M to
supplement IDA's contribution towards the seed gardens, research, technical
assistance to rubber processing and extension services components. ARDC
refinance would cover 90% of loans for irrigated development and up to 80% of
the amounts loaned for other purposes. The lending banks themselves would
expect the borrowing agencies to find 5% to 15% of the investment costs out
of their own resources. The lending banks would also provide short-term
finance for the working capital needs of all project participants (4.08).
Execution of the Subsidiary Loan Agreement between GOI and ARDC would be a
condition of Credit effectiveness.
D.  Procurement and Disbursement-/
3.22      Equipment for the nine new crumb rubber factories (US$2.2 M) would
be procured by international competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance with IDA
guidelines. Equipment for the Palai crumb rubber factory, farm equipment,
vehicles, laboratory and office equipment (US$3.1 M) would also be awarded
under ICB except when contracts could not be bulked at US$100,000 or more,
when they would be awarded in accordance with local procurement procedures
in which competition is assured by adequate representation of local suppliers
and local agents of foreign suppliers. Local manufacturers would receive a
margin of preference of 15% on equipment or the prevailing customs duty if
lower. Engineering and technical advisory services (US$0.5 M) would be en-
gaged in accordance with IDA guidelines for selection of consultants. As is
usual in the case of agricultural credit operations, ICB procedures would not
be suitable for procurement financed by loans to smallholder farmers
(US$34.9 M) comprising farm development inputs; about 75,000 farmers would be
involved, individual amounts would be small and the operation would be spread
over seven years. On-farm development for cashew and seed gardens (US$1.1 M)
would be carried out by State agencies on force account. Contracts for civil
works (US$4.8 M), because of their small size and scattered locations, would be
let on the basis of competitive bidding, advertised locally and in accordance
with local procedures which are satisfactory. The balance of project costs
US$5.0 M (excluding contingencies) would consist of staff and related expend-
itures not involving procurement. Draft tender documents for the equipment of
the new crumb rubber factories and contracts in excess of US$200,000 would be
submitted to IDA for approval before bidding documents are issued and bid
evaluations submitted before making the contract awards. An assurance was
obtained in this regard during Credit negotiations.
1/ All values stated under this heading exclude contingency provisions.



- 15 -
3.23      Disbursements from the proposed Credit would cover:
(a)  100% of foreign expenditure on imported items;
(b)  100% of ex-factory price of locally manufactured items;
(c)  100% of costs of technical assistance services
and overseas training;
(d)  50% of expenditures on locally procured items;
(e)  50% of loans disbursed to smallholders and KSEB
and refinanced by ARDC; and
(f)  50% of all civil works, and development costs
relating to cashew farms and seed gardens.
Disbursements for items in subparagraphs (a), (b), (c) and (d) would be made
against full documentation. For items (e) and (f), disbursements would be
made against certificates of expenditure. The documents supporting the latter
type of disbursement would not be submitted to IDA for review but would be
retained by GOI and GOK and the participating agencies and would be audited
quarterly by the Government Auditor General. 1/ Such documents would be made
available for inspection by IDA during the course of project supervision
missions. Appropriate assurances were obtained from GOI and GOK in this
regard. A schedule of estimated disbursements is given in Annex 9. Any
amount not used for the project would be cancelled.
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
General
4.01      Coconut and pepper area development including intercropping, cocoa
cultivation, dairy development, related extension support and seed gardens
would be GOK's responsibility and implemented through a Special Agricultural
Development Unit (SADU) set up under the Kerala Agricultural Production
Commissioner. Cashew development would be undertaken by KSPC. Rubber pro-
cessing activities would be coordinated through IRB. Agricultural research
would be handled jointly by CPCRI and AUK. Investment credit to participating
farmers would be provided by KCLMB and CB; credit required for construction of
crumb rubber processing factories would be provided by SCB. The foregoing
credit would be refinanced by ARDC in accordance with conditions established
under ongoing ARDC credit projects.
1/   The Auditor General does not audit documents retained by ARDC.



- 16 -
Special Agricultural Development Unit (SADU)
4.02      SADU would be a new administrative entity under the Agricultural
Production Commissioner and staffed with officers transferred from existing
GOK Departments and new recruits. SADU would supervise the implementation
of new coconut plantings, coconut rehabilitation, intercropping in coconut
areas, and development of seed gardens. The chief executive of SADU would
be an officer at the level at least of an Additional Director. He would be
supported by four deputies at Joint Director level for specialized responsi-
bilities: Cooperative institutions covering agricultural credit and input
supplies; coconut cultivation and intercropping; pepper cultivation and seed
gardens; and finance and administration.
4.03      Project extension services under SADU would supplement existing
arrangements and be based on a package unit system (3.14). The number of
units proposed for the development under this project is deliberately modest
in relation to the total area cultivated under project crops. A total of
105 package units would be set up and there would be no problem in recruiting
special staff required for these units - about 400 at peak requirement -
from the available pool of agricultural graduates. As development is completed
staff would be transferred to other extension units in areas where SADU would
initiate similar developments based on experience gained in the proposed proj-
ect. For field level implementation, each of the six project area Districts
would be managed as a division. The seed garden operations would be adminis-
tered as the seventh division. Each of these divisions would be headed by an
officer at least at the level of Assistant Director, and supported by techni-
cally qualified staff as appropriate. Details are in Annex 10.
Kerala State Plantations Corporation (KSPC)
4.04      A new Project Department has been set up within KSPC to implement
the cashew development component. This Department would be headed by a
full-time Project Director who would be directly responsible to the Managing
Director of KSPC. Additional staff would be appointed both for the headquar-
ters and the field. Details are in Annex 4.
Rubber Processing
4.05      The crumb rubber factories would be owned and operated by CRMS and
KRMF.  Each of these factories would have staff for its processing and market-
ing activities and be headed by a trained Factory Manager. He would be respon-
sible to a Management Committee, reporting through the Chairman of the Society.
In order to assist the participating Cooperative institutions in their invest-
ment planning and project implementation and to coordinate all related activi-
ties, a Rubber Processing Department would be set up as a new administrative
entity within IRB. This Department would be headed by a project leader
appointed at the level of an IRB Director. He would be assisted by three
specialist senior executives and provided with supporting staff and facilities.
Details of staffing needs and terms of reference are in Annex 7 and Appendices.



- 17 -
Assurances were obtained from GOI and GOK that the processing factories
would be staffed adequately and that qualification, terms and conditions of
employment would be acceptable to IRB, and that the Processing Department
itself would be adequately staffed to carry out the functions stated in
Annex 7, Appendix 3.
Agricultural Research
4.06      Research work would be carried out by CPCRI and AUK.  The former
is a national institution, being a divisional unit of the Indian Council of
Agricultural Research (ICAR); the latter is, however, a Kerala State institu-
tion. The research programs proposed for both institutions would be coordi-
nated by a Joint Technical Committee headed by the Vice-Chancellor of AUK
and would include representatives from CPCRI, GOK, KDOA, GOI Development
Directorate and ICAR. This Committee would also evaluate progress of the
research work to be undertaken. Present arrangements for disseminating re-
search findings through KDOA extension staff are satisfactory (2.13 and
Annex 6, para 5). AUK would continue to carry the main responsibility for
adaptive research, and for training the extension staff. CPCRI will comple-
ment this by providing special training courses.
Agricultural Credit and Farm Inputs
4.07      ARDC which has satisfactorily operated several IDA financed proj-
ects, is a suitable channel for providing credit to all project participants
through its refinance operations (Appendix in Annex 12). Long-term credit to
participating smallholders would be provided by KCLMB and CB based on a bank-
ing plan to be prepared by ARDC. SADU extension service staff would counsel
the farmer and help him to prepare his development program and the loan appli-
cation. The loan application with the recommendation of SADU would be sub-
mitted to the participating bank for appraisal of the application and for grant
of the loan which would be disbursed to the farmer over a period, depending on
the nature of development undertaken. Long-term credit provided would cover
the farmer's investment costs, less his own contribution which would vary from
5% to 15%. The loans would carry interest at 11% and be for periods up to
fifteen years including a grace period of three to seven years. Interest not
paid during the grace period would be capitalized and treated as part of the
loan (Annex 12, Table 1). For short-term credit needs, existing arrangements -
seasonal loans provided by Cooperative and Commercial Banks - are adequate
and would be continued. Similarly, existing arrangements for distribution
of planting material, fertilizer and chemicals by KDOA are adequate.
4.08      Finance for energizing farmers' pumpsets would be provided to KSEB
by the same banks providing loans for the pumpsets. Long-term needs of
KSPC for cashew development would be provided by a participating bank of its
choice. Finance to CRMS or KRMF for construction of the crumb rubber fac-
tories would be disbursed by SCB. However, ARDC would refinance all these
activities according to terms, conditions and procedures to be specified in
a Refinance Agreement to be concluded between ARDC and the participating
banks. ARDC would prepare a project banking plan which would lay down a
program for strengthening the rural banking system. Preparation of a banking
plan satisfactory to IDA would be a condition of Credit effectiveness.



- 18 -
Accounting and Audit
4.09      Agencies participating in project development - SADU, KSPC, IRB,
CRMS, KRMF, CPCRI, AUK - would prepare annual budgets, quarterly progress
reports and annual accounts, as they relate to project activities. Assurances
were obtained from GOI and GOK that copies of these documents would be
submitted to IDA as follows:
(a) Annual work programs and related cost estimates not later
than two months prior to the commencement of the financial
year; and
(b) Quarterly progress reports within one month of the close
of each quarter.
Project accounts prepared by the agencies concerned would be subject to
annual audits by the Government Auditor General's Office or other independent
auditors. Assurances were obtained from GOI and GOK that audit for these
agencies, satisfactory to IDA, would be made and that the audited accounts
together with longform and shortform reports with which GOI is familiar, would
be submitted to IDA within five months of the close of their financial year.
The auditing arrangements for ARDC and participating banks would be as have
been agreed for other Bank Group projects in which ARDC participates.
Project Implementation
4.10      A Project Coordination Committee (PCC) has been established with the
Agricultural Production Commissioner as Chairman. PCC would have representa-
tion from Kerala State Ministries of Agriculture and Cooperative, SADU, ARDC,
KSPC and IRB. PCC would have attached to it a full time senior official who
would function as its Chief Executive. He would be provided with necessary
support staff and facilities. Its functions will include: coordination of
procurement under the project; liaison with GOI, GOK and IDA; project monitor-
ing; and IDA disbursements. An Evaluation Unit would also be established to
follow-up project implementation, identify problem areas and evaluate project
benefits realized by participating farmers and agencies. Terms of reference
and staffing needs of the Unit and PCC are given in Annex 10. An assurance
was obtained from GOK that this Unit would be established not later than
September 30, 1977 and maintained for minimum ten years.
4.11      As timely establishment of the new agencies mentioned in paras 4.01
to 4.08 is vital to the successful implementation of the project, SADU, PCC,
and the Cashew Project Department in KSPC were established before Credit nego-
tiations. The Rubber Processing Department in IRB would be established not
later than March 31, 1977. An assurance was obtained from GOI and GOK, that
during the project period, all these agencies would be adequately staffed to
carry out the respective functions proposed for them. Project implementation
schedules agreed during negotiations are given in Annex 11.

V. PRODUCTION, MARKETING AND FINANCIAL RESULTS
Yields and Production
5.01      The largest production increase would be from coconut.  With im-
proved cultivation, yields would increase from 20 nuts/tree to more than 40
nuts in unirrigated areas and 60 nuts in irrigated areas. The incremental
nut production from the 30,000 ha to be rehabilitated is estimated to be 71 M
nuts at the end of the seventh year and 149 M nuts (13,500 tons coconut oil
equivalent) when all plantings reach maximum production 1/. All trees in the
5,000 ha new plantings would reach bearing stage ten years after project com-
mencement. With annual average yields, at full bearing, of 60 nuts without
irrigation and 80 nuts with irrigation, the annual production (all incremen-
tal) from new plantings would peak to 56 M nuts (4,500 tons coconut oil). 1/
Yield assumptions (see Annex 13, Table 3) and rate of uptake by farmers are
based on experience gained in implementation of ongoing projects (coconut) and
on data obtained from research stations and trials (cocoa, cashew and dairy).
Production projections are in Annex 13, Table 2.
5.02      Interplantings of cocoa  would come into bearing in four years.  As
cocoa is a relatively new crop to India, very conservative yield assumptions
are made. Assuming a yield at full production to be 650 kg/ha, annual pro-
duction of cocoa from the 3,000 ha interplantings would be 1,950 tons by
year fifteen. Intercropping with fodder grass for dairy cattle would result
in an increase in annual milk production of 15,000 tons. Incremental annual
production from other intercrops by year ten is forecast to be 26,000 tons of
tapioca, 7,000 tons vegetables, 300 tons pulses, and 3,300 tons ginger and
turmeric.
5.03      Present pepper yields in the project areas average 270 kg/ha.
Yields would be 1,100 kg/ha from the new vines and 650 kg from the rehab-
ilitated vines. Assuming a gradual improvement in yields in the 10,000 ha
replanted and rehabilitated, pepper production would more than double from
2,700 tons to 6,200 tons by the end of year seven and increase to 8,700 tons
by year thirteen when the replanted vines reached full production.
5.04      Cashew cultivation by KSPC under the project would have a signifi-
cant impact on yields - increasing from 165 kg per ha at present to 1,500 kg
in the KDOA farm to be rehabilitated and to 2,000 kg in the new plantings.
Annual production from the project area is, however, small in relation to the
total volume of cashew produced in the State. Additional production at full
bearing would be 6,000 tons compared with the Kerala's 1976 production of
about 118,000 tons.
1/   At the end of fifteen years from project commencement.



- 20 -
Markets and Prices
5.05      Incremental output of all products would be handled by existing
marketing channels, village markets and merchants, Cooperative Societies and
Federations, State Corporations, brokers, wholesalers, processors and ex-
porters - (Annex 13, paras 1 and 2). These channels are considered adequate
to handle project production but for milk. Project milk would be marketed by
the Kerala Livestock and Milk Marketing Board (Annex 1, para 30). The National
Dairy Development Board, at the request of the Kerala Board, is carrying out a
survey to plan new investments. By 1980, when project milk would become
available, adequate marketing facilities would be provided by the Kerala Board
(Annex 13, para 13). An assurance was obtained from GOK that the Kerala Board
would prepare a plan, not later than December 1977 to market milk from project
areas. Except for the incremental pepper and cashew output which would be
mainly exported, all production would be for the domestic markets. In the case
of rubber there is no incremental production under the project but 25,000 tons
of low grade rubber would be upgraded into technically specified rubber. This
crumb rubber would be marketed as far as practical through KRMF.
5.06      Market prospects and prices for project commodities are discussed
in Annex 13, paras 3 through 18. Prices used in project models and for finan-
cial analyses are based on Indian prices and have been projected by relat-
ing them to trends in international prices (Annex 13, Table 1) 1/. GOK has
projected that Kerala farmgate price for coconut would not drop below Rs 700
per 1,000 nuts 2/, as compared with Rs 300 based on 1976 world price which
according to Bank's projection, should increase by Rs 200 between 1976 and
1985 (from Rs 300 to Rs 500). Based on 1976 average world market price for
cocoa, Kerala farmgate price equivalent is Rs 13/kg. However, a lower price
of Rs 10/kg (equivalent of US$0.61/lb CIF New York projected by Bank for 1980)
has been used. Although the outlook for cashew is more favorable, 1975 aver-
age prices of Rs 3/kg has been used ignoring future possible increases. For
pepper a price of Rs 10/kg (also 1975 average) has been used. This price is
much lower than the recent high prices (Rs 16 to 20/ kg). No study of the
long term market outlook for pepper is available. A special analysis was
therefore made to test project viability at even lower prices for this com-
modity, the demand for which is inelastic. This test shows that prices could
fall to about 50% of present levels and investment in pepper remains viable
(Annex 15, para 11). Milk price is taken at Rs 1.60/liter, the price fixed by
the Milk Marketing Board, although current free market prices are 20% to 25%
higher than the Board price. Minor intercrops are priced at 1976 rates.
Financial Projections
5.07      The individual farmer's investment per ha would vary from Rs 24,500
when a farmer undertakes irrigated coconut rehabilitation coupled with dairy
1/   Prices used for economic analyses are discussed in Annex 13, Appendix.
2/   1975 average price was Rs 740 per 1,000 nuts, with a high of Rs 950
in January and low of Rs 550 in July-August. Projections for farm
models, cash flow statements and financial rates of return are based
on a price of Rs 700 per 1,000 nuts.



- 21 -
farming to Rs 6,600 for coconut rehabilitation without irrigation. The rates
of return vary from 49% for coconut rehabilitation without irrigation down to
18% for irrigated rehabilitation. Annex 14, Tables 1 to 6 show that if loans
were taken to finance the full amount of the investments 1/, they could be
repaid in full between years eleven and fifteen, even allowing for improvement
in the farm family's consumption levels. This would be adequate incentive
for farmers to participate in the program. Thus more than 45,000 farmers re-
habilitating their coconut holding of 0.5 ha or less would enjoy gradual in-
come increase (from a pre-project income of Rs 1,500) of 33% by year 5 to 100%
by year 15. About 8,000 farmers who would undertake irrigated rehabilitation
would however enjoy only moderate income increases of 10% to 25% during the
repayment period of 13 to 15 years; the investment would nevertheless be
attractive to them since equity built during that period would be high, be-
tween Rs 18,000 and Rs 25,000 and the well and pumpset included in this
investment would immediately improve their living standards because of the
availability of water for drinking, bathing and washing purposes. The rates
of return for seed gardens, cashew and crumb rubber processing are estimated
to be 13%, 22% and 34% respectively. The low return for seed gardens, a
public investment, is due to the low prices assumed for seed sales. This
assumption was deliberate since seed gardens are intended to render a profit-
free service to farmers. Rates of return computations and cash flow state-
ments for these three sub-projects are in Annex 14.
VI. BENEFITS AID ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION
Principal Benefits
6.01      The major economic benefit would be increased production of crops
of major importance to the Indian economy. Most of them would be sold directly
for foreign exchange or cause savings in foreign exchange by reducing the need
for imports. Annual foreign exchange net earnings at full development from
pepper and cashew would be Rs 110 M (US$12 M); foreign exchange net savings
from coconut oil and cocoa would be Rs 125 M (US$14 M). The crumb rubber pro-
cessing component would make available technically specified rubber to tire
manufacturers and help lower their processing costs.
Indirect Benefits
6.02      The project would also give rise to other benefits which are more
difficult to quantify. Longer term production increases of coconut, cashew,
cocoa, pepper, and other spices would come from provision of HYV planting
materials to farmers from the seed gardens. The strengthened research activi-
ties of CPCRI and AUK, particularly in the field of coconut root wilt disease,
could make it possible to restrict this disease from spreading beyond the
250,000 ha already affected. The demonstration effect of the improved culti-
vation practices followed by project participants should spread to surrounding
1/   In fact loans would cover 95% to 85% of the future investments as farmers
must provide own contribution of 5% to 15% depending on the size of
farms.



- 2 2 -
farmers and increase incomes there too. Experience gained in this project
could benefit other areas in Kerala as well as in the neighboring States of
Karnataka and Tamil Nadu since the project is replicable. The irrigation
development on 9,000 farms will improve availability of water for domestic
purposes which may well be shared by neighboring families. Electric reticu-
lation related to these will permit connections to farmers' houses also with
all the related benefits of rural electrification.
Economic Rate of Return (ROR)
6.03      Costs.  Despite heavy unemployment in the State all hired labor has
been costed at Rs 8.00 per day for men and Rs 6.50 for women which rates are
payable under the State's minimum wages legislation. Family labor is shadow
priced zero because it is inconceivable that employment would be available
to family labor outside the farmer's own landholding. Project costs relating
to agricultural research and seed garden components are excluded from the cost
stream as the economic benefits, though very material, would be difficult to
quantify.
6.04      Benefits.  Prices used for import substitution crops (coconut and
cocoa) and for export crops (pepper and cashew) are Indian ports CIF and FOB
prices respectively. Long term prices used are in line with world market
prices as projected by Bank, whenever available. Conservative assumptions
have been made for crop production increases. Exchange rate used in the
computations is Rs 10.50 to I US dollar (rather than the ruling rate of Rs
9.00) to reflect the relative scarcity of foreign exchange.
6.05      Based on these assumptions ROR for the whole project is estimated
to be 30%. ROR for the individual subprojects are:
+ 10%      - 10%
ROR      costs      Benefits
Coconut new planting (unirrigated)    46            41        41
Coconut new planting (irrigated)        25          23        22
Coconut rehabilitation (unirrigated)  33            29        28
Coconut rehabilitation (irrigated)    15            13        12
Pepper rehabilitation                   27          25        25
Cashew development                      39          36        35
Rubber processing                       68         61         60
Return on coconut development with irrigation is low, in relation to unirri-
gated development. However, there would also be significant benefits from
improved health of the farm families and their neighbors through use of water
for drinking, bathing and washing purposes and these have not been quantified.
Sensitivity of ROR to variations in costs and benefits are detailed in Annex
15, Table 1. The effects of variations in labor costs, exchange rates, and
development delays have also been tested (Annex 15, para 10).
Risks
6.06      The most significant risk inherent in the project lies in the com-
plexity of the project itself, which would involve a number of different



- 23 -
crops, thousands of smallholder farmers, several government departments, co-
operative societies and national and State institutions. This could result
in some components of the project moving more slowly than others. Develop-
ment programs with relatively longer gestation periods and involving small-
holder farmers, as in the case of the proposed project, also raise risks of
inadequate receptivity. However, the risks i.rvclved were considered and
conservative assumptions made in regard to project phasing, costs, yields and
commodity prices. Measures to coordinate, monitor and expedite all phases
of implementation have been discussed with GOI, GOK and other agencies, and
necessary arrangements made. Risks not covered by these steps are no greater
than can normally be expected with operations of this type, and acceptable in
view of the benefits which would accrue to a large number of comparatively
small farmers and to the economy as a whole.
Social Benefits
6.07      Social benefits as a result of the project would be very substan-
tial: increased incomes (by 100 to 300%) of some 75,000 coconut and pepper
smallholders, the majority of whom belongs to the poorest segment of the
society (para 5.07); about 50,000 small rubber growers would enjoy higher
prices for their output. Some of the farmers would also benefit from
increased milk production and consumption and from increased output of sub-
sistence food crops. In all, some 700,000 persons would benefit directly,
being the family members of the participating farmers. In addition,
the project would create about 50,000 man-years of additional employment
during the development period and 13,000 man-years annually thereafter
because of the need for more intensive management and to cope with larger
outputs. There will also be additional employment in labor intensive in-
dustries related to project crops (oil milling, coir industry and cashew
processing). Persons other than participating family members might also
share in these increases.
