INDIA
KERALA AGRIGULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture in Kerala
General
1.        Kerala, the southernmost State in India, is a long narrow coastal
strip between the Arabian Sea and the Western Ghats and lies between 8 N and
12N. It is 580 km long, and 120 km broad in the middle and 30 km in the ex-
treme north and south (Map IBRD 12410). Kerala is one of the smallest States
in India with an area of 39,000 sq km and supports a population of more than
23 million. The State per capita income is lower than all-India average 1/.
2.        Topographically, Kerala has three broad natural divisions running
parallel south to north: the highlands in the east, the undulating midlands
in the center and the flat lowlands bordering the coast in the west. The
highlands behind the Western Ghats range in elevation between 1,000 m and
2,500 m. The mountains are covered with evergreen forests; the lower eleva-
tions are suitable for plantation crops, and tea, coffee, cardamoms and to a
limited extent rubber are cultivated. These highlands are sparsely populated
with about 172 persons/sq km. The lowlands, in contrast, have a population of
1,385/sq km. There, in addition to agriculture, the main occupations are coir
making and fishing.
3.        The lowland region consists of a strip of land along the coast not
more than 30 km in width at any point, having a near level topography, with
sandy to sandy loam soils. Rice is grown in lower areas and coconut in the
higher elevations. Intercropping under coconut is common and a great variety
of crops, including pepper, are grown in coconut groves.
4.        Between the highlands and the lowlands are the midlands with eleva-
tions under 1,000 m. From the foot of the Ghats to the seacoast the hills
become flatter with gentler slopes and the valleys become wider. The soil
in this region is of lateritic type. The midlands produce a variety of crops
including rice, tapioca, banana, ginger, lemon grass, pepper, arecanut, cashew,
coconut and rubber. This region has a population density of 778/sq km. Cash-
ewnut processing is an important occupation in these areas.
1/   The most recent estimate of GNP per capita for India is US$150.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
Climate
5.        Seasons in Kerala are defined by reference to the South-West and
North-East monsoons which are a distinct feature of the west coast of India.
The South-West monsoon lasts from June to August and the North-East monsoon
from October to December, having a rainfall spread of six to seven months.
The annual precipitation averages 3000 am with a small variation (below 20%)
from year to year. Within the State, however, the rainfall variation is
substantial. In the Trivandrum District in the South the average annual
rainfall is 2,000 mm while the average is as high as 3,580 mm in Ernakulam
District and 3,450 mm in the northern Districts of Kozhikode and Cannanore.
High rainfall coupled with tropical location generates high humidity, averag-
ing 70% in most Districts. High humidity throughout the year keeps evapora-
tion low and hence reduces the demand for irrigation water but also favors the
incidence of pests and diseases.
6.        Extremes of temperature are not experienced in most parts of the
State.  The mean minimum moves within a range of 19 C-26 C, while the mean
maximum varies between 270C and 370C. However, from the Western Ghats to
the seacoast, within 120 km, the climatic conditions show remarkable varia-
tion from humid temperate to humid tropical which enables Kerala to grow a
wide variety of crops ranging from rice, coconut and rubber of the humid
tropics to tea, coffee and cardamom in the colder climate.
Agrarian Structure
7.        Agriculture is the dominant activity, providing employment to about
60% of the working population and generating 58% of State income. Cultivated
area is 60% of the State territory. In more than 20% of the holdings, dairy
cattle are raised. There is little scope for extending the area under cul-
tivation as forests, hills and uncultivable lands spread over vast portions of
the State in the highlands. Hence the population pressure on the land in the
coastal and midland zones of Kerala is very high. Almost two-thirds of the
holdings are less than 0.5 ha (Table 1). Those below I ha account for 82% of
total number of holdings but represent only 34% of the total area. 1/ A number
of agrarian reforms are aimed at improving the socio-economic conditions and
include distribution of Government land to agricultural workers.
Tree Crops
8.        Tree crop agriculture covers about 70% of the cropped area in
Kerala making it the leader State in India in terms of total production.2/
Kerala and to a lesser extent the two other Southern States of Tamil Nadu and
1/   The landholdings in the project area are larger than the State average -
Annex 3, para 1, and Annex 12,para 6.
2/ Paddy covers 30% of the gross sown area.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
Karnataka account for nearly all of the national production of tree crops
except for tea (Table 2). The cultivation of tree crops in Kerala is mainly
by smallholders.
9.        Coconut is the traditional crop in Kerala, now cultivated in about
750,000 ha, accounting for a third of the value of agricultural production.
Two-thirds of total Indian production of coconut is from Kerala. Grown in
homesteads and in holdings often mixed with other crops, it is typically a
smallholder activity; the average size of a coconut holding is as small as
0.2 ha and 95% of the holdings are under 2 ha. There are about 2.5 million
holdings in the State with an annual production of 2,700 million nuts.
Coconut is cultivated throughout the State under different soil conditions -
ranging from sandy seashore to clays and laterite soils of the midlands, to
forest soils on the slopes of the Western Ghats.
10.       Oil milling and coir -miaking, the two major industries in the State,
are dependent on coconut production. Some 300,000 tons, representing 80%
of the milling copra in the country is produced in Kerala. Half of this is
milled in Kerala, accounting for an annual coconut oil output of about 95,000
tons processed in 1,110 milling establishments. In addition, the coir indus-
try employs about 600,000 people for the manufacture of yarn, rope and mats,
mainly for exports (Table 3). The importance of coconut in the State economy
goes even further since no part of the coconut tree is wasted: the trunk is
used as building material as well as for making furniture, the leaves are used
for thatching houses, coconut shell and leaf stalks serve as fuel, and the sap
provides toddy and brown sugar.
11.       Average yields of coconut are low and declining - currently they
average 4,000 nuts/ha against 6,000 in 1969/70 and over 10,000 from properly
cultivated trees. Overaged trees, failure to replant, inadequate supply of
high quality seedlings and low application of fertilizers are factors which
have adversely affected production. Coconut has also been affected by a
number of pests and diseases. Root wilt, leaf rot, bud rot, leaf blight, stem
bleeding, Rhinoceros beetle, leaf-eating caterpillar and red palm weevil are
amongst the more devastating coconut pests and diseases. About a third of the
area under coconuts in Kerala has been badly affected by root wilt and leaf
rot disease. Considering the economic importance of coconut cultivation to
the country, the place that Kerala has in coconut production and the signi-
ficance it has for the farming community in Kerala, GOK has decided to en-
courage investments in developing this tree crop by rehabilitation and new
planting.
12.       In 1973-74, GOI sanctioned an intensive package program for imple-
mentation in three States including Kerala. As this program is capable of
increasing production of coconut quite rapidly, it has been proposed to ex-
pand and continue it during the Fifth Five Year Plan. Growers, mostly small-
holders, follow traditional methods of cultivation resulting in uneconomic
yields. It has been proved by research and results from field trials in
Kerala that a 100% increase in yield is possible if the improved cultural



ANNEX 1
Page 4
practices are adopted and a 50% increase can be achieved by irrigation in
the dry season. Positive response was obtained as early as year 3 of treat-
ment in existing package programs. The object of the program is to increase
productivity per unit area through an intensive production program. This is
to be achieved through:
(a) stimulating the flow of credit from various institutional
agencies to enable growers to apply the recommended dose
of fertilizers, to promote irrigation facilities and to
adopt plant protection measures;
(b) popularizing the package of practices to improve yields
through organized demonstration in the growers' fields; and
(c) launching a well-organized extension education campaign
to motivate the growers to participate in the program
with confidence.
13.       Cashew is one of India's major foreign exchange earners.  The coun-
try itself produces about 140,000 tons annually and imports a similar amount
for processing prior to export. The import of raw nuts, however, has been
declining, from 193,000 tons in 1972 to 116,000 tons in 1975 because India's
main suppliers, Mozambique and Tanzania, are developing their own processing
facilities. It will be necessary to expand Indian cashew production substan-
tially if export markets are not to be lost. Kerala alone produces about
118,000 tons of raw cashew annually, from about 105,000 ha, or some 84% of
national production. Cashew yields in Kerala, at 1.1 ton/ha, are about double
the yield in other parts of India but can be increased substantially with
improved management. Kerala therefore provides the best prospect for in-
creasing national production. Kerala is also the center of the cashew pro-
cessing industry. With 264 factories it has 92% of India's processing capacity
and provides employment to 125,000 workers. It accounts for a similar propor-
tion of the country's US$110 M exports of processed cashew.
14.       Pepper.  India with her production of 26,000 tons accounts for 35%
of the world output of pepper, and Kerala accounts for 98% of India's pro-
duction. Pepper area in Kerala is estimated to be 120,000 ha and the average
yields are low at 250 kg/ha compared with 1,000 kg obtained from planta-
tions in Indonesia. This marked difference is attributed to unimproved
varieties, old age of the vines, disease (slow and quick wilt) and inadequate
cultivation practices. Panniyur-I is a new hybrid variety developed for its
production potential with yields up to 4 times higher than the present average
level. The Kerala Department of Agriculture is at present multiplying pepper
cuttings - Panniyur-I and other superior varieties like Kalluvally, Kottanadan
and Karimunda - and distributing the planting material to farmers at cost.
15.       Other Spices.  Turmeric and chillies are produced in large quanti-
ties in the northern Districts. Small quantities of clove, coriander, nutmeg,
cinnamon, garlic and mustard are grown, mainly for local consumption. Most
of these spices are grown on household plots. Turmeric and chillies are also
grown as intercrops under coconut.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
16.       Rubber is an important crop in Kerala.  It is grown as a mono
crop mostly on the lower slopes of the Western Ghats below 300 m where agro-
climatic conditions are particularly suitable. The State is the largest
producer in the country, with 92% of the total production. Rubber plantings
in India have increased by 50% during the twelve years ended 1975-76 to almost
225,000 ha. Average yield has improved from 265 kg/ha in 1961 to 760 kg/ha
in 1975 although this is below potential - in Malaysia yields average 1,050
kg/ha. The smallholder sector now accounts for 60% of the rubber produced in
the State. The average size of rubber holdings is 1.2 ha.
17.       Cocoa.  Cocoa is a relatively new crop to India 1/.  All the
plantings are in South India since it is a crop of the humid tropics. Under
Kerala conditions the production of cocoa is suited to smallholder agricul-
ture, particularly as the fermentation of beans is a relatively simple process.
The total area covered by cocoa is estimated to be about 6,000 ha most of it
planted since 1970. However, a considerable amount of research and develop-
ment work has been carried out in India. The Central Plantation Crops Research
Institute (CPCRI) situated at Kasaragod in the north of Kerala, has carried
out a considerable amount of experimentation on cocoa intercropping-varieties,
spacing, shade and husbandry required. Results from experiments in other
countries are born out by experimental and field results in South India - that
cocoa grown under mature coconuts at a stand of 175 trees/ha can yield at
least 700 kg/ha of dry beans at full maturity in the 9th and 10th year.
Due to intensive extension efforts cocoa has come to have farmers' acceptance
in Kerala. The soil and climatic conditions are also well suited for cocoa
cultivation.
Garden Crops
18.       Tapioca 1/.  Second in importance to rice only as a staple food,
tapioca is grown on about 318,000 ha in the State often as an intercrop
in coconut and other tree gardens. It is also cultivated in most of the
homesteads. The poorer sectors of the population depend largely on tapioca
for food. Many varieties are grown with maturities ranging from six to twelve
months. Tapioca thrives on all types of land in Kerala from the coastline up
to an elevation of 1,000 m. An average yield of 18 tons/ha is obtained.
Due to the development of the starch industry, tapioca has become an impor-
tant cash crop as well. It is estimated that 60% of the production is con-
sumed as food, 20% by the industry and the remaining 20% exported mainly to
the neighboring States.
19.       Pulses.  Kerala produces about 14,000 tons of pulses grown on
37,000 ha. This is small in relation to the State's requirement estimated
1/   It does not yet appear in production statistics.
2/   Also called cassava or manioca.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
at 525,000 tons (at the rate of 62 grams per person). The State is therefore
encouraging the use of such legumes in crop rotation to increase their supply
and also to improve soil fertility. Often grown on the moisture left in the
soil after paddy, pulses are also grown as intercrops under coconut.
20.       Ginger.  India produces more than 50% of the world's ginger and
Kerala accounts for more than 50% of that. Kerala produces about 26,000 tons
of dry ginger from 12,000 ha devoted to this crop. It is usually cultivated
as an annual crop, often as an intercrop under coconut. It takes nine to
ten months to mature and every year a fresh crop is taken. The rhizomes
mature in December-January and are hoed up much in the same way as potatoes.
21.       Fruits and Vegetables.  The whole of Kerala is dotted with groves
of mango, jack, banana and pineapple. Similarly cucumber, pumpkin, gourds,
brinjal, tomato and other vegetables are grown in almost all parts of the
State for local consumption. The area under vegetable is estimated at 35,000
ha; average yield has been estimated at 10 tons/ha.
Agricultural Institutions
22.       Agriculture in India is a State responsibility, GOI providing policy
guidelines through the Five Year and Annual Plans, and funds as necessary
through budget allocations. GOI follows two approaches with regard to the
planning, support and direction of major tree and perennial crops - Commodity
Boards and Directorates. Responsibilities of the Commodity Boards are wide
and include planning, extension, research and even marketing in the case of
coffee. The Directorates, however, serve only to examine policy and to work
out proposals for the Five Year Plans in consultation with the State Govern-
ments.  Commodity Boards have been established under the GOI Ministry of
Commerce for cardamom, coffee, rubber and tea. Other crops including coconut,
arecanut, cashew, cocoa and spices come under Directorates which are organi-
zations of the GOI Ministry of Agriculture.
State Institutions
23.       Agricultural extension services:  In Kerala, the State Department
of Agriculture (KDOA) is responsible for implementing GOI and GOK plans and
policies and administers extension services relating to all crops except those
under the Commodity Boards (para 22). The Block, deemed to be the unit most
closely in touch with the farmer, had assigned to it three extension workers
each specialized in agriculture, animal husbandry and cooperatives to service
about 30,000 farmers. In addition to being required to serve so many farmers,
these workers were entrusted with many administrative tasks. There were also
numerous unfilled vacancies. Hence achievement at field level has been poor.
It became apparent that extension work had been spread too thin and a new
approach was therefore envisaged. A new development strategy, popularly named
Intensive Package Programs, was launched in 1973/74. As in the Training and
Visit System of extension supported by the Association in other Indian States,
its aim is to concentrate on a limited area and on major crops. Extension



ANNEX 1
Page 7
workers concentrate on cultivation techniques, use of better seed and fer-
tilizer application, plant protection, and stimulating the flow of credit.
When special package programs are approved by GOI, GOK receives funds from GOI
and engages the necessary staff, either by transfer or by new recruitments.
There is an adequate pool of qualified graduates with agricultural background
and training.
24.       The package programs introduced in 1973-74 related to the two major
crops of Kerala - paddy, through the Intensive Paddy Development Schemes which
have shown 40% yield increase, and coconut through the Intensive Coc6nut
Package Program described in para 12 of this Annex. For each two units or
1,000 ha of this Scheme, there is a field level team of two Agricultural
Demonstrators and one Junior Agricultural Officer.
25.       Supervision of all extension work within a District is exercised by a
Deputy-Director, with District Officers and subject-matter specialists. Three
Regional Joint Directors of Agriculture assisted by two Assistant Directors are
in charge of all activities in a region comprising three Districts. The
Regional Joint Directors function as the executive arm of the KDOA in the
Region to facilitate supervision of field work. Three Additional Directors
dealing respectively with food crops, commercial crops and planning at State
level assist the Director of Agriculture who is the ultimate authority in
KDOA. The Director of Agriculture is responsible to the Agriculture Pro-
duction Commissioner, who is the Chief Officer of Agriculture in the State.
26.       Research institutions in the State concerned with tree crops (ex-
cept rubber) are described in Annex 6. Rubber research is carried out by the
Rubber Research Institute of India (para 32).
Institutions Dealing with Animal Husbandry
27.       The Indo-Swiss Project in Mattupatty was started in 1964 as a joint
venture between GOI, GOK and the Swiss Government. The basic objective was
to breed cattle adapted to local conditions, by crossing exotic breeds with
indigenous animals. The program has proved to be successful and has helped
in providing animals suitable for economic milk production. Further work aims
at consolidating and improving the new breed - Brown-Swiss Cross. The bull
station managed by the Indo-Swiss Project produces now about 300,000 doses of
semen. It has also undertaken applied research for improving the natural
grasslands and introducing fodder species suitable for the farming system in
the State. The area of operation, initially confined to the highlands, has
now been extended to the other parts of Kerala, and research findings support
the feasibility of dairy development in the entire State.
28.       The Animal Husbandry Department is responsible for veterinary
services and extension. At field level, Veterinary Surgeons (equivalent to



ANNEX I
Page 8
JAO) and Livestock Assistants carry out extension work connected with
breeding, health care and timely vaccinations given at the farmers' premises.
The Department also carries out artificial insemination work through the Intensive
Cattle Development Project which followed the All Indian Key Village Scheme
launched in 1951. In all 400,000 inseminations were conducted in 1974-75
under this project but with only limited success.
29.       The Dairy Development Department started its intensive cross breeding
program in 1973. Under this program, in addition to the above field insemina-
tion units have been set up in 721 panchayats for conducting artificial insem-
inations using high quality semen. A total number of 85,000 inseminations
were made in 1974-75.
30.       There are therefore adequate facilities in Kerala for providing the
necessary artificial insemination services. However, due to lack of coor-
dination between the Indo-Swiss Project, Department of Animal Husbandry and
Department of Dairy Development there has been duplication of effort. Fur-
thermore because of inadequate extension, results from artificial insemina-
tion services were discouraging. Such duplication would be avoided under the
project by intensifying extension services and by coordinating artificial in-
semination services through the Kerala Livestock Development and Milk Market-
ing Board, created recently by absorbing the Dairy Development Department and
a section of the Animal Husbandry Department. The Board, wholly owned by GOK,
has as its main objectives increase in productivity of livestock by:
-    provision of crossbreeding facility (made available under
one agency);
-    production of concentrate feed in its own plants;
-    supplying fodder seed to farmers; and
-    marketing of milk.
Institutions Dealing with Rubber
31.       Indian Rubber Board (IRB), established as one of the Commodity
Boards, is responsible for all work on the production and processing of rubber.
The Board is attached to the GOI Ministry of Commerce. It has a full-time
Chairman appointed by the Central Government. He is the principal executive
officer and is responsible for the proper functioning of the Board and imple-
mentation of its decisions and discharge of its duties under the Act. Apart
from the Chairman, the Board has 24 members representing the Parliament,
Central Government, Governments of rubber growing States, large estates, small
growers, dealers, manufacturers, labor and other interests.
32.       The Chairman exercises administrative control over the Board's three
departments. The Department of Administration, apart from looking after gen-
eral administration and finance, controls the marketing of rubber, attends to



ANNEX 1
Page 9
labor welfare measures and collects statistics. The Rubber Production Depart-
ment is responsible for formulation and implementation of all development
schemes; providing supplies, extension services and training to small growers;
and undertaking economic research. Licensing and registration of rubber
estates and holdings are also the functions of this Department. The Research
Department is represented by the Rubber Research Institute of India which
carries out basic and applied research on important aspects of rubber produc-
tion and processing. It has at present four major divisions namely Botany,
Agronomy, Plant Pathology and Chemistry/Rubber Technology. The Research
Department also runs a crumb rubber processing pilot plant.
Rubber Marketing Societies
33.       Since 1960, IRB has been organizing Cooperative Rubber Marketing
Societies (CRMS) in order to assist the smallholders in the marketing of their
rubber. Today there are 36 CRMS of which 27 are affiliated to the State Rub-
ber Marketing Federation (para 34). Only 17 are working, the others are
defunct or transact very little business. Too small to-beweffective, and
operating in a highly competitive field where they represent less than 2% of
the total number of units trading in rubber, 40% of them incurred losses in
their marketing operations. With a few exceptions, the Societies made no
profit to pass on to the smallholder members. They do not have the competi-
tive edge over the village merchants. As a result less than 10% of the
smallholders' rubber is traded through CRMS although over half of them are
members of the very same Societies. IRB has envisaged a new approach to the
problem which is proposed under this project - integrating the processing and
marketing of smallholder rubber through CRMS (Annex 2, para 22 and Annex 7,
para 4).
34.       The Kerala Cooperative Rubber Marketing Federation (KRMF) was
registered in March 1971. Its membership consists of 27 CRMS, IRB and GOK.
Out of the paid up capital of Rs 2.1 M on June 30, 1975, Rs 0.15 M and
Rs 0.40 M were contributed by member CRMS and IRB respectively. The balance
of Rs 1.55 M was contributed by GOK. IRB has sanctioned a working capital
loan of Rs 1 M and KRMF has a cash credit limit of Rs 2.23 M from the Kerala
State Cooperative Bank. Total purchases of rubber during 1974-75 amounted to
Rs 4.13 M, half of which was sold directly to manufacturers through its sales
office in Bombay. KRMF runs a rubber fertilizer mixing unit. The Federation
also arranges for aerial spraying of rubber plantations by helicopters through
contracts placed with the Punjab State Cooperative Marketing Federation. KRMF
has yet to undertake processing activities. It has, however, indicated that
it will be able to own and operate up to three crumb rubber factories out of
the ten units proposed under this project.
35.       The Kerala State Plantation Corporation (KSPC) was incorporated in
1972. It is a public sector undertaking wholly owned by GOK which had contri-
buted Rs 4.37 M as share capital as of March 31, 1975. Its registered office
is in Kottayam and it is controlled by a nominated board of eleven members
including the Kerala Agricultural Production Commissioner. KSPC has mainly
rubber and oil palm plantations and is now developing on a smaller scale



ANNEX 1
Page 10
cinnamon, cardamom, cashew and coffee plantations as well. Of the 8,000 ha
of rubber, 6,000 ha is under tapping. The rubber is processed into smoked
sheets, latex concentrate and crepe in its own factories. Recently KSPC has
undertaken the manufacture of crumb rubber as well. During 1974-75, rubber
sales amounted to 4,187 tons. Agroclimatic conditions in Kerala have been
found suitable for oil palm and, under its program of 5,000 ha, KSPC has
planted 1,000 ha. The seedlings were imported from Malaysia and Nigeria.
KSPC has an installed capacity of 3 tons per day for palm oil extraction.
KSPC had, as of March 31, 1976, obtained loans totalling Rs 32.3 M from GOK.
Secured loans obtained from Commercial Banks arranged through Agricultural
Finance Corporation Ltd at the same date stood at Rs 9.5 M. Fixed assets con-
sisting of developed property, buildings, plant, etc. amounted to Rs 75.32 M.
Profit earned during 1974/75 was about Rs 5.75 M.
Other Institutions
36.       Small Farmer Development Agency (SFDA).  During the Fourth Five
Year Plan, SFDA Schemes were established in 87 Districts throughout India,
including the Districts of Cannanore and Quilon in Kerala. SFDA is engaged in
drawing up Schemes designed to improve the productivity of small and marginal
farmers (in Kerala small farmers are defined as having holdings between one
and two hectares; a marginal farmer has up to one hectare). While assistance
to farmers includes arranging for the supply of inputs and services and for
the establishment of processing and marketing facilities required, SFDA is
primarily responsible for the distribution of GOI subsidies. Subsidies of
25% for small farmers and 33-1/3% for marginal farmers are given towards the
cost of minor irrigation facilities and animal husbandry investments financed
by the Cooperative and Commercial Banks. In two first SFDA Districts, over
a period of five years a total of 3,200 pumpsets, 4,100 wells and 60 filter
points were financed. Since August 1975, Trivandrum and Trichur Districts
have also been covered. During a period of five years SFDA are expected to
invest Rs 15 M and help 7,000 small farmers and 43,000 marginal farmers in
each District.
37.       Credit Institutions are discussed in Annex 12 and marketing insti-
tutions in Annex 13.

ANNEX 2
Page 1
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Scope and Size
General
1.        This Annex discusses briefly the project rationale and the strategy
adopted to secure optimum benefits. The project thrust is improvement of the
economic status of smallholder farmers in Kerala. This project has been
planned by GOK to be a catalytic phase in the agricultural development of the
State. Although 'tree crops'l/ are the main project base, development of other
crops in the chosen coconut areas has been included to provide support for all
agricultural activities of the participating smallholders.
2.        GOI's decision to increase the allocation of resources to the devel-
opment of tree crops agriculture recognizes that:
(i) with emphasis placed in past years on annual food crops,
in particular cereals and pulses, tree crop agriculture
generally got less attention and faces declining pro-
ductivity;
(ii) with few exceptions, the output of perennial crops has
stagnated or shown only a modest growth, and consequently
has failed to make its most effective contribution to
foreign exchange earnings/savings;
(iii) tree crop cultivation in India is mainly a smallholder
operation and any improvement in that sub-sector would
affect a large number of small farmers; the processing
of most of these crops continues to be very labor-
intensive, with potential to generate employment; and
(iv) except for tea, the production of perennials is con-
centrated in South India, mainly Kerala, where the
average per capita income is below national average.
3.        Project investments would cover:
-    planting of coconut in new areas;
-    rehabilitation including replanting of coconut;
-    intensive intercropping including cocoa and fodder
for dairy cattle under coconut;
1/   Including pepper - the pepper vine requires tree support.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
rehabilitation including replanting of pepper;
new planting and rehabilitation of cashew, and pilot plant
for cashew apple processing;
-    seed gardens for coconut, cashew, cocoa and spices;
-    crumb rubber factories for processing rubber produced
by smallholders; and
-    extension services, technical assistance, research and
training related to the foregoing activities.
Development Plans
4.        GOI and GOK have long recognized the need to improve yields and
help farmers by removing known handicaps and discovering others which impede
higher productivity. Efforts already made are described in Annex 1. The
agricultural development planned under this project encompasses supply of
improved planting materials, scientific management including fertilizer
application and plant protection, irrigation where available, systematic
and intensive intercropping, research and extension service, and provision
of credit to smallholders enabling them to undertake the development without
necessarily having to curtail their current level of consumption.
5.        In cashew development, however, it is planned also to demonstrate
the benefits of systematic cultivation which unwittingly has not been given
the proper attention that a high value crop of its type rightly deserves.
The new techniques to be adopted in cashew cultivation (Annex 4) are best
demonstrated by an institution with financial capacity and not by smallholders
with limited resources.
Project Location
6.        For the smallholder development, an area approach, on a package
system - each package unit covering 500 ha, has been adopted. Area selection
is based on special surveys carried out for this purpose by GOK. 1/ The more
important factors which governed the choice of project area locations were
availability of suitable land; incidence of plant diseases; senility and low
productivity of plantings; keenness of farmers to participate; receptiveness
by farmers to extension service advice; availability of adequate ground water
for irrigated development; and availability of contiguous areas suitable for
the proposed type of development.
7.        The project locations chosen on the foregoing considerations are:
-    for coconut new planting, 10 package units (5,000 ha) in Cannanore,
Kozhikode and Malappuram Districts;
1/   Bench mark survey carried out by the Kerala Department of Agriculture
in the Spring of 1976.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
for coconut rehabilitation, 75 package units (37,500 ha) 1/ in
Cannanore, Kozhikode, Malappuram and Trivandrum Districts in
areas free of root wilt disease; and
for pepper rehabilitation, 20 package units (10,000 ha) in
Cannanore, Idukki and Kottayam. 2/
8         The number of units proposed for the development under this project
is deliberately modest in relation to the total area cultivated under each of
these crops. In order to make the area approach and total development package
work, it is essential for the extension support to be adequate. A total of
105 package units was considered to be a comfortable target for this first
phase the success of which should provide the confidence needed for subsequent
larger scale activity and also to demonstrate to smallholders the need and the
mode to improve cultural practices and yields.
Project Phasing
9.        Conservative assumptions have been made in regard to GOK's capacity
to arrange for the recruitment and training of managerial and extension staff
of appropriate caliber in adequate numbers. Similarly due allowance has been
made for the probability that all participating farmers in a package unit of
500 ha would not respond spontaneously. It has been estimated that 25% of the
participating farmers in a package unit would respond in the initial year, 50%
in the following year and the remaining 25% in the third year of commencing
work in that package unit. GOK is capable of arranging staff and facilities
to commence work in 22 units in project year one, 55 in the year two and 28
in year 3 (Table 1).
10.       The smallholder development is planned as follows: 3/
1/   With an estimated 80% farmer response area rehabilitated would effectively
be 30,000 ha.
2/   Cashew development by Kerala State Plantations Corporation - 2,280 ha
for rehabilitation and 1,470 ha new planting, would be located in
the Cannanore District (Annex 4).
3/   See Annex 4 for phasing of cashew development.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
Coconut
New              Coconut            Pepper
Planting         Rehabilitation    Rehabilitation

Year 1                   250              1,500              625

Year 5                  375               2,000              625
Total            5,000             30,000           10,000
Coconut
11.       Despite an increase of about 50,000 ha in coconut planted area
between 1970 and 1975, total production has dropped from about 4,000 to
3,700 million nuts. Increased production is essential if India is to avoid
imports to satisfy the growing local demand for coconut oil. This could
be achieved by:
-    increasing the area under coconut, but in Kerala
little new land is available for coconut plantings;
rehabilitation of existing areas by adopting improved
agricultural practices including timely and regular
fertilization and plant protection operations offer
greater possibilities of increased yields over the
short run than new planting;
-    replanting with high yielding and disease resistant variety
seedlings to replace the senile and unproductive trees in
the existing coconut areas;
-    introduction of irrigation facilities where ground water
availability has been proved; and
-    systematic intercropping with high value crops so as to support
new investments to be undertaken by the farmer and to afford
him a better standard of living; cocoa and fodder could be
grown profitably in areas where irrigation facilities
could be made available.
12.       Thus the strategy for coconut development proposed under this pro-
ject is a combination of all foregoing aspects (details in Annex 3).
Irrigation and Energization of Pumpsets
13.       Kerala in general receives precipitation from the South-West as
well as North-East monsoons, and rainfall is spread over six to seven months'



ANNEX 2
Page 5
of the year. However, there are many parts in the State, specially in the
north, which suffer from drought. It is easier to establish the new coconut
plants where irrigation is available for the dry months. At present farmers
are obliged to resort to pot watering carrying the water from far off wells.
Irrigation is a major factor which would contribute to significant increases
in nut production. Increases averaging 50% have been reported in trials and
field plantings. Intensive intercropping is also possible if irrigation is
available.
14.       GOK has already carried out surveys of ground water resources in the
project areas based on which it is estimated that a minimum of 20% of the new
coconut planting areas and 25% of coconut rehabilitation areas would have
adequate ground water suitable for irrigating the coconut and the intercrops
to be raised in an intensive manner. The irrigation component provided under
the project includes shallow wells and filter points together with electrically
operated pumpsets. It is estimated that provision of shallow well irrigation
to coconut land would be financially viable only for a holding of 1 ha or
more. Filter point installation would, however, be viable even with 0.5 ha of
coconut land.
Cocoa
15.       The soil and climatic conditions in some of the project areas are
suited for cocoa cultivation. There is a known unsatisfied demand for cocoa,
and the satisfied demand is met mainly from imports (Annex 13). Cocoa can be
developed as a major commercial crop without significant additional labor
inputs by growing it as an intercrop under coconut which will provide the
necessary shade for the cocoa plant.
Dairy
16.       Another means of increasing the farmer's income from his limited
landholding is to grow fodder as an intercrop for feeding dairy cattle. Milk
consumption in Kerala is much below the 210 grams per adult per day recommended
by WHO. A program to raise cattle by the coconut farmer would also demonstrate
to the farming community in general the economics of milk production and the
need for greater milk consumption. For these reasons, among others, cocoa and
fodder are to be promoted as the major intercrops.
Pepper
17.       Pepper in Kerala has seldom been grown as a pure crop.  It is grown
mostly as a mixed crop in coffee and cardamon areas. Poor yields are the
result of senile and uneconomic vines, wilt disease, and inadequate extension
staff to persuade farmers to adopt package practices and scientific cultivation
methods recommended by KDOA. An intensive area approach would be followed and
project farmers encouraged to grow pepper as a monocrop (Annex 3).



ANNEX 2
Page 6
Cashew
18.       Cashew also is a smallholder crop in Kerala and increased produc-
tion to meet import deficit of rawnuts could be achieved by encouraging the
smallholder farmers. GOK is paying considerable attention to promoting cashew
cultivation, both new plantings and rehabilitation of existing plantings by
smallholders by provision of subsidy of Rs 100/ha for fertilizers. Project
development, however, is restricted to two plantations - one for rehabilitation
and the other for establishing new plantings.
19.       In the past cashew in India was treated as a wasteland crop.  Sys-
tematic management is even more important than physical inputs. GOK has
recognized that the inherent poor quality of the planting material has also
been responsible for the low yields in the older plantings. Research has
proved that improved farm techniques such as cultural, manurial and plant
protection treatments are efficacious in increasing the production from the
plantings in bearing. Private holdings available for new planting are limited
and scattered, and those available for new plantings could often be brought
under other crops. Therefore expansion of cashew area in any significant
manner has to come from forest lands and the GOK Forest Department has
reserved lands for this purpose.
20.       Thus the rehabilitation of the GOK's cashew plantation of 2,280
ha will also serve to demonstrate to smallholders quick improvement in yield
to be achieved by scientific management. The new planting of 1,470 ha virgin
land earmarked by the Forest Department for cashew would not only serve as
the first phase in an increase of cashew area but also to demonstrate the
advantages of treating the crop with the same care given to other tree crops,
starting from good planting material and extending to fertilizer application
and pest control. Provision has also been made for a pilot plant to process
cashew apple into wine, beverage and preserves. Vast quantities of cashew
apple are now not put to any economic use. Cashew Development is discussed
further in Annex 4.
Seed Gardens
21.       Poor quality of planting material used for almost all crops in the
State has been recognized by GOK as a major factor contributing to low pro-
ductivity and low resistance to diseases. GOK is making arrangements for
making adequate quantity and acceptable quality of planting material necessary
for the new planting and replanting of coconut, pepper, cashew, and cocoa
planned under this project. However, the future seed needs of the Kerala
State outside the project activities are so great that funds are provided in
this project for seed gardens to be established. These gardens would produce
high yielding and disease resistant varieties of planting material for coconut,
cocoa, cashew, pepper and other spices (details in Annex 5).



ANNEX 2
Page 7
Rubber Processing
22.       Rubber, like other tree crops in Kerala, is grown predominantly by
smallholders. Few smallholders possess the essential processing facilities to
produce quality rubber. The majority of them continue to follow crude and un-
hygienic methods to process their latex. About 20% of their production now is
sold as scrap to middlemen at very low prices. Rubber yields are low despite
the remarkable improvement between 365 kg/ha of 1961 and the present 765 kg/ha.
Due to the increase in total planted area of more than 50% over the last ten
years coupled with the intensified extension service, and special replanting
programs under the Rubber Replanting Subsidy Scheme, rubber production in the
State is expected to increase two-fold within the next ten years. Additional
processing capacity has, therefore, to be provided for this increase. The
Indian Rubber Board has thus planned to encourage the setting up of crumb
rubber processing factories to meet part of the future increased processing
capacity required. Crumb rubber processing could give the maximum benefit
from upgrading smallholders' scrap rubber.  This move is also welcomed by the
Indian tire manufacturers who are the major consumers of this raw material.
23.       In order to maximize the benefits to the smallholders the crumb
rubber produced in the new factories would use more (about 75%) of scrap grade
rubber and less field latex. This would enable a steady market for the small-
holders' scrap grades. Upgrading the scrap grades into technically specified
grades would enable higher prices to be realized by the smallholders. The
processing and marketing of the crumb rubber would be entrusted to the Co-
operative sector so as to enable the smallholder to realize a sense of par-
ticipation in the new development, and also to share in the profits in the
processing and marketing of his rubber produce (details in Annex 7).
Research, Training and Technical Assistance
24.       The Kerala Agricultural University (AUK) and Central Plantation Crops
Research Institute (CPCRI) have been doing research on many aspects of project
crops particularly on coconut, cocoa, pepper and cashew. But the research
input on these crops has been meager considering the importance of these crops
in the national economy. In order to tackle the various problems concerning
the production of these crops, research efforts have to be intensified so as
to provide the necessary research support for the successful implementation
not only of the development proposed under this project but more importantly
also of the massive future development planned by the State Government.
25.       CPCRI and AUK would also provide technical training to extension
service staff required for the project. The Special Agricultural Development
Unit would arrange for project orientation and on-the-job training of project
staff to be engaged on agricultural development. Overseas training and fel-
lowships would be provided where appropriate facilities are not available
locally. Technical assistance support too has been provided to the extent it
is considered essential for maximizing project benefits. Details are given in
Annex 6 for agricultural development, and in Annex 7 for rubber processing.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
Extension Services
26.       Intensive technical support is considered crucial for the successful
implementation of development to be undertaken by smallholders. Special care
has therefore been taken to provide adequate extension services to help pro-
ject farmers. These facilities would supplement the services presently pro-
vided by KDOA and the Department of Animal Husbandry. The extension services
approach planned under this project would be similar to that followed by KDOA
in the Intensive Package Programs for coconut and paddy commenced in 1973/74
(Annex 1, paras 12 and 24). Veterinary Surgeons and Livestock Assistants who
are to help farmers also engaged in dairy would be posted in addition to the
Agricultural Officers. Provision has also been made in the project for
vehicles for staff, low cost buildings in the project areas for administration
offices, and spraying equipment for plant protection campaigns. Further
details including extension organization and management are in Annex 10.

INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Smallholders' Farm Development
Introduction
1.        Development of the coconut and pepper areas planned under this
project would be undertaken entirely by smallholders. Intercropping is
envisaged in the 5,000 ha to be newly brought under coconut and in the
30,000 ha 1/ existing coconut areas to be rehabilitated. Irrigated develop-
ment would be confined to about 1,000 ha of the new coconut areas (20%) and
7,500 ha of the rehabilitation areas (25%). Intercropping with cocoa and
fodder grass is planned only under mature coconut. No special irrigation
facilities or intercropping programs are recommended in the 10,000 ha pepper
areas to be rehabilitated. Average land holdings in the project areas are
estimated at 0.4 ha for coconut and 0.5 ha for pepper. It is estimated that
about 75,000 farming families would participate in the project engaging in
coconut, pepper, cocoa and dairy development. The more important assumptions
used in the farm models (see attached Tables) are summarized in the Appendix
to this Annex.
Coconut New Planting
2.        Coconut new planting would cover 10 package units totalling 5,000 ha
20% of which or 1,000 ha would be irrigated development. Suitable areas free
of root wilt disease have already been identified by GOK in CannanoVe, Kozhi-
kode and Malappuram Districts. A planting intensity of 175 trees/ha would
be followed to permit systematic intercropping. Seedlings from West-Coast-Tall
varieties and Laccadive ordinary and hybrid seedlings (T x D and D x T) would
be supplied from KDOA nurseries.
3.        Cultivation practices would generally be in accordance with the
package of practices recommended by KDOA. Annual application to trees from
three years of age onwards would normally be 0.34 N, 0.17 P205 and 0.68 K20.
For earlier years the dosage would be as follows:
1/   80% anticipated response from a total area of about 37,500 ha.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
3 months after planting              -  1/10th the full dose
mentioned above
1 year         - do -                -  1/3rd       "
2 year         - do-                 -  2/3rd       "
Organic manure at the rate of 25 - 30 kg/mature tree would also be applied.
Two annual sprays with fungicide against leaf disease and packing of leaf
axil in the tree crown with a mixture of sand and insecticide to ward off
Rhinoceros beetle, would be recommended. Intercropping would be undertaken
with tapioca, banana, pulses, vegetable and ginger. Cocoa and green fodder
are not recommended in the new coconut plantings and would be restricted to
mature areas to be rehabilitated.
Coconut Rehabilitation
4.        Bench-mark surveys carried out by GOK have shown that 80% of the
farmers in the 75 package units identified would participate in the program,
giving an effective rehabilitation area of 30,000 ha - an average of 400 ha
in each package unit. Irrigated development would be 7,500 - 25% of the
30,000 ha area effectively rehabilitated. An average of 175 trees/ha,
all of high vitality and yield potential would be achieved by a combination
of selective thinning, replanting, replacement of senile and unproductive
trees, and following improved cultural practices. It is estimated that on
an average the farmers would replant 20 trees/ha rehabilitated.
5.        As in coconut new plantings, quality seedlings of selected varie-
ties would be used for replacement of uneconomic trees in the areas to be
rehabilitated. The KDOA's schemes for production of quality coconut seedlings
would be intensified to ensure timely supply of the seedlings required for
the replanting necessary in the rehabilitation areas. Cultural practices
would be in accordance with the package of practices recommended by KDOA.
2 kg dolomite or in its place, 0.5 kg Magnesium sulphate. and 1 kg lime per
tree would be applied in addition to N, P, K doses recommended. Organic
fertilizers would be used whenever possible, particularly in the initial
period of the rehabilitation and in soils which are low in organic content.
Plant protection measures would be as recommended for coconut new plantings
(para 3).
6.        Cocoa performs best under well distributed rainfall with short
dry seasons. In the coconut growing tracts of Kerala, including the project
areas, the dry seasons would be too severe for cocoa unless irrigation could
be provided. Cocoa is a shade tolerant plant and could adapt well under
mature coconut trees provided soil fertility could be assured. Intercropping
of cocoa under mature coconut has proved to be successful in countries like
New Guinea and Malaysia. In Kerala too cocoa has already been planted under



ANNEX 3
Page 3
coconut and, as a mixed crop, with arecanut with promising results. Interplant-
ing of cocoa would be limited to mature coconut areas with irrigation facili-
ties. Only holdings in which the stand of trees would not exceed 175/ha
would be chosen for interplanting with cocoa. Identification of holding for
this purpose would be done by the project extension staff. Continuous
and compact areas would be developed for this inter-crop to facilitate process-
ing which would be developed through cooperative efforts of the farmers.
7.        Planting material used would be of Forestero material originating
from seeds imported from Sabah in East Malaysia. Cocoa planting would be in
single rows, between the coconut rows, at an intensity of about 300 plants/ha.
The double row method of planting, between alternate rows of coconuts,
would also be permitted. In gardens where line planting of coconut has not
been followed, cocoa interplanting would be in suitable interspaces according
to recommendations to be made by the extension services. Agricultural manage-
ment and fertilizer practices would similarly conform to extension service
recommendations. At planting time organic manure would be applied at the rate
of 10 tons/ha. From the second year onwards each plant would, in addition to
being mulched with compost, receive 1 kg/year of 10:4:14 NPK mixture. It is
claimed by GOK and CPCRI that black pod has been absent or relatively infre-
quent in Kerala. Relative humidity in Kerala with its long dry seasons is
known to be much lower than in other cocoa producing countries. Even with
irrigation the climatic conditions in the project areas are less conducive
to outbreaks of black pod which could be kept within tolerable limits.
However, adequate preventive control measures would be taken.
8.        Dairy development in coconut holdings would also form an integral
part of the rehabilitation work. In Kerala, in more than 20% of the farm
holdings dairy cattle are raised. Mixed farming under the project would be
encouraged only where irrigation facilities could be provided. Improved
varieties of fodder grasses would be interplanted, preferably mixed with
suitable fodder legumes, in coconut gardens. Facilities for upgrading cows
now owned by the farmers would also be provided. Hybrid Napier or Guinea
grass mixed with Stylosanthis gracilis, Peuraria phaseoloides or cowpea would
be planted. The number of cows raised on each farm would be at the rate of
three cows for every one ha of the interplanted (fodder) area. The choice of
varieties of fodder grasses and legumes, the breed of the cow and the number
of cows for each holding would be in accordance with extension staff recom-
mendations. Supply of planting material (for fodder crops) and improved
breeds of cattle, insemination dispensary, and other services would be ensured
to the project by coordinating the program with the activities of the Indo-
Swiss Project at Mattupatty and the Cattle Development Programs implemented by
the Departments of Animal Husbandry and Dairy Development. Grass-slips would
be planted at a spacing of 40-50 cm and in between rows of grass, legume seeds



ANNEX 3
Page 4
would be sown using a seed rate of 10-12 kg/ha. Organic manure at 20 tons/ha
and inorganic fertilizers at the rate of 50 kg P205 and 50 kg K20 would be
applied as basal dressing at the time of planting. After each cutting, cattle
dung, and 18-1/2 kg N as Urea or Ammonium Sulphate would be applied to the
grass.  In addition, 50 kg of P205 and 100 kg of K02 would also be applied as
top dressing in two split doses, one before and one soon after the monsoon.
9.        Trials carried out by CPCRI, KDOA and the Indo-Swiss Project over
five years have demonstrated that one ha of well managed fodder under coconut
under irrigated conditions in Kerala can provide up to 60 tons of green matter
which could maintain five cows. In the project, however, only 80% 1/ of the
coconut area is assumed to be interplanted in fodder grass and a conservative
yield of 30 tons of fodder taken. As the cows would also receive concentrate
feed, 10 tons of green fodder is considered adequate to maintain one milking
cow. Thus, three cows are taken to be the average number raised in a 1 ha
holding. Cattle would be made available by the Indo-Swiss Project supplement-
ed by upgraded local cattle available in Kerala and neighboring States of
Tamil Nadu and Karnataka.
10.       The irrigation component has been provided only for the coconut
area development and would cover a total area of 8,500 ha - 1,000 ha in the
new plantings and 7,500 ha in the rehabilitation areas. Investments would
cover new dug wells, deepening and repair of existing wells, filter points
in sandy soils, supply and installation of pumpsets which would be electrically
powered. The smaller holdings would have 1.5 hp pumps installed. 3.5 hp
pumps would be used only in holdings of more than 1 ha.
Pepper Rehabilitation
11.       Pepper rehabilitation component would cover 10,000 ha of pepper
gardens in the Districts of Cannanore, Kottayam and Idukki. In the latter
two Districts the rehabilitation work would mostly be confined to high range
areas where diseases of pepper are found to be serious. The pepper rehabili-
tation would also be on the "Intensive Package Unit" approach in compact
units of 500 ha each. The activities would broadly include:
-    replanting of 50% of the vines with superior types;
-    application of fertilizers to both the existing and
the new planted vines;
-    adoption of plant protection measures to both the
existing and the new planted vines; and
-    improvement of cultivation practices in general.
1/   For cocoa and fodder interplanting it is assumed that the farmer will want
to reserve 20% of his land for minor intercrop such as tapioca and
vegetable.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
12.       For replanting, rooted cuttings of the hybrid pepper Panniyur-I and
accredited conventional varieties like Karimunda, Kalluvally and Kottanadan
would be used. The latter types would be preferred for less open situations.
Planting material required would be made available through the schemes for
multiplication and distribution of rooted pepper cuttings implemented by KDOA.
Organic manure would be applied at the rate of 9 kg per pit with 0.5 kg of lime.
Inorganic fertilizers to supply 100 gm N, 40 gm P 20, and 140 gm K 0 per vine
would also be applied. In the 50% vines retained in the garden this dosage
would be an annual maintenance application, while the new planted vines would
receive an increasing schedule according to extension service recommendations.
Both the retained and the replanted vines would receive plant protection
treatments. Plant protection schedule for a particular area would be decided
by the extension officers by reference to the pest or disease situation in
that area and in conformity with the package of practices recommended by
KDOA.

KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Smallholder Farm Development
In preparing the accompanying Farm Model Tables the following
major assumptions have been made:
i.   Land holdings used for different kinds of project
development are:
0.5 ha for unirrigated coconut new planting and
rehabilitation; and
1.0 ha for irrigated coconut new planting and
rehabilitation, and for pepper rehabilitation. 1/
ii.   20% of the 5,000 ha coconut new plantings and 25% of the
30,000 ha rehabilitation areas would be provided with
irrigation facilities.
iii.   Intercropping with cocoa, and fodder for dairy would be
confined to irrigated holdings in the rehabilitation areas.
Only 80% of each irrigated holding would be devoted to
cocoa or fodder, leaving the remaining 20% available for
other intercrops.
iv.   Other intercrops raised in all coconut areas would in the
main be tapioca, pulses, ginger and vegetables.
v.   No intercropping would be carried out in pepper rehabili-
tation areas;
vi.   Ideal tree stand for coconut new planting and rehabili-
tated area is 175/ha;
vii.   Casualty rate for coconut seedlings planted would be 10%
to be replaced in the year after planting;
1/   1.0 ha would be the minimum holding necessary for a new well with 3.0 hp
pumpset to be financially viable. The minimum size for filter point and
1.5 hp pump would be 0.5 hp.



ANNEX 3
Appendix 1
Page 2
viii.   Only hired labor is deemed part of project costs.  In
coconut new planting and pepper replanting operations
two-thirds of the first year's labor input is hired.
In all other cases, hired labor is taken at one-third
of total required.
ix.   Daily wage is taken at Rs 8.00 for men and Rs 6.50 for
women, being the rates specified in the State minimum
wage legislation.
x.   Coconut harvesting labor is all hired and computed at
Rs 16.00 plus 8 nuts for every 100 trees harvested.
xi.   Capital and operating costs relating to irrigation
facilities with 3.0 hp pumpset are as given in Appendix
2 of this Annex.
xii.   Three cows are raised in 1 ha of irrigated holding; the
calving rate is 0.75; and the average daily milk pro-
duction is 3.7 liters per cow.
xiii.   Crop yields and commodity prices are given in Annex 13,
Tables 3 and 1.

KERALA AGRIGULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Irrigation Costs for Smallholder Farm Development
1.        Ground water surveys carried out by GOK, 20% of the coconut net
planting areas and 25% of the rehabilitation areas would be provided with
irrigation. Furthermore, sandy soils prevailing in about half the irri-
gable areas would permit low cost irrigation from filter points. In the
other half of the area new wells would be dug.
2.        Average land holding to receive a filter point was taken at 0.6 ha
(the minimum viable size being about 0.5 ha) for which a 1.5 hp pump would
be provided. Averaging land holding where a new well would be dug is likely
to be 1.5 ha (the minimum viable size being about 1.0 ha for which a 3.0 hp
pump would be provided. Detailed investment costs for both irrigation sets
are in the Table on page 2. The same Table gives the operating costs for
the bigger set used in the farm models (Tables 2, 4 and 5).
3.        Total irrigation costs for Smallholder Farm Development sub-project
are made up as follows:

ANNEX 4
Page 1
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Cashew Development
Introduction
1.        The cashew tree requires well drained soil and warm humid climate.
However, it is a hardy tree that grows even on wastelands of low fertility.
It can be grown in almost all soil types from sandy sea coast to laterite hill
slopes up to an elevation of 700 m above sea level. Cashew grows in areas
where rainfall is more than 600 mm. Roots of cashew trees penetrate through
thick layers of laterite gravel, as long as the soil between the gravel is
loose, but compacted soils and hardpans limit root development and thus
availability of soil moisture. The tree has been used a lot for erosion
control. Because it is hardy and highly drought resistant, capable of thriv-
ing in wastelands where no other crop can grow, it is generally considered
that the tree needs very little treatment and should be grown on the poorest
of soils. The potential production capacity of cashew on good soils is
practically unknown, though trials carried out in other countries have shown
that cashew responds very well to fertilizer treatment. The main product is
the kernel, a popular dessert nut. The nuts are attached to the so-called
cashew "apples" the swollen peduncle.
2.        Cashew in Kerala, extending to more than 105,000 ha has hitherto
been essentially a smallholder crop and individual growers have paid little
attention to this valuable crol. While the inherent poor quality of the
planting materials used during the earlier years has been mostly respon-
sible for the low yields in the older plantations, experience of scientific
cashew cultivation indicates that improved farm techniques, such as cultural,
manurial and plant protection practices, are very effective in increasing the
nut production from the bearing plantations. Production could be increased
merely by undertaking a campaign of prophylactic spraying against pests and
diseases. It is estimated that at least 40% of the crop lost annually due to
incidence of pests and diseases could be saved by such plant protection.
Project Proposals
3.        In contrast to the coconut and pepper development under the project,
which would be in the smallholder sector, the cashew component would be
developed on a plantation scale by the Kerala State Plantations Corporation
(KSPC). Development would be of three parts:



ANNEX 4
Page 2
rehabilitation of an existing plantation in Cannanore;
a new plantation in virgin jungle land also in Cannanore;
-    pilot plant for cashew apple processing.
The rehabilitation program would demonstrate the financial viability of
cultivating this crop on a scientific basis. The new plantation would,
in addition to serving as an increase in the cashew area so necessary to
expand production of raw nuts, demonstrate the benefits of treating the cashew
crop with inputs and care similar to those given to other commercial crops.
The pilot plant for apple processing would serve to establish economic uses
for cashew apple, production of which is estimated at more than one million
tons and is now largely wasted.
Cashew Rehabilitation
4.        KSPC would take over the cashew plantation of 2,280 ha in the Cannanore
District now owned and operated by KDOA. Mainly due to government budgetary
constraints this plantation was neglected and starved of essential maintenance
expenditure. KSPC is not constrained by normal government budgetary procedures.
Since it is an autonomous corporation it is in a position to work on a self
financing basis. KSPC would rehabilitate it applying scientific management
practices including:
-    replanting of 250 ha with HYV to replace uneconomic
trees;
-    adequate fertilizer application; and
-    regular sprayings against pests and diseases.
New Plantings
5.        New plantings of cashew would be on about 1,500 ha of land to be
made available by the Forest Department. Cultivation of these new plantings
would be based on the most up-to-date production techniques.
6.        For both replantings and new plantings, the planting material used
would be selected only from trees possessing the desirable characteristics -
high yields, tolerance to disease, short flowering period, compact pinnacles,
high percentage of perfect flowers, good fruit-set, and large and uniform size
nuts. Plantings would be at an intensity of 200 plants per ha. A system of
adaptive thinning would be followed in later years to ensure adequate spacing
between trees, depending on the canopy growth of each tree. Fertilizer
application would be in accordance with the following recommendations made
by KDOA, subject to modifications warranted by soil analysis.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
N gm     P2 5gm       K2O gm
1st year/tree          80         40         40
2nd year/tree         160         80         80
3rd year/tree         250        125        125
Plant protection measures, principally against Helopeltis antonii (Tea
mosquitoe) would be undertaken. A minimum of three rounds of spraying
at the pre-flowering flush and inflorescence stages would be done.
Pilot Plant for Cashew Apple Processing
7.        Cashew apple is a by-product of the cashew industry.  From this
juicy apple it is possible to make many edible products, such as jam, juice,
wine, spirits and fruitcake. It is reported that in the Cannanore District
itself some 250,000 tons of fruit could be made available if some viable
industries could be set up to use this commodity as raw material. The sea-
sonal nature of fruit availability and its perishable nature require care-
ful studies before setting up units of commercial size.
8.        The proposal is to set up a pilot plant for making cashew
apple wine using a process already evolved by the Central Food Technological
Research Institute in Mysore. The quality of the wine produced under this
process is reported to be superior to Brazilian wine prepared out of cashew
apples. The plant capacity would be for processing 4 tons of fruit per day.
An integrated process for the preparation of jam, juice concentrate, candy
and chutney has also been developed by the Mysore Institute and arrangements
would be made to adopt this process in the pilot plant. The plant would
be located in the Cannanore District in one of the main cashew cultivation
centers, such as Hosdurg, Kasaragod or Taliparamba. Since cashew is a seasonal
crop, the plant could work on cashew apple at the most for three months of the
year. For the remaining months suitable alternatives (fruit and vegetables)
would be found to establish means of better utilization of processing plant
capacity. The apple residue left after extraction of the juice could be used
as cattle feed. The pilot plant production would be marketed by the new Proj-
ect Department of KSPC (para 9).
Organization and Management
9.        KSPC was set up a new Project Department within its existing
legal structure with specific responsibility for implementing the cashew
development sub-project. The Department would be headed by a full-time
director of high caliber. He would be directly responsible to the Managing
Director of KSPC and assisted by three deputies: a technically qualified
Plantation Superintendent who would be directly responsible for managing
the two plantations; an Industrial Assistant to be in charge of pilot
project operations; and a Finance and Administrative Assistant to be res-
ponsible for finance, procurement, marketing and administrative functions.
The Superintendent would be resident on the new plantation, supported by an



ANNEX 4
Page 4
Assistant Superintendent, making periodic visits to the plantation where
rehabilitation activities are planned. The latter unit would be placed in
the control of another Assistant Superintendent. Field operations would be
supervised by Field Assistants who would each be expected to supervise day-
to-day operations on about 500 ha. Suitable arrangements would be made to
provide social amenities for the supervisory staff, the laborers and their
families. An organization of the cashew development subproject is given in
the attached Chart. Capital and operating costs for new plantation, rehab-
ilitation, and pilot project activities are given in the accompanying Tables.

ANNEX 5
Page 1
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Seed Gardens
General
1.        The Kerala Department of Agriculture (KDOA), having recognized the
importance of high quality planting material, has set up several nurseries,
particularly for coconut, cashew, and pepper. However, so far no agency
has set up a seed multiplication project for plantation crops. 1/ This sub-
project is therefore designed to meet the future needs of HYV, disease tolerant/
resistant and otherwise superior quality seed for the large areas of plantation
crops remaining to be replanted and for the new areas to be brought under
cultivation. GOK has decided that KDOA should undertake seed multiplication in
a government-owned farm for coconut, cashew, cocoa, pepper and other important
spices. GOK has already identified more than 500 ha of land suitable for this
purpose. The effective area under seed garden is, however, expected to be
about 435 ha.
Coconut
2.        The coconut seed garden would be established in 300 ha and planted
with selected superior dwarf variety coconut to serve as mother trees.
One-half of the area would be planted with local dwarf (preferably Chavakkad
yellow and Gangabondam) and the other half with imported dwarfs - preference
being given to Malayan Orange or Red. Sufficient number of seedlings of
West Coast Tall variety from identified pre-potent trees would also be planted
to be used as pollen parents. Pollination would be assisted using selected
parents. During the pre-project period suitable sites and sources of supply
for local dwarfs and progenies of pre-potent trees would be located and
negotiations entered into regarding the import of dwarfs. GOK has estimated
its future annual requirements of planting material for a replanting program
of 1.5% of existing areas, and for planned area expansion at about 6 million
nuts. Existing sources in the State could supply nuts for about 1.5 million
seedlings. The proposed seed garden would provide 1 million nuts in project
year eight, 2 million by year eleven, and 2.75 million in year fifteen
when all 300 ha would be in full production. Production from this garden
would also be supplemented from State Farms Corporation garden of 200 ha now
being established.
Cashew
3.        A cashew seed garden in 50 ha would be established from superior
high-yielding cultivars identified in the country. As far as possible vege-
tatively propagated clonal material would be used. High density planting
1/ The State Farms Corporation of India has made arrangements, only recently,
to enter into seed production for plantation crops on a limited scale.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
(preferably through hedge system of planting) would be adopted for the garden
to facilitate selective thinning of substandard types and ultimately retain-
ing only superior high yielding progenies in the garden. This seed garden
which would be planted in HYV material, adequately fertilized, and efficiently
managed, should yield about 2,000 kg nuts per ha when in full production.
Assuming that only two-thirds of such production would be suitable for use as
seeds, 1,335 kg would be available. In Kerala, one kg of seeds is known to
yield 60 seed nuts. Thus 1 ha can provide about 80,000 seeds, adequate
to plant 400 ha. The seed garden of 50 ha would therefore be capable of
producing annually seeds for about 20,000 ha replantings and new plantings.
At full production, the output from these gardens should make it possible
for GOK to offer surplus seed nuts to the other cashew growing States in
India.
Pepper
4.        Pepper is propagated best from cuttings of the runner shoots which
originate at the base of the vines. Cuttings would be collected only from
high yielding and healthy vines possessing desirable characters. Rooted
cuttings both for new planting and replanting programs under the project would
be supplied mostly from Panniyur I and accredited older varieties of Karumunda,
Kalluvally and Kottanadam. In Kerala the total area now under pepper is about
120,000 ha and a replanting program of 3% would represent an area of about
3,600 ha to be replanted annually. This area at an intensity of 3,300 vines
per ha would require about 12.0 million rooted cuttings for planting. The
present production capacity in the State is estimated at 1 million cuttings.
This could be expected to increase to 2.5 million cuttings in the near future.
One ha of mature pepper has an annual production potential of 100,000 cuttings.
The 60 ha garden planned by the State Farms Corporation could in due course
produce 6 million cuttings, leaving a shortfall of 3.5 million cuttings to be
provided. Thus, an additional 35 ha under the project should be adequate to
fill the gap of 3.5 million cuttings.
Clove and Nutmeg
5.        Seeds of clove and nutmeg are difficult to obtain and the demand
from smallholder farmers is on the increase. These seeds, especially cloves,
deteriorate very quickly. Under the Central Nursery Scheme sponsored by GOI
in its Fifth Five Year Plan about 500,000 seedlings are planned to be produced
in Kerala, Karnataka and Tamil Nadu. As the actual demand from farmers is
expected to be far in excess of this planned supply, tree spice seed gardens
would be established under the proposed project on 25 ha.
Cocoa
8.        Since farmers are being encouraged by GOK to interplant cocoa
under irrigated coconut, there is a growing demand for disease resistant
planting material. Improved Forestero types, particularly of Amelanado



ANNEX 5
Page 3
parents, would be planted in the cocoa seed garden. As the total extent of
lands to be brought under cocoa is not known with any certainty now, only
20 ha has been provided for the cocoa seed garden.
Phasing
9.        The establishment of the seed gardens would be phased as indicated
below:
Project Year/a          2    3    4       Total
-------- ha ------------
Coconut                                 100  200   -        300
Cashew                                   50   -    -         50
Pepper                                   35   -    -         35
Cloves                                    3   3    4         10
Nutmeg                                    5   5   10         20
Cocoa                                     5   5   10         20
Total                                                  435
La   No plantings planned for Project Year 1.
The capital and operating costs of the various seed gardens are summarized
in Tables 1 and 2.
Organization and Management
10.       Seed gardens would be a distinct administrative entity, under the
Special Agricultural Development Unit, with exclusive staff. The chief
executive of this sub-project would be an officer at the level of Joint
Director of Agriculture. As seed garden work, particularly hybridization
operations in coconut, would involve large scale employment of labor, an
officer at Joint Director level is considered necessary. Assisted by a Farm
Superintendent who would be in direct charge of operations in the coconut seed
garden, he would also coordinate and supervise the activities in all other
gardens. He would be assisted by another technically specialized officer at
the level of Deputy Director of Agriculture to be in direct charge of cashew,
pepper, tree spices and cocoa gardens. Also attached would be Junior Agricul-
tural Officers and Demonstrators specialized in the different crops and
support staff for administrative and financial functions. Details of staffing
for the seed gardens are shown in Table 3.
