INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Research and Training
Introduction
1.        Agricultural research in India relating to project crops - coconut,
cocoa, cashew, pepper and intercrops - is carried out by the Agricultural
Universities and by units of the Indian Council of Agricultural Research
(ICAR). In Kerala, this research has been carried out since 1972 by the
Agricultural University of Kerala (AUK) and the Central Plantation Crops
Research Institute (CPCRI) which is a unit of ICAR.
Agricultural University of Kerala
2.        The following State research units are now responsible to AUK:
-    three Coconut Research Stations and three Coconut Sub-
stations;
-    one Rice Research Station and four Rice Sub-Stations;
-    two Agronomic and one Horticultural Research Station;
-    two Pepper Research Stations;
-    one Cashew Research Station;
-    one Banana and Pineapple Research Station;
-    one Lemongrass Research Station; and
-    one Cardamom Research Station.
AUK has three constituent Colleges - College of Agriculture, College of
Horticulture and College of Veterinary and Animal Sciences. AUK programs are
of applied nature with reference to the particular agroclimatic conditions of
Kerala. Apart from carrying out experiments in its various research stations,
AUK undertakes numerous adaptive trials in cultivators' gardens.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
The Central Plantation Crops Research Institute (CPCRI)
3.        CPCRI was established in 1970 as a national research institution
under ICAR and financed by GOI. It was formed by the amalgamation of the
Central Coconut Research Stations at Kasaragod and Kayamkulam (Kerala), the
Central Arecanut Research Station, Vittal (Karnataka) and its five regional
stations. Its headquarters are in Kasaragod; the Pepper Research Station at
Taliparamba in Cannanore District has been recently transferred to the CPCRI.
A regional research station for spices has been recently set up in Kozhikode
District but due to financial constraints it still has very little staff and
equipment. Sub-stations have also been set up in different agro-climatic
zones. CPCRI is conducting research on coconut, arecanut, cocoa, oil palm,
cashew and the major spices. The concentration of efforts, however, is on
coconut research. Variety collections are maintained of coconut, cashew and
pepper. Much work is done on selection of suitable crosses for hybrids, with
emphasis on the root-wilt tolerance of these hybrids. Research is also
carried out on intercropping of coconut with grass, vegetables, cocoa, etc.
In the studies on pests and diseases, special attention is paid to the root-
wilt disease of coconut, particularly at the Station Kayamkulam. In pepper
cultivation the development of control methods for quick wilt and slow wilt
and the pollu disease is an important part of the program. The research
programs of the AUK and the CPCRI are complementary and implemented with close
cooperation and coordination.
4.        Besides conducting and guiding research in the different disciplines
related to plantation crops, CPCRI coordinates the research work on planta-
tion crops done by other Institutes, Universities and State Departments of
Agriculture. It also serves as a center of information in the country on
all matters relating to plantation crops.
5.        CPCRI specializes in basic research with concentration on diseases,
whereas AUK specializes on adaptive research with emphasis on agro-techniques.
Both institutions also undertake training programs aimed at the farmer, both
directly and through GOK's extension service staff. These institutions have
achieved commendable results through their past activities, particularly on
coconut, cashew, pepper and intercropping. But the research input on these
crops has been small in relation to their importance in the national economy.
Research efforts have therefore to be intensified to provide the necessary
support not only for the project program but also for the ambitious agricul-
tural development programs planned by GOK. Provisions have been made in the
Fifth Five Year Plan to enhance the research inputs. However, supplementary
facilities were considered essential to support the development proposed under
this project.
Project Proposals
6.        The research programs of AUK and CPCRI which would be supported by
the funds provided under this project are summarized in the Appendix. Provi-
sion includes research and support staff, fellowship and training overseas,



ANNEX 6
Page 3
laboratory facilities, transport and technical assistance in specialized
fields. Project costs are shown in Table I. Effective implementation of the
programs listed in Appendix would require additional laboratory facilities and
more testing equipment, especially the more sophisticated equipment like the
electronmicroscope required for root-wilt disease research (Tables 2 and 3).
Funds for employing additional research and teaching staff considered absolutely
essential have also been provided (Table 4). To enable AUK to undertake
surveys and conduct research in the plantings in the project area, it is
necessary to provide transport facilities. Seven vehicles - I car, 2 vans and
4 four-wheel drive vehicles - have been included for this purpose.
Training and Technical Assistance
7.        AUK and CPCRI would continue to provide in-house training to
farmers and extension service staff.  Some AUK and CPCRI staff too would
themselves require overseas training in some research fields and in the use
of sophisticated equipment required for specialized investigations envisaged.
The services of experts in the fields of Nematology, Pathology and Micro-
biology for a total of 18 man-months would be obtained to assist in the work
carried out by CPCRI.
8.       Training and technical assistance aspects relating to the Rubber
Processing sub-project are described in Annex 7.

Appendix 1
Page I
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Research and Training
Future Programs Requiring Support
1.        Funds provided in the project would strengthen the following research
programs carried out by the Agricultural University of Kerala (AUK) and the
Central Plantation Crops Research Institute (CPCRI) in respect of the major
project crops.
2.        Coconut - AUK Program
(a) Survey of gardens to find out superior types, particularly
those which show resistance to major diseases and pests.
(b) Ascertain the best criteria for selecting the hybrid seed-
lings in the seedlings stage, because the F1 progenies
show wide variation as the parents are heterozygous.
(c) Study of agro-techniques: Detailed studies on manurial
requirements both macro-nutrients and micro-nutrients;
formulation of leaf analysis standards for wide scale
adoption by cultivators.
(d) Irrigation studies to determine the best method of irri-
gation and frequency of irrigation for different agro-
climatic regions in the State.
(e) Determining the economics of inter-cropping, best
combination of intercrops and their effect on various
crop components in the long run.
(f) Study of the control of Nephantis serinopa and
other pests and diseases of coconut.
(g) Evolving high yielding varieties which are tolerant
or resistant to different pests and diseases.
(h) Working out production programs for different types
of holdings.



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 2
3.        Coconut - CPCRI Program
(a) Electron microscopy of tissues of plants and vectors for
detection of virus and virus-like agents.
(b) Studying presence of spirochaete/spiroplasma.
(c) Isolation and identification of fungi associated with
the vascular disorganization.
(d) Pathogenicity test.
(e) Role of insects associated with coconut in transmission
of the disease.
(f) Infectivity and distribution pattern of nematodes, and
their role as vectors.
(g) Nematicidal trials.
(h) World collection of germplasm and screening varieties and
hybrids for resistance/tolerance to the disease.
(i)  Histopathological and histochemical studies.
(j) Development of suitable agrotechniques for root (wilt)
affected area including water management, multiple
cropping and intensive cropping.
(k) Isolation and mass culturing of antagonistic organisms
from soil.
(1) Studies on soil and tissue microfauna other than nematodes/
lower fungi associated with root (wilt) disease.
(m)  Serodiagnostic tests for identification of pathogens in
the early stage of disease.
(n) Metabolic derangement and translocation pattern in the
disease affected palms.
(o) Studies on enzymes, phenol oxidizing isoenzymes.
(p) Removal of affected palms and integrated soil and plant
treatment in the border area (Shencottah) to assess the
effectiveness of the above treatments in checking the
spread of the disease.
(q) Foliar analysis and fertilizer response studies.



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 3
4.        Pepper - AUK Program
(a) Breeding better varieties.
(b) Studies on diseases and their control.
(c) Studies on the agro-techniques such as manurial require-
ments, cultural operations etc.
(d) Standardization of a leaf analysis technique for fer-
tilizer recommendations;
(e) Studies on spike shedding;
(f) Evolve suitable management practices (including cultural
and land shaping aspects, moisture and soil conservation
measures and cropping programs) for the crop which is
grown on lands with undulating topography and uneven dis-
tribution of rainfall.
5.        Pepper - CPCRI Program
(a) Collection and evaluation of germplasm material - indigenous
and exotic.
(b) Study the impact of input technologies on the yield perform-
ance and quality attributes of pepper.
(c) Investigate and identify the physiological and genetic
barriers limiting the production potential and formulate
purposeful breeding programs.
(d) Elucidate the physiological/biochemical path-ways in the
biosynthesis of chemical principles in pepper and suggest
breeding programs for quality improvement.
(e) Characterize the quality standards for pepper for market
acceptance.
(f) Study the effect of maturity, plant protection chemicals
and improved agrotechniques on the quality.
(g) Epidemiology studies on 'Quick wilt' and 'Slow wilt' of
pepper.
(h) Determine the role of nematodes in the incidence of 'Slow
wilt' disease of pepper.
(i) Screening germplasm materials and evolution of varieties
with resistance/tolerance to major diseases and pests.



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 4
(j) Evolving effective control measures against Quick wilt
and Slow wilt diseases.
(k) Evolving varieties tolerant to drought.
(1) Evolving integrated pest management practices including
biological control against the major pests.
(m)  The exploitation of natural heterogenity of the crop by
raising large number of open pollinated seedlings and
by screening them for desirable character.
6.        Cashew Program (AUK and CPCRI)
(a) Genetic improvement of the crop for new planting and
replanting needs.
(b) Survey, selection, multiplication of superior genotypes
in existing plantings.
(c) Propagation techniques.
(d) Nutritional studies.
(e) Orchard management practices.
(f) Pest and disease control.
7.        Cocoa Program (AUK and CPCRI)
(a) Standardization of agro-techniques such as manuring, intercul-
tivation and irrigation for different agro-climatic regions.
(b) Standardization of tissue analysis as a means to find out the
nutritional requirements of the crop.
(c) Ascertain the optimum pruning methods for maximum production.
(d) Studies on optimum shade requirements under Kerala conditions.
(e) Standardization of vegetative propagation methods for large
scale multiplication of selected varieties or types.
8.   Extension Training
The farmers should be educated on the various aspects of cultivation
of these crops, the extension workers should have up-to-date knowledge on the
technology of increased production of these crops. Therefore, the following
program as a means of extending the research findings at the University level
is proposed:



ANNEX 6
Appendix 1
Page 5
(a) In-service training for officers of the Kerala
Department of Agriculture; and
(b) Training program for research staff in the improved
use of sophisticated equipment and on the latest
methodology of different investigations.
To achieve the above objective, it is necessary to strengthen the technically
specialized personnel in this field and to provide additional facilities.

ANNEX 7
Page 1
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Rubber Processing Sub-Project
Introduction
1.        Rubber in India is grown on about 225,000 ha and more than 125,000
holdings. Kerala's share of this area is estimated to be 95%. Like most other
tree crops in Kerala, rubber is grown predominantly by smallholders although
they account for only 60% or so of the natural rubber production. A large
proportion of these smallholders do not grow other crops nor do they have any
alternate sources of income. Hence, most of the families depend entirely on
rubber for their livelihood.
2.        Most smallholders process their rubber into ribbed smoke sheets
(RSS) in their homes without proper facilities. Rubber sheets are dried
inside the houses and smoking is done in the kitchen. Such crude methods
result in poor quality product and a large percentage of scrap. Existing
facilities are thus inadequate, inefficient, unhygienic and uneconomic.
3.        With the increase in total planted area of more than 50% over the
last ten years, coupled with intensified extension, and special replanting
programs, rubber production in the State is expected to increase twofold
within the next ten years. Additional processing capacity has, therefore,
to be provided for this increase. Indian Rubber Board (IRB) has thus planned
to set up crumb rubber processing factories to meet part of the future in-
creased processing capacity required.
Project Proposals
4.        Under this project funds would be provided for:
-    the establishment of nine small new 10 ton/day crumb rubber
factories;
-    extension of the existing crumb rubber factory at Palai in the
Kottayam District;
-    construction of godowns and provision of marketing facilities;
-    setting up within IRB a Rubber Processing Department and a
central testing laboratory for coordinating the processing
and the marketing of technically specified rubber; and



ANNEX 7
Page 2
-    training and technical assistance.
5.        Each of these factories would be designed to produce 10 tons crumb
rubber per day operating three shifts. In order to ensure that smallholders
would derive the maximum benefit from the processing and marketing of rubber,
IRB and GOK have favored that these new crumb rubber factories should be
owned and operated by Cooperative Rubber Marketing Societies (CRMS). In some
of the project areas, the CRMS might not be large enough or the majority
membership of the Society in the locality might not want it to enter into a
rubber processing venture. In such a case, it is proposed that the apex or-
ganization, the Kerala Cooperative Rubber Marketing Federation (KRMF), should
set up and operate that factory. The factories would be equipped to produce
B Grade Crumb rubber 1/ using a blend of approximately 70%t80% scrap grades
and 30%/20% higher grades (lot sheets or field latex). As the factories would
be designed also to handle field latex, switching over to production of lower
quality C Grade or of premium quality A Grade would be feasible and add
flexibility.
Location of Factories
6.        The major factors that would determine the location of the new
factories are:
-    concentration of smallholder rubber producers;
-    availability of smallholder latex, sheet rubber;
-    availability of scrap grades now sold at low prices;
-    location of existing or projected crumb and crepe rubber
factories;
-    availability of suitable land and labor;
-    facilities for and cost of treating factory effluent;
-    proximity to supporting services; and
-    management capability of prospective owner Cooperative.
The new factories would be sited on the more important rubber growing tracts
with a view to reducing collection costs of the raw material. As Kottayam is
the most densely planted District in Kerala, three of the new factories would
probably be located there. Quilon District would have two factories and one
each would be located in Alleppey, Cannanore, Kozhikode and Palghat Districts.
1/   A Grade is the highest quality crumb rubber, and C Grade the lowest.
B Grade is an in-between quality most likely to be produced because
it is consumed in large quantities by tire industries' (Appendix 1,
para 1).



ANNEX 7
Page 3
Phasing of Factories
7.        The time lag between selection of site to commissioning the factory
would be about 18 months. The phasing of the factories allows realistic time,
particularly in view of the organizational and training effort required both
at the CRMS level and in the IRB. Institution building has to be the fore-
runner to successful implementation of this subproject. This is no mean task
dealing with institutions which hitherto have had no experience in rubber
processing or in managing industrial units. The phasing of the proposed
factories would be:

Each factory when commissioned would operate for a time on one eight-hour
shift. The factories would be put on the second and third shift operations
as quickly as organization and management would permit because of the sig-
nificant reduction in the unit costs of production to be made when working
at higher throughput.

in Table 3. As CRMS do not have the organization or experience in marketing
processed rubber on a large scale, technically specified crumb rubber to be
produced by the Cooperatives would be marketed as far as practical through
KRMF. Costs of providing additional marketing facilities to KRMF are shown
in Table 4.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
Organization and Management
9.        All but five of the thirty-six CRMS in Kerala have had no experience
in rubber processing and only the CRMS at Palai has some experience of process-
ing crumb rubber. The apex federation KRMF itself has yet to undertake any
major processing activities. It might be considered that these institutions
are not the most suited to establish and manage new crumb rubber factories.
However, emphasis was placed primarily on improving the financial return to
the thousands of smallholders who would participate. It was also considered
desirable to take the opportunity to strengthen management capability generally
of the Cooperative institutions in Kerala. It was therefore decided to
encourage the Cooperative sector to own and operate these factories proposed
under the project but to provide adequate organizational and managerial
safeguards, more importantly:
(i) At the CRMS level, the Secretary of the Society should not
directly involve himself in factory operations or manage-
ment. The chief executive of the processing activity should
be a Factory Manager who would be assisted by exclusive
technical and administrative staff.
(ii) Establishment of a Rubber Processing Department within IRB
to be headed by a project leader at IRB Director level and
supported by three full-time IRB Officers, namely, a Rubber
Specialist (Chemical), Processing Engineer, and Economist/
Financial Advisor. All three Specialist Officers of this
implementation unit should be given suitable overseas
training in technical, organizational and commercial
aspects of crumb rubber processing and marketing.
(iii) Provision of overseas training to the factory managers of
the five Cooperative Societies which would set up the crumb
rubber plants in the first two project years.
(iv) Obtaining engineering services (for a total of about 27 man-
months) from consultants experienced in the construction and
operation of crumb rubber processing plants to assist the
IRB Rubber Processing Department in site selection, and to
be responsible for preparation of designs and specifications
for the factory buildings and machinery, including effluent
treatment, and in supervising the factory construction and
commissioning.
(v) Establishment by each participating CRMS of a Factory Manage-
ment Committee which would have representatives of IRB,
Registrar of Cooperative Societies, and the State Cooperative
Bank and the Factory Manager as members.



ANNEX 7
Page 5
(vi) Procuring factory machinery and equipment under inter-
national competitive bidding procedures, and letting
factory construction and related civil works contracts
under local tender procedures.
(vii) Obtaining the services (for a total of 12 man-months) of a
crumb rubber processing specialist, to visit India periodi-
cally and assist IRB executives in their implementation and
coordination work.
10.       The staffing requirements for the Rubber Processing Department in
IRB and for the individual crumb rubber factories are summarized in Appendix
2. TOR for technical and engineering advisory services are in Appendix 3.
Cost estimates for the Training and Technical Assistance components are in
Table 5. Operating costs are shown in Table 6.

Appendix 1
Page I
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Crumb Rubber Processing
Introduction
1.        Crumb Rubber, also described as 'block rubber' because of its appear-
ance, has been produced and sold as technically specified rubber for many years
by major rubber producing countries, led by Malaysia. The raw material required
for producing rubber can be field latex, RSS or scrap, or a blend of these.
The quality of the end product depends on the proportion in which the premium
grade and low grade rubber are blended. The highest value product is Grade A
usually made totally out of latex, or high grade RSS. The lowest quality is
Grade C made totally out of scrap grades. An in-between quality, styled Grade
B rubber, is the one consumed in largest quantities. The Grade B rubber could
be produced with 30/70% blend of RSS and scrap grades. If field latex is
used, the scrap grades could be raised up to 80%. As the major project
objective is to increase the value obtained by smallholders for their scrap
grades of rubber, preference has been shown in factory design and production
planning for processing Grade B crumb rubber.- Grade B also provides a more
favorable rate of return on investment. However, adequate flexibility would
be built in to enable these factories to switch wholly or partly from using
lot sheets to latex, and also from Grade B to Grade A and Grade C if market
circumstances warrant such changes.
Grade B Processing
2.        The quality of the smallholder scrap material is rather poor, being
high in dirt content and low in Plasticity Resilence Index (PRI) - a measure of
rubber quality. Test results in Kerala of early and recent batches of Grade B
block rubber derived from scrap materials reflect the poor quality of the raw
materials as well as the application of inadequate processing knowhow. The
dirt content in RSS is generally lower and the PRI higher than scrap grades.
To ensure that the output of the factory consistently meets the Grade B
specifications, it is necessary to blend about 30% by weight of lot sheets
(20% of field latex) with the scrap materials. Arrangements should be made to
obtain lot sheets from the smallholder before they are completely dry. Sheets
of about 90% dry rubber content (DRC) are convenient to handle, economical to
transport and easy to process at the factory.
Treating Scrap Rubber
3.        Prior to processing, the scrap material is soaked in water storage
tanks for three days; this allows sedimentation of dirt and softens the rubber



ANNEX 7
Appendix 1
Page 2
for ease of handling. The surface layer of scrap rubber with 85% drc is
virtually dry and not tacky; storage in the tanks should not result in signi-
ficant agglomeration of the scrap material. Since a larger proportion of the
scrap material has high dirt content, it is essential that it is passed
through a creper/hammermill for pre-cleaning. The water-recirculating trough
beneath the creper/hammermill serves to blend the scrap granules and to
settle "heavy" dirt. The granules are then passed over a static screen and
fall into a reception tank. The static screen separates the granules from
"light" dirt like bark, etc. The tank acts as a buffer since the process
is operated as a stop-start operation. Some settling of dirt also occurs
here. The scrap granules are then formed into a blanket by 6-9 passes through
three crepers. The "side-by-side" arrangement of the crepers provides further
opportunity for blending. The scrap blankets are then stacked one over the
other.
Factory Design
5.        A possible layout of a factory producing 10 tons Grade B crumb rubber
per day is shown in the attached Chart. The layout, design and specifications
for the factories to be constructed under the project would, however, be
prepared on a case-by-case basis by the CRMS concerned in consultation with
the IRB Rubber Processing Department and the Engineering Consultants.
Disposal of Factory Effluent
6.        The disposal of factory effluent would be an important considera-
tion in the choice of factory sites. Effluent from crumb rubber factories
must be treated before discharge into a waterway, which is large enough to
dilute the effluent at least tenfold. The factory would as far as possible
be sited in an area where the population density and agricultural activities
downstream from the factory are low. Improper planning of effluent disposal
could lead to grave problems because Kerala State is densely populated and
its people use the rivers frequently. This problem of potential localized
pollution of the rivers can be particularly grave during the dry months.
The standards of effluent discharge into rivers set by GOI are also rather
stringent. Consequently, in the estimates for capital investment, adequate
provision for effluent treatment has been made. The estimates are based
on the anaerobic stabilization treatment system developed recently in
Malaysia. This is a simple system of passing the effluent through a rubber
trap which would retain the solids to be recycled in the factory and let the
liquid flow into an anaerobic pond. The liquid would be retained here for
about a week and then passed into a stabilizing pond to remain for a further
two to three weeks before being discharged into a drain or stream. The
effluent from the ponds could also be used in gardens as a source of nitrogen.
IRB Processing Department would be responsible for setting acceptable standards
for effluent treatment to be followed by each of the factories.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 1
Page 3
Technical Certification of Crumb Rubber
7.        The Rubber Research Institute of India in collaboration with the
Institution of Standards of India has established a scheme to regulate and
control the issue of Test Certificates by individual producers of crumb
rubbers. Under this scheme, the individual factory will sample and test
its own production and issue its own test certificates. The Control Labora-
tory for Issue of Test Certificates (in the Rubber Research Institute) regularly
samples the factory's production and cross-checks on the factory's testing
standard. This arrangement avoids the need to send samples to a central
testing laboratory which could result in delay between production and dispatch
of a consignment of rubber. The individual factory will test 10% of the bales
produced. During the initial phase of production, the Control Laboratory will
test 10% of the rubber bales. But the level of testing could be reduced
progressively to between 1% and 3% depending on the grade of rubber tested.

INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Rubber Processing - Terms of Reference
I. Rubber Processing Department
1.        The Rubber Processing Department will be established as a division
within the Indian Rubber Board (IRB) to be the apex implementation unit for
the crumb processing sub-project. It will be headed by a full-time Project
Manager placed at the level of IRB Director. He will be assisted by three
full-time senior executives specialized in the fields of chemical engineering,
process engineering, and financial management, and given special overseas
training in crumb rubber production, processing and marketing management. The
Department will be provided with its own accommodation, equipment, vehicles,
support staff and facilities adequate to plan, coordinate, advise and monitor
all crumb rubber processing and marketing operations proposed under this
project.
2.        The functions of the Department will include:
-    liaison with the Project Coordination Committee set up
for the Kerala Agricultural Development Project;
-    general coordination and monitoring of Crumb Rubber
Processing sub-project;
-    detailed planning of the sub-project implementation;
-    liaison with the Cooperative Rubber Marketing Societies,
Kerala State Cooperative Rubber Marketing Federation,
Kerala State Cooperative Bank and the Agricultural
Refinance and Development Corporation;
-    representation in the Crumb Rubber Factory Management
Committees set up by the Cooperatives;
-    advising participating Cooperatives on business policy
and recruitment of senior factory executives.
-    recruitment of consultants for engineering and technical
advisory services;
-    arranging training, locally and overseas, of Depart-
ment executives, factory managers and other factory
personnel;



ANNEX 7
Appendix 3
Page 2
operating the IRB's crumb rubber pilot plant at Kottayam;
arranging field surveys of smallholder rubber areas to
recommend location of factories;
assisting the Cooperatives and the engineering service
consultants in preparing the factory layout, and speci-
fications; and in setting effluent treatment standards
for each factory;
issuing of bidding documents for factory and related procure-
ment, subsequent evaluation of bids received, and recommending
contract awards;
reviewing progress reports from Cooperatives;
preparation of quarterly progress reports on project implementation;
liaison with GOK, GOI and IDA;
IDA disbursements relating to rubber processing sub-project.
II. Engineering Services
3.        The services of a consulting company with minimum five years'
experience in the planning, designing, construction and operation of crumb
rubber processing factories and in marketing technically specified rubber will
be engaged by the Indian Rubber Board (IRB) for a total of about 27 man-months
to provide engineering services related to design, construction supervision
and initial operation of about 10 crumb rubber processing factories.
4.        The consultant company shall appoint a specialist chief qualified
by experience in managing similar engineering assignments to be responsible
for the company's other specialists to be assigned under the proposed contract.
5.        Engineering Services will include:
-    assisting in the selection of factory sites;
-    assisting in the factory investment planning;
-    preparation of designs and detailed specifications
for factory buildings, equipment and supporting
facilities;



ANNEX 7
Appendix 3
Page 3
assisting in the preparation of bid documents for
factory civil works and equipment procurement;
assisting in bid evaluation, preparing bid evalua-
tion reports and commenting on recommendations for
bid awards;
supervising and certifying crumb rubber factory build-
ing construction and machinery installation;
assisting in and supervising the factory operations
for up to three months after date of commissioning of
the factories;
arranging and supervising the training of personnel
as envisaged in Section III on Training (see below);
and
generally advise IRB and Cooperatives on engineering
matters related to the crumb rubber processing sub-
project.
III. Technical Advisory Services
6.        The services of a crumb rubber processing specialist will be engaged
for a total of twelve man-months to visit India periodically and assist the
senior executives of IRB Processing Department in their planning, coordination
and monitoring work.
7.        The specialist should be a professionally qualified chemical or
mechanical engineer with not less than five years  experience in crumb rubber
processing which should have been gained either as manager of a crumb rubber
factory or as a consultant on crumb rubber processing.
8.        As soon as possible after his appointment, the specialist will
visit India and spend up to six months and assist IRB in preparing:
-    the detailed implementation program for the sub-project;
-    the TOR for the senior executives of the IRB Processing
Department;
-    overseas training programs for the executives and for
prospective factory managers;
-    detailed recommendations for the organization and manage-
ment of the new factories;
-    recommendations for improving the performance by the Palai
crumb rubber factory;



ANNEX 7
Appendix 3
Page 4
-    a proforma progress report to be prepared by factory managers
for review by IRB Processing Department; and
-    a monitoring program for the factories to be carried out by
IRB Processing Department.
9.        Thereafter he will periodically visit India, spending about two
months on each occasion and:
-    review progress;
-    make field visits;
-    assist IRB Processing Department executives and factory
managers on technical, organizational or other matters
as may be requested by these persons;
-    prepare progress report at each visit; and
-    make further recommendations as he considers necessary.
IV. Training
10.       Training programs would include technological aspects relating to
construction of factories, managerial aspects covering procurement of raw
materials, production control, marketing and financial management of crumb
rubber factories.
11.       The chemical engineer, the processing engineer and the economist/
financial analyst would each be trained in a country, preferably Malaysia,
for a period of three months in crumb rubber processing and marketing aspects
specializing in their respective fields. The project leader and a marketing
executive would also be sent overseas for short period orientation in crumb
rubber business.
12.       Each of the ten crumb rubber factory managers would be given train-
ing for about six months. Training overseas, preferably in Malaysia, would be
arranged for managers of the first five factories set up by the Cooperative
Rubber Marketing Societies of Federation. The other managers would receive
their training locally in the new factories set up.
13.       Machine operators, mechanics, laboratory technicians and foremen
will be given instruction, training and orientation at the crumb rubber pilot
plant operated by the Rubber Board, and at Palai factory located in the
Kottayam District.

AN}NEX 10
Page 1
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Organization and Management
General
1.        The Government of Kerala (GOK) recognizes the need for effective
organization and management to ensure successful implementation of this
complex project involving several components and about 75,000 farmers.
Appropriate care has therefore been taken to strengthen all institutions par-
ticipating in project activities. All activities to be undertaken directly
by GOK (coconut, pepper and seed gardens) would be entrusted to a Special
Agricultural Development Unit (SADU). Cashew development would be entrusted
to the Kerala State Plantations Corporation (KSPC). Rubber processing sub-
project would be supervised by the Indian Rubber Board (IRB). The research
component would be handled jointly by Central Plantation Crops Research
Institute (CPCRI) and the Agricultural University of Kerala (AUK). Invest-
ment credit would be supervised by ARDC.
Special Agricultural Development Unit (SADU)
2.        This unit has been set up with specific responsibility for small-
holder development and establishment of seed gardens. Smallholder activities
include coconut and pepper area development.   Coconut area operations cover
coconut new planting and rehabilitation, irrigated development, and inter-
cropping including cocoa and dairy farming. SADU would be headed by the
Kerala Agricultural Production Commissioner. The chief executive of SADU
would be a full-time officer at least of the level of Additional Director.
SADU would be provided with exclusive staff and facilities.
3.        The SADU Director would be assisted by four deputies for the
following specializations:
-    Cooperative movement, agricultural credit and input supplies
-    Coconut area development
-    Pepper and seed garden development
-    Finance and administration
These deputies would be appointed at the level of Joint Directors. The two
technically specialized Joint Directors would be supported by three assistants
appointed at Deputy Director level, also with specializations. These five
officers would carry primary responsibility for each of the following fields:



ANNEX 10
Page 2
-    Coconut rehabilitation
-    Coconut new planting
-    Pepper rehabilitation
-    Dairy farming
-    Seed gardens
Comprehensive extension services support is required for all smallholder com-
ponents. Accordingly, most of the technical staff would be stationed in the
field. Staffing details are shown in Appendix I and in Charts I and II.
4.        SADU headquarters would be in Trivandrum, the State capital.  For
purposes of field administration there would be seven District level offices -
one for each of the project area Districts and one for the seed gardens
component as follows:
I    Cannanore - for coconut area development including new
planting, rehabilitation, cocoa, fodder for
dairy, and other intercropping and pepper
rehabilitation;
II    Kozhikode - for coconut area development as in Cannanore;
III    Malappuram - for coconut area development as in Cannanore;
IV    Trivandrum - for coconut area development as in Cannanore;
V    Kottayam - pepper rehabilitation;
VI    Idukki - pepper rehabilitation;
VII    Seed Gardens - for coconut, cashew, cocoa, pepper, and spices.
At the District level offices, there would be one officer appointed at the
rank of Deputy Director to be in charge of each of the coconut Districts and
an Assistant Director for each of the pepper Districts and the seed gardens.
Dairy farming would require supervision from officers specialized in animal
husbandry. Four District Livestock Officers at Assistant Director level would
therefore be attached to the Cannanore, Kozhikode, Malappuram and Trivandrum
Districts.
Extension Services at Field Level
5.        Intensive technical support is considered crucial for the success-
ful implementation of development to be undertaken by smallholders. Special
care has therefore been taken to provide adequate extension services to help
project farmers. These facilities would supplement the services presently
provided by KDOA and the Department of Animal Husbandry. The extension
services approach planned under this project would be similar to that
followed by KDOA in the "Intensive Package Programs" for coconut and paddy



ANNEX 10
Page 3
commenced in 1973/74. These are designed to concentrate on a limited area
and on major crops, applying modern techniques of farming and using better
seed, stimulating the flow of credit and technical assistance (Annex 1,
para 12). Each extension unit consisting of 1,000 ha is supervised by a
team of three - one Junior Agricultural Officer and two Agricultural
Demonstrators. KDOA, through its extension service recommends to farmers
in the State, a special package of cultivation practices to be applied
for each of the major crops. Such packages generally follow the "Package
of Practices" published periodically by CPCRI for the respective crops.
Special Package Programs are sponsored by GOI which also provides financial
assistance. GOK engages incremental staff as needed from the pool of agri-
cultural graduates in Kerala. Extension education is now given by AUK which
in 1975 commenced in-service training for the staff of KDOA and the Depart-
ment of Animal Husbandry.
6.        Project extension services would be administered on this Intensive
Package system but staffing intensity in the project area would be doubled;
this is considered necessary to achieve a 80% coverage within three to four
years. Two Agricultural Demonstrators lead by one Junior Agricultural Officer
would form a team and cover a 500 ha package unit comprising 800 to 1,200
farm families - the staffing intensity at field level would be kept at one
extension worker for every 300-400 families during the initial three to four
years of development in any one extension package unit and to 600-800 there-
after until development is completed (two/three additional years). One Vete-
rinary Surgeon and three Livestock Assistants-per 500 ha of fodder grass
(supporting about 1,800 cows) would be posted in addition to the Agricultural
Officers. These would be needed for timely insemination, disease control by
way of vaccination, veterinary care and extension education on the feeding
and management of cross-bred cows and heifers, along side with the agricultural
extension work on forage production. As development is completed in project
areas staff would be transferred to extension units in other areas where
similar developments would be initiated, based on experience gained in the
proposed project.
7.        Since the density of farming population in any package unit would be
very high (with holdings averaging 0.4 ha), group meetings would easily be
called by extension officers in farmers' fields. These meetings would be
advertised through the local Cooperatives. Junior Agricultural Officers
would be assisted from time to time by specialists from KDOA and AUK. Also
mobile units from the Farmers' Training Centers - four in the State - would
be made available for use in project area. These would provide field officers
with publications and audio-visual equipment such as flip charts, blackboards
etc. Provision has been made for small extension office buildings in all
package units. Extension officers would be required to spend most of their
time in the field. They would also participate in local Cooperative Board
meetings as members or special invitees and in Panchayat 1/ meetings in order
to advertise the credit program and to ensure proper coordination between
credit and extension. Field staff would counsel farmers and help them to
1/ Elected local committee paralled to the Government Administration.



ANNEX 10
Page 4
prepare individual development programs and loan applications which would
be collected by the same officers who would pass them on to the Credit Co-
operative Societies. Field officers' daily records of activities would be
scrutinized by their supervisors who would make regular visits to the pack-
age units. The latter would verify whether meetings and field visits
carried out were effective and advise the field staff on improvements.
8.        For all field staff, mobility will be essential.  Transportation
facilities have therefore been provided - one motorbicycle for each field
worker (338) and one car for each supervisory staff (20). Provision has also
been made for vehicle operating and maintenance costs for the project period
(based on 20,000 km/year for cars and 10,000 km/year for motorcycles). Motor-
cycles would be sold to staff on credit, which is the best means of ensuring
proper care and maintenance. In addition, the project would finance six
10-ton trucks for transport of input supplies, mainly seedlings - 1.6 million
coconut seedlings would have to be transported from KDOA's nurseries over a
period of seven years. Also about 3,000 rocker-sprayers and 2,000 knapsack
sprayers are included in project costs. Spraying against coconut leaf disease
would be carried out twice a year in project farms by SADU engaging casual
workers. Full cost of spraying would be charged to farmers and included in
the investment loans.
9.        There is a good network of private and government facilities for
servicing of vehicles and equipment. GOK has an approved list of private
workshops where government vehicles and equipment could be repaired and
serviced. The government facilities owned and operated by the Engineering
Wing of KDOA are considered adequate for project needs.
10.       In-service training for all extension workers would be provided
by KDOA and AUK with assistance as necessary from CPCRI. Funds have been
included in the project for this purpose. Research support would be given
by both institutions. Researchers would not only serve on the advisory
panels to guide the implementation of the projects but also undertake
operational research in farmers' fields.
Kerala State Plantations Corporation (KSPC)
11.       The cashew component involves the management of two large farms -
one for new planting and the other to be rehabilitated - and cashew apple
processing. KSPC is thus best suited to operate these commercial scale
ventures. Since the Corporation had hitherto not engaged in such develop-
ment operations in cashew, it was necessary to set up within KSPC a new
Cashew Project Department to implement this sub-project. The Department
would be headed by a full-time Director who would report directly to the
Managing Director of KSPC and have its own administrative and technical
staff adequate to function independently of KSPC's other Departments
(Annex 4, para 9).



ANNEX 10
Page 5
Crumb Rubber Processing
12.       The crumb rubber factories would be owned and operated by coopera-
tives - Cooperative Rubber Marketing Societies (CRMS) and Kerala Rubber Mar-
keting Federation (KRMF). Most of these cooperatives have had no experience
in rubber processing. They would therefore be provided with technical and
organizational support through IRB. A Rubber Processing Department would
be set up within IRB to be headed by a Director as project leader. He would
be assisted by specially trained executives. The Department would be provided
with exclusive staff and facilities to enable it to coordinate the activities
of the cooperatives and to supervise the implementation of this sub-project.
The cooperatives themselves would have Factory Management Committees, exclu-
sive staff and facilities to enable the factories to be managed independent
of the societies' other activities (Annex 7, para 9).
Agricultural Research
13.       Agricultural Research component would be implemented by CPCRI and
AUK through a Joint Committee headed by the Vice Chancellor of AUK with repre-
sentatives of CPCRI, Kerala Department of Agriculture, Development Directorate
of GOI, and ICAR. The sub-project relating to AUK would be carried out at
its Vellanikkara Campus, and that of CPCRI at Kasaragod headquarters and its
regional stations in Kayamkulam and Kozhikode.
Project Coordination and Monitoring
14        P'roject coordination and monitoring would be effected through a
Project Coordination Committee (PCC). Since the project activities also in-
volve participation by Government departments, agencies and other institutions
outside the province of the Ministry of Agriculture, PCC would be headed by
the Kerala Agricultural Production Commissioner and have representation from
the State Ministries of Agriculture and Cooperation, SADU, ARDC, KSPC, IRB,
and GOI. The PCC would arrange to draw widely on the services of technical
advisory panels comprising representatives from relevant government depart-
ments, corporations, cooperatives, participating banks, marketing and other
institutions. PCC would be entrusted with specific responsibilities as
follows:
-    Project monitoring
-    Coordinate project procurement financed by the IDA Credit;
-    Liaison with all agencies participating in project implementation;
-    Liaison with GOI, GOK, and IDA;
-    IDA disbursements;
PCC would have attached to it a senior official full time to function as
its Chief Executive. He would be provided with necessary support staff and
facilities (Appendix 1).



ANNEX 10
Page 6
15.       A Project Evaluation Unit (Appendix 2) would be set up to follow up
project progress, identify problem areas and evaluate project benefits. This
unit would also serve to measure project impact on participants, comparing
costs and benefits realized with appraisal estimates and projections. The
chief executive of the Unit would be independent of all participating agencies
and report direct to the Agricultural Production Commissioner who would alsobe
the head of both PCC and SADU. Terms of reference for this Unit are given in
Appendix 2.
Project Implementation Schedule
16.       The phasings of project implementation activities agreed during
negotiations, are shown in the Charts in Annex 11.

ANNEX 10
Appendix 2
Page 1
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Evaluation Unit
1.        The proposed project comprises many types of development, involves
several institutions, and would affect the future of thousands of participat-
ing shareholders. The project is also capable of replication because the
development planned under this project represents only a small proportion
of tree crop area in Kerala and in the neighboring States slated for rehabi-
litation. In order to assist in the successful implementation of the project
and to ascertain that the benefits forecast now are in fact realized, a
Project Evaluation Unit would be established under the Agricultural Production
Commissioner. The Unit would be established before December 31, 1977 and
maintained for a minimum of ten years after commencement.
2.        The Chief Executive of this Unit would be an official at least of
the level of Deputy Director and have adequate support staff. He would be
responsible to and report directly to the Kerala Agricultural Production Com-
missioner. The Unit would be independent of all other institutions partici-
pating in project implementation to enable the Unit Chief to be objective
in his assessment and to ensure freedom in reporting.
3.        Project evaluation by this Unit would cover physical, technical,
financial, managerial and economic aspects of the project. The Unit would
carry out its activities by keeping close and continuous liaison with GOI,
GOK, and individuals and institutions directly and indirectly participating
in the project. The Unit would receive from project participants all in-
formation considered relevant for its functions. The following documents
would all be relevant for the Unit's evaluation and reporting work:

4.        The Unit would also make its own arrangements to collect information
and data required for its studies, both from project records and from outside
sources. Such arrangements should include field visits to:

Headquarters, Branches, District Offices of all institutions
participating in the project,
Rubber Factories and Warehouses, and
Coconut, Pepper and Cashew Farms and Plantations.
The Unit Chief Executive should also maintain close liaison with the Chief
Internal Auditors of the participating institutions.
5.        The Unit would thus be able to identify major problems, deviations
from appraisal estimates and annual budgets, and possible corrective action
to be taken either by project managements or by governments. Reporting to
the Agricultural Production Commissioner could be oral and written.
6.        Topics reported on would include:
Deviations from agreed Implementation Schedules - causes, extent,
effects and action;
Project modifications from original plan - nature, reasons and
effects;
Variations of important assumptions made in the appraisal report;
Cost overruns and arrangements to meet them;
Efficiency of the participating institutions;
Credit Services to farmers;
Agricultural extension services;
Verification of project benefits realized by participants; and
Unusual features of significance.
7.        Written evaluation reports would be prepared by the Unit periodically -
at least half yearly - and submitted to the Agricultural Production Commissioner.
Copies of all evaluation reports would be made available to IDA for information.
The Unit together with SADU, ARDC, IRB and PCC would also assist IDA to pre-
pare the project completion report.

KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMEN' PROJECT
Bankin and Credit Services
Coconut and Pepper Area Development
1.        Coconut new plantings under the project would take place in
Cannanore, Kozhikode, and Malappuiram. Coconut rehabilitation would be in
these three Districts and also in Trivandrum. Pepper rehabilitation would
be in Cannanore, Kottayam and Idukki. Each Package Unit of 500 ha is also
treated as an administrative unit for credit purposes. The distribution of
package Units in the project area Districts would be:
Package Units
Coconut         Coconut
Districts          Rehabilitation   New Plantings   Pepper
Cannanore                  24                 3          10
Kozhikode                  26                 3           -
Malappuram                 10                 4
Trivandrum                 15                 -          -
Kottayam                            -                    2
Idukki                     -                  -          8
Total Package Units  75                 10         20
2.        In the six Districts selected for the coconut and pepper develop-
ment, there are sixteen Primary Land Mortgage_Banks (PLMB). In Cannanore
District, where much of the financing will be done, there are five PLMB of
which three (Cannanore, Kasargod and Tellicherry) are well established with
outstanding loans exceeding Rs 10 million. The fourth (Wynad Bank) has not
been faring well, and the Hosdurg Bank has been only recently organized.
These two PLMB would need particular attention. The Kozhikode and Badagara
PLMB in the Kozhikode District are strong - the latter has recorded 100%
recoveries. The Kozhikode PLMB, has recently improved its recovery perfor-
mance and is now eligible for unrestricted lending. The two PLMB in Malap-
puram have to increase their business quickly; their recovery performance is
satisfactory. The two older PLMB in Trivandrum are considered strong while
the third one at Neyyantinkara is new and has yet to commence lending in
earnest. The four PLMB in Kottayam and Idukki Districts have in the past
built up large business. However, not much new business was brought in during
1975-76. Thus, while the loaning operations of most of the PLMB are still in
low key, there would be no difficulty, whether on account of poor recoveries,
or for organizational or other reasons, in e-xpanding their lending operations



ANNEX 12
Page 2
The position of overd-s should, however, be reviewed on a -%<r.+Tuous basis by
all PLMB since good recovery performance would be crucial             allocation
for their lending programs.
Commercial Banks (CB)
3.        The Lead Banks (see para 18 in Appendix) in the Six Districts are:
District                            Lead Bank
Cannanore                   Syndicate Bank
Kozhikode                   Canara Bank
Malappuram                   Canara Bank
Trivandrum                   Indian Bank
(jointly with Indian Overseas Bank)
Idukki                      Union Bank of India
Kottayam                     State Bank of Travancore
All these banks, except State Bank of Travancore, are all-India institutions
operating country-wide and have experience in financing agriculture. They
have also been implementing a number of Credit Schemes sanctioned by ARDC.
The State Bank of Travancore which operates mainly in Kerala is a subsidiary
of the State Bank of India and has the largest number of branches in the
Kerala State. In addition, there are ,s many as twelve other public sector
banks including State Bank of India and fourteen non-nationalized banks.
The public sector banks together had 368 branches operating in the State.
A more detailed description of the Agricultural Credit Institutions is given
in the Appendix.
Banking Plan
4.        GOI, GOK and ARDC have agreed that the farmers should have the free-
dom to choose the financing bank. It has also been agreed that long-term credit
needs of project farmers would be met by the Cooperative and Commercial Bank-
ing systems, i.e. PLMB and CB. PLMB would obtain necessary funds from the Kerala
Cooperative Central Land Mortage Bank (KCLMB) which would utilize its own funds
initially. Both KCLMB and CB lending would be refinanced by ARDC. CB would
provide necessary short-term credit support to project participants out of
their own resources. For participants financed by the PLMB, short-term credit
requirements would be met by the Primary Credit Societies (PCS) which would
receive support from the District Central Cooperative Bank (DCCB). The DCCB
could take into account the extra funds required for short-term agricultural
finance for project activities and obtain sanction from Kerala State Cooper-
ative Bank (SCB) and RBI for enhanced credit limits.
5.        Since there is a large number of CB, it was considered desirable to
limit the number of participating banks to avoid unnecessary duplication of
efforts.  Thus, in each District the Lead Bank and two or three other CB would
be selected to participate. Among the CB selected for a District the bank
whose branch operates nearest to a package unit would be the obvious choice



ANNEX 12
Page 3
for that unit. 'Thus in each package unit, long-term financing would be done
by one CB selected for the purpose and the PLMB operating in the area. In the
case of farmers financed by PLMB, - "one-window" approach, for both long-term
and short-term requirements, would be e-rouraged.  The PCS would provide the
short-term credit requirements fro}, iL oin resources and also function as
an agent of the PLMB for disbursement and recovery of long-term loans. The
PCS would receive applications from farmers for long-term credit and after
preliminary scrutiny pass them on to the PLMB for final appraisal and approval
of the loan. In the case of CB, the same branch bank would handle both short-
term and long-term credit. The selection of CB for each project area would
be done by ARDC. The Project Banking Plan would be drawn up by ARDC in con-
sultation with GOK and the participating banks.
6.        The bench-mark survey conducted in the project areas by GOK in
March 1976 shows that the largest number of holdings were less than 1 ha.
For the coconut rehabilitation scheme, 35% have been considered as holdings
below 0.5 ha and 42% as holdings from 0.5 ha to 1.0 ha. Thus, 77% of the
holdings are below 1 ha. For all IBRD/IDA lendings under agricultural pro-
jects in India, concessions regarding down-payment and repayment period have
been extended by ARDC to small farmers - defined as those having pre-develop-
ment net income not exceeding Rs 2,000 (adjusted to 1972 prices). The pro-
bability is that the majority of the cultivators participating in this project
would fall within the definition of small farmers.
Refinance and Terms
7.        The KCLMB would, on behalf of PLMB, submit to ARDC for approval
specific schemes under the coconut new planting, coconut rehabilitation and
pepper rehabilitation sub-projects. The PLMB would insist on down payment at
5%, 10% or 15% as the case may be from small, medium and other farmers based
on the definition adopted for the ARDC Credit Projects financed by IDA and
now applied uniformly for all ARDC refinancing. ARDC refinance would cover
90% for irrigated development and 75% of the amounts loaned for other pur-
poses. The balance 10% to 25% would be contributed by GOK as its share of the
Special Debentures. The interest rate for ARDC refinance would be not less
than 7-1/2% for irrigation and land development and 8% for other purposes.
The interest charged to the ultimate borrower by the lending bank would be not
less than 10-1/2% and 11% respectively. The KCLMB and PLMB would share the
margin of 3% on an agreed basis.
8.        The CB would submit their schemes directly to ARDC.  Down payment
requirements, the period for repayment of refinance and the interest rates
would be in line with those applied to refinance the Cooperative stream
loans.
Kerala State Electricity Board (KSEB)
9.        In providing funds to KSEB, for electric reticulation for irrigation
pumpsets covered under the coconut rehabilitation and new planting programs,



ANNEX 12
Page 4
the procedure currently adopted by the ARDC for similar purposes in terms of
its circular of October 21, 1975 would be foliowed. Participating banks
would prepare proposals in respect of the cost of electrification to be in-
curred by the KSEB for the reticulation while drawing up each lending scheme
involving irrigation. Unit costs would be approved by ARDC when sanctioning
each scheme. Energy requirements would be identified by the lending bank
from farmers' loan applications supported by the farm investment plans which
include a pumpset. These investment plans would already have been recommended
by the project extension service staff. The lending bank would then request
KSEB to provide the electric reticulation for the scheme. The bank's field
officers would themselves inspect the sites to confirm that the reticulation
had been completed before making the loan available to KSEB. Funds made avail-
able to KSEB would carry the same rate of interest as is applicable to the
loans sanctioned by the financing bank to the relevant farmers. The period
of loans would not exceed 7 years and the amount would be repayable in equal
annual installments. GOK would provide guarantee to the lending banks secur-
ing the capital repayment and payment of interest by KSEB. The rate of inte-
rest on refinance would be settled by ARDC at the time of disbursement of
refinance. The lending banks would, however, be allowed a fixed margin of 3%.
The current rate on refinance is 7-1/2% and the KSEB will receive funds from
the lending banks at not less than 10-1/2%. Refinance would cover up to 80%
of the loans made to KSEB by the lending banks.
Cashew Development
10.       The cashew development would be undertaken by the Kerala State
Plantation Corporation (KSPC). KSPC obtains loans from the member banks (CB)
of the Agricultural Finance Corporation, a company set up by the nationalized
Commercial Banks in India. KSPC has not so far obtained any refinance from
ARDC. KSPC would submit its investment proposals for the cashew sub-project
to a bank of its choice operating in Kerala. The lending bank in turn would
scrutinize the proposals and forward a Scheme to ARDC seeking refinance and
justifying the technical feasibility and financial viability. The lending
bank would look to KSPC to contribute about 15% of the investment cost and
grant loans for the balance. ARDC would refinance up to 80% of the amounts
lent by the lending bank. Refinance would carry interest not less than 8%
and the lending bank would charge not less than 11% to KSPC retaining a margin
of 3%.
Refinancing Processing Factories
11.       The setting up of crumb rubber processing factories would be under-
taken by Rubber Marketing Societies (CRMS) or the Kerala Rubber Marketing
Federation (KRMF). According to the agreed phasing, three factories (includ-
ing Palai) would commence production by the middle of 1979. Three more would
commence production by the end of 1980. The remaining four factories would be
commissioned only in 1982. In order to ensure adequate funds and uniform



ANNEX 12
Page 5
appraisal procedures, the financing arrangements for all the ten factories
would be handled by SCB directly. SCB has adequate resources and would be
able to acquire and develop necessary expertise for financing the factories
and supervising the loans. Proposals for each factory would however, be
sent by SCB to ARDC for appraisal and sanction. This procedure would ensure
availability of adeqtuate technical and financial expertise for such appraisal.
After AR1)C sanction. the financing of the factory construction would be done
by the SCB which would provide up to 85% of the cost, leaving 15% to be met
by CRMS or KRMF. Refinance would be provided by ARDC up to 80% of loans made
by SCB, the balance being met out of resources of SCB. The interest rate on
refinance would be determined by ARDC at the time of disbursement and would be
not less than 8%. The SCB would have a margin of 3% and charge to CRMS and
KBMF interest at not less than 11%.  The loan repayment period would be
determined separately In each case and would normally be less than 10 years.

INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Credit Institutions
Introduction
1.        In India, institutional credit for agriculture is handled by the
Reserve Bank of India (RBI), the Agricultural Refinance and Development
Corporation (ARDC) the State Cooperative Credit System and the Commercial
Banks (CB). The Cooperative Credit system consists of the long-term credit
structure, comprising the two-tier Land Mortgage Banks (LMB) structure, State
Cooperative Central Land Mortgage Banks 1/ and Primary Land Mortgage Banks
(PLMB), and the short and medium-term three-tier structure - State Coopera-
tive Banks (SCB) at the apex, Central Cooperative Banks at the District level
(DCCB) and Primary Credit Societies (PCS) at the village level dealing directly
with the farmer.
Reserve Bank of India (RBI)
2.        As the Central Banking Authority, RBI regulates money supply and
supervises the banking system covering both the Commercial and Cooperative
banks. Its Agricultural Credit Department has considerable experience in
dealing with agricultural credit matters. The interest charged by RBI for
seasonal agricultural operations is 1/2% below the Bank Rate of 9% (with a
rebate of another 1-1/2%), and medium-term loans for agricultural purposes at
1-1/2% below the Bank Rate. RBI regulates lending programs of LMB and coordi-
nates the arrangements for support from the GOI, State Governments, Life
Insurance Corporation of India and CB for the "Ordinary Debenture" programs.
As part of credit policy, RBI has regarded lending for agricultural purposes
by CB as "priority sector" advances eligible for concessions as regards
liquidity requirements.
Agricultural Refinance and Development Corporation (ARDC)
3.        ARDC was established on July 1, 1963 in the context of the high
priority accorded to agricultural development in the Country's Five-year
Plans. Its specific functions are to supplement the resources of existing
institutional agencies disbursing long-term and medium-term credit; reorient
their operational policies; and provide refinance facilities to member banks
I/   Also known as State Land Banks and Land Development Banks.



ANNEX 12
Appendix
Page 2
for projects which cannot be financed by them either because of the sizeable
outlays or because such lending cannot be brought within their normal rules of
business. ARDC's board has nine members including a Deputy Governor of RBI,
who acts as Chairman, and three GOT nominees. The Managing Director is
appointed by RBI in consultation with the Board. ARDC has its head office in
Bombay and fourteen regional offices in State capitals including one in
Trivandrum.
4.        Institutions entitled to refinance facilities from ARDC include
Land Banks, Scheduled Commercial Banks and SCB, all of which are ARDC share-
holders. ARDC provides refinance only for such projects which conform to
criteria of technical feasibility and financial viability and are capable of
implementation within a compact geographical area to facilitate loan apprais-
al and close supervision. The emphasis has been on projects involving a large
number of farmers with a satisfactory coverage of small farmers. At present,
ARDC provides refinance at 7-1/2% per annum in respect of loans for minor
irrigation and land development and, at 8% for other purposes, the rates
charged to the borrowers by the lending banks being 10-1/2% and 11% respec-
tively. Period of loan repayment has regard to the repaying capacity of the
borrowers for the purpose financed which in no case exceeds the useful life of
the assets financed. In the case of minor irrigation investments, small
farmers are allowed a concessional repayment period of 15 years against 9
years allowed to other farmers. Loans for equipment including irrigation
pumpsets, however, have to be repaid within 7 years by all farmers.
5.        ARDC has considerable experience in handling credit related to
IBRD/IDA projects. As at the end of September 1976, there were 26 projects
(11 Agricultural Credit, 4 Command Area Development, 3 Dairy Development,
2 Markets, 1 Apple Processing and Marketing, 2 Seed Projects, the Integrated
Cotton Development, Drought Prone Areas Project and General Line of Credit
to ARDC) in which ARDC was actively involved. Under these projects, ARDC
had disbursed by September 1976, about Rs 3 billion against which IBRD/IDA
reimbursement totalled some Rs 2 billion. As of September 1976, ARDC had
sanctioned more than 2,800 Credit Schemes in which its own financial commit-
ments totalled Rs 11 billion. Of these, about 1,500 schemes involving Rs 8
billion (73% of total) were for minor irrigation purposes. ARDC is therefore
considered a suitable channel for providing credit to project participants
by refinancing loans granted by the Cooperative and Commercial Banks.
Cooperative Banking System
6.        Short-term loans (up to 15 months) to farmers for cultivation and
medium-term loans (15 months to 5 years) for agricultural investments within
the Cooperative stream have been organized on the 3-tier basis, consisting of
the Kerala SCB at the State level, eleven DCCB at District level, and about
1730 PCS at the village level. All PCS in a District are affiliated to the
DCCB which in turn is a member of the SCB. All the institutions in the three-
tier structure raise resources through deposits supplemented by borrowings
from the institution higher in the three-tier family. SCB has recourse to



ANNEX 12
Appendix
Page 3
RBI for resources. SCB was established in 1915, and its membership consists
of the eleven DCCB and GOK which has also contributed its equity. In 1972
SCB was granted a license under the Banking Regulation Act. It is managed
by a board of fourteen directors including eleven representatives, one each
from DCCB, and three appointed by GOK including the Kerala Secretary of Agri-
culture and Registrar of Cooperative Societies.
7.        SCB operates within short-term credit limits sanctioned annually
by RBI. Such limits are based on DCCB's financial soundness, lending pro-
grams presented by them, their ability to raise internal resources (encourag-
ing them to be less dependent on outside help in due course) and their ability
to provide adequate non-overdue loans as collateral for RBI finance. These
borrowings are supplemented by SCB's own resources in order to increase the
quantum of credit provided to DCCB. Medium-term loans taken by SCB from RBI
have been small. Although Rs 7.6 M was sanctioned during 1975-76, actual
withdrawals were only Rs 4.3 M.
8.        The eleven DCCB in Kerala have been improving their deposit
resources and loans business in recent years. They have 115 branches in the
State through which they have been able to tap deposits from the people and
provide funds to PCS more easily. The operations of DCCB are closely inter-
linked with those of the affiliated PCS which deal directly with farmers at
field level. Due to reorganization of PCS, aimed to retain only the viable
ones, the number was reduced from 2,049 Societies in June 30, 1973 to 1,731 by
June 1975. They operate in all the 1,635 villages in the State and cover 70%
of the rural population. Their membership is more than two million of whom
about half were borrowing members. Their paid-up capital and reserves amount
to about Rs 200 M. A remarkable feature is their success in mobilizing
deposits locally which increased from above Rs 127 M on June 30, 1973 to Rs
210 M by June 30, 1975. They accept various types of deposits and some of
them run "Chit" funds to encourage thrift. Yet they have to depend largely on
the DCCB and such borrowings were more than the deposits viz. Rs 411 million
at the end of June 1975. By and large, PCS in Kerala have been faring much
better than Societies in other States as would be evident from the data
below:

The figures as on June 1975 for all India are yet to be published. Figures
