9.        The Kerala KCLMB provides long-term credit in the State and
operates through PLMB which loan funds directly to the farmers in all the
eleven Districts. For operational convenience KCLMB has opened three regional
offices at Cannanore, Ernakulam and Trivandrum which have powers to sanction
and disburse loans when these exceed the limits allowed to PLMB. KCLMB's
equity capital of Rs 10.27 M at December 31, 1975 was contributed by the
thirty PLMB (Rs 6.19 M) and GOK (Rs 4.08 M). Although GOK enjoys only one
vote as any other member, it has nominated the Director of Agriculture and
the Secretary of Agriculture as directors on the board in addition to the
Registrar of Cooperative Societies (Ex-officio). KCLMB is allowed to accept
1 year and 2 year deposits from institutions and non-borrowers. The amount
collected is, however, very small as no banking facilities are provided.
For its day-to-day needs KCLMB depends on interim accommodation from the SCB
and CB which is paid back when Ordinary Debentures are floated. These KCLMB
debentures which are redeemable between 10 and 15 years are guaranteed by
GOK and are purchased by GOI, GOK, Life Insurance Corporation, CB and the
KCLMB in other States. For long-term requirements, KCLMB floats Special
Debentures supported by ARDC. These debentures, also guaranteed by GOK,
are issued at 7-1/2% to 8% and contributed by GOK and ARDC in the ratio
of 90:10 for minor irrigation purposes and 75:25 for other purposes. The
redemption period is generally 7 to 15 years.
10.       The accent on long-term lending is to ensure that credit is used
for easily identifiable productive purposes such as sinking of new wells,
purchase of pumpsets, electric motors, tractors and other agricultural equip-
ment. In the Schemes sanctioned by ARDC, purposes are already identified when
the sanctions are accorded and the size of credit and terms are settled. Loan
periods vary from 7 years to 15 years depending on the purpose of the loan.
Generally, loans for pumpsets, tractors and other farm machinery are for a
period of 7 years. Out of a total of 6,347 loans in 1974/75, 1,812 loans were
sanctioned under the normal program for amounts ranging from Rs 1,000 to Rs
2,000 and 3,318 for amounts between Rs 2,000 and Rs 5,000. KCLMB has been
working at profit and the net profit for 1974-75 was Rs 1.28 M.
11.       Although there are now thirty PLMB in Kerala, they are not evenly
distributed regionally. Palghat District has only one bank and three other
Districts have two banks each. Cannanore District has the highest number
with five PLMB and three branches. Some of the older PLMB have built up
good business and many of them have current loans exceeding Rs 10 M. Seven
are relatively new but have been approved by the RBI as already viable or



ANNEX 12
Appendix
Page 5
potentially viable. GOK would contribute share capital to these and also
ensure that competent and trained staff are posted. GOK has no plans at
present for organizing more PLMB. All but eight of PLMB are working at a
profit. In December 1975, PLMB had a membership of 115,000 of which 71,000
were borrowing members. Equity capital is contributed by members at 5%
of the amount of loans taken and such contributions totalled Rs 12.9 M.
PLMB, are, therefore, overwhelmingly dependent on borrowings from KCLMB
since they are not allowed to float debentures, collect deposits, or to
undertake commercial banking; they are not banks in the real sense of the
term, functioning essentially as cooperative societies.
12.       All loans to farmers are issued against registered mortgages of land
and up to 50% of their value, based on current sales of land in the area. In
the ARDC schemes, the value of the land at full development (presumptive
value) is taken into account and this improves considerably the eligibility
for a loan. Where the real estate security is not adequate, a charge on the
other assets owned or to be acquired is obtained. Land prices are high
ranging from Rs 10,000/- to Rs 40,000 per acre in the case of crops like
coconut, pepper and rubber. Loans are normally limited to Rs 10,000 each but
this can be much higher in special cases such as tractor loans. PLMB are
allowed to sanction loans up to Rs 7,500 (now raised to Rs 10,000) and they
have to refer loans exceeding this limit to one of the three regional offices
of KCLMB for sanction. PLMB obtain loans at 9% from KCLMB and charge the
farmer at 10-1/2% for minor irrigation loans and 11% for other purposes.
Repayment is annual and loans do not generally exceed a period of 15 years.
In coconut and other horticultural and plantation schemes, grace periods are
allowed. Disbursement of loans is normally made by PLMB after funds are
obtained from the KCLMB.
13.       There has been a noticeable improvement in recoveries of loans
by PLMB as a result of the policy announced by RBI for regulating the lending
programs of LMB both in regard to the ordinary loans as well as loans under
special schemes refinanced by ARDC. This policy follows the agreements
reached between GOI and IDA in March 1975, when a General Line of Credit
Project to the ARDC (504 IN) was negotiated. Instructions were issued by the
RBI in July 1975, spelling out the details of this regulation in terms of
which the eligibility of any LMB to participate in a lending program will
depend on its recovery performance based on the June-end position in the
previous year. These institutions will be free to lend without any restric-
tions if the overdues are less than 25% of the demand. When the overdues
exceed this limit, their lendings will be restricted to a percentage of what
they advanced in the previous year and this will progressively come down with
the increase in overdues as follows:



ANNEX 12
Appendix
Page 6
Range of overdues at the primary         Eligibility of program as
branch level as on June 30 as            percentage of loans issued
percentage to demand for the year.       during the previous year.
0 - 25                              Unrestricted
26 - 35                                    80
36 - 45                                    70
46 - 55                                   60
56 - 60                                   50
61 - 100                                 Nil
PLMB will not be allowed to lend any amount if their overdues exceed 60% of
demand.  However, if the State Government is willing to contribute to share
capital of these institutions and thereby increase their liquidity position,
such redeemable equity can be taken into account to reduce notionally the
level of overdues but the benefit of such contribution will be limited to
10% of the demand, even if the actual contribution is more. By this arrange-
ment, the banks will be eligible for a slightly larger program as they can
move into the next higher range.  PLMB whose overdues exceed 60% of the de-
mand can be financed by the KCLMB out of its own resources but mortgages
created by such lendings will be kept separate from the ordinary or special
programs. As a result of these policy instructions, the effects of which are
being felt all over the country, the LMB in Kerala are gearing themselves to
effect a recovery at 75% of demand to enable them to lend without any restric-
tions. At the end of June 1975 only eight of the thirty PLMB in Kerala had
overdues exceeding 25%. KCLMB is hopeful that it will be possible to bring
down the overdue level below 25% in all but one of these cases. PLMB and
KCLMB on their part have organized recovery drives and are even publishing
names of defaulters in local newspapers to induce them to make repayments.
14.       KCLMB has a board of 15 directors, of whom 11 are elected repre-
sentatives of PLMB and 4 are GOK officials including the Registrar of Coop-
erative Societies who is also trustee for the debenture holders. GOK officers
include the Secretary of Agriculture and the Director of Agriculture. The Man-
aging Director is the chief executive and, according to the by-laws, has to be
an officer from the Indian Administrative Service or RBI. The thirty PLMB
are distributed among the 3 regional offices for purposes of sanction of
loans and general coordination. The Cannanore regional office is in charge
of ten PLMB while the Ernakulam regional office has eleven banks under its
charge. The remaining nine are with the Trivandrum regional office. The main
duties of the regional offices are to sanction loans for amounts exceeding
the powers given to the PLMB and to disburse funds (including those sanc-
tioned by them) and participate in recovery work. The work in the regional
office includes scrutiny of loan applications, checking of mortgage bonds
and post scrutiny of loan files in respect of sanctions made by PLMB.
15.       At KCLMB level, out of 95 officers, 9 are senior level executives,
45 middle level officers and 41 junior level officers. Practically all the



ANNEX 12
Appendix
Page 7
senior level and middle level officers are graduates and have undergone train-
ing either in the regular Land Mortgage Bank course in the training centers
of the Committee for Cooperative Training or in the agricultural project
course run in the College of Agricultural Banking, Poona. All newly re-
cruited agricultural officers are given an orientation course by faculty
members of the College. KCLMB has now set up its own training school for
the staff of all PLMB in the state and the first course commenced in August
1976.
Integration of Short-term and Long-term Credit Structures
16.       In pursuance of one of the recommendations made in the ARDC Credit
Project (540-IN), RBI in consultation with the GOI set up a committee in
August 1975 to consider the feasibility of integration of the short-term and
long-term credit structures, since farmers are unable to obtain seasonal and
long-term credit from the same organization. A study made in Kerala has shown
that the problem is rather acute as the long-term structure is much less devel-
oped and investment credit is not therefore readily available. The study
covering two Districts has revealed that the percentage of borrowers availing
of long-term loans in relation to those obtaining short-term loans was as
low as 3% in one District and 7% in the other. This was attributed to lack
of promotional efforts on the part of PLMB and in many cases farmers were
reported to be not even aware of the facilities available for obtaining
investment credit. GOK as well as the Cooperative Banks and LMB in Kerala
are reported to be in favour of integrating the two cooperative credit
structures. They have agreed to bring about a linkage at the village level
to enable the farmer to obtain both production credit and investment credit
from a single contact point, i.e. "one window" approach. PCS will become
village units of the PLMB and receive applications for long-term loans from
farmers. They will make a preliminary scrutiny of the applications and title
deeds and obtain the necessary information from village records. The PLMB
will, thereafter, undertake technical and financial appraisal, value the
property, examine the legal title and sanction the loan. The PLMB will
authorize the PCS to disburse the loan and also effect annually recoveries of
loan installmen'ts. These proposals have yet to be introduced at the field
level.        I
Commercial Banks (CB)
17.       In India there are 86 CB.  The 22 public sector banks including
State Bank of India, its 7 subsidiaries and the 14 nationalized banks handle
the major share of commercial banking in the country. The other 64 CB consist
of foreign bank branches, and smaller commercial banks. They are all subject
to statutory regulation by RBI, and have access to refinance facilities from
RBI, ARDC and Industrial Development Bank of India. CB are comparatively
new to the field of agricultural financing; the real impetus has been after
the introduction of control of banks in 1968 and the nationalization of 14
major banks thereafter. CB were expected to provide funds in an increasing
measure to certain priority sectors which included agriculture. The branch
expansion of CB reflect their new role, the emphasis being on opening of



ANNEX 12
Appendix
Page 8
offices in under-banked or unbanked rural areas (population between 10,000 and
100,000). At the end of June 1976, 14,074 or 66% of the 21,220 offices of
CB were in rural and semi-urban areas. CB provide funds directly for agricul-
ture by way of short-term crop loans and medium term investment credit for
minor irrigation, plantations, dairy, poultry, etc. They also provide funds
indirectly by financing distribution of fertilizers and other inputs, loans
to Electricity Boards, financing farmers through Cooperative Societies etc.
By the end of December 1975, amounts outstanding for agricultural purposes
totalled some Rs 10.2 billion as against Rs 1.9 billion in June 1969. Ninety
percent of the loans were given out by public sector banks. Southern India,
which includes Kerala, claimed 37% of the total advances. Direct advances
as of June 30, 1975 amounted to Rs 5.6 billion and of these 56% was for
medium and long-term loans.
18.       Under the Lead Bank Scheme, each of the CB in the public sector
and a few in the private sector were assigned certain Districts in various
States to survey the potential and draw up development plans. The surveys
have been completed and formulation of credit plans has been undertaken in
some cases. As CB are all-India institutions, their operations in Kerala
State will, by and large, reflect the general trend except for certain
special features peculiar to the State. The State Bank of India and its
subsidiary, the State Bank of Travancore, as well as most of the nationalized
banks have their offices in Kerala besides 14 non-nationalized banks. The
number of offices in Kerala increased from 548 in June 1969 to 1,473 in
June 1976. As many as 177 offices were opened in 1975-76 of which 71 were
in unbanked areas. The population covered per bank office decreased from
19,000 in June 1974 to 14,000 in June 1976. The State Bank of Travancore
holds a predominant position and has the largest number of branches.
19.       The position of recoveries of loans by CB in Kerala has been
improving as may be seen from the figures below:

Loan Procedures
20.       CB also fix scales of finance like PCS while Oranting short-term
loans for agriculture for which a margin of 10% to 25% is maintained. The
same margin is prescribed for medium-term loans also. Rates of interest
vary from 12-1/2% to 15% and are generally higher than those charged by
Cooperatives. The Union Bank was charging a lower rate of 10% on loans
to farmers with holdings less than 3 ha. CB Branch Managers are given powers
for sanction of loans for amounts varying from Rs 500 to Rs 10,000 depending
on various considerations including the size of the branch. Crop loans do not
require a mortgage, and loans for pumpsets and other agricultural equipment
loans are made against hypothecation of the relevant assets. For agricultural
financing, CB have a number of field officers. Union Bank had 24 such officers,
all of whom were agricultural graduates. The Syndicate Bank has farm represen-
tatives who are agricultural graduates, one for every 10 to 15 branches.
Apart from the comparatively higher qualified and better paid staff employed
by CB, there is now a general air of confidence in these banks in handling
agricultural financing which was not in evidence up to three years ago.

INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Marketing
Existing Marketing Channels
1.        Agricultural marketing assumes special importance in Kerala since a
high portion of agricultural production is sold. Marketing channels for most
of the project commodities are already well established in Kerala. The
traditional marketing channels are the village fairs, village merchants,
processors, brokers, agents, exporters and industry. A newer development is
the entry of Cooperative Societies, State-sponsored Cooperative Federations,
and government Corporations and Boards into the marketing of agricultural
products.
2.        There are more than 2,000 markets in Kerala holding daily, biweekly
and weekly fairs for disposal of agricultural produce. However, the major por-
tion of the State's production is sold in the villages themselves as will be
evident from the following Table:
Percentage of Quantities Marketed
Through
Crops                 In Markets   In Villages   Cooperatives
Coconut                 17.6           81.1            1.4
Tapioca                 50.4           49.6             -
Arecanut                42.2           56.1            1.7
Pepper                  60.1           34.7            5.2
Ginger                   9.5           90.5             -
Cashew Nut              34.8           65.2             -
Source: Kerala Department of Agriculture
There are about 100 Primary Marketing Societies in Kerala. These Societies
are all members of the apex marketing federations, the Kerala State Cooperative
Marketing Federation and the Kerala State Rubber Marketing Federation. Al-
though the share of trade handled by the Cooperatives has been minimal in the
past, these institutions are expected to play a major role in the future with
the encouragement given to them by GOK and the Cooperative credit institutions.
Other government sponsored institutions that would participate in the market-
ing of project produce are:



ANNEX 13

Kerala State Coconut Development Corporation;
Kerala State Plantations Corporation;
Kerala State Cashew Development Corporation;
State Trading Corporation of India;
Cashew Export Promotion Council; and
Spices Export Promotion Council.
Markets Prospects and Prices
3.        Coconut.  With an annual production of 6 billion nuts, India ranks
third among the major producers following Philippines and Indonesia. Unlike
these countries, India is not an exporter and the problem is one of satisfying
the growing domestic demand. India is a net importer of oils and fats and
coconut oil is the third most important edible oil consumed in the country.
Due to import restrictions, India's consumption of coconut (fresh nuts and its
derivates) is marginally above its production. It is estimated that 50%
3 billion nuts) is consumed as raw nuts and the balance converted into copra,
20% of which goes for edible purposes leaving only the remaining part for
milling.
4.        The incremental output from the project at full development in
year 15 is forecast to be 205 million nuts (18,000 tons coconut oil equiv-
alent), representing an increase of 5% over Kerala's current annual production,
and 3.5% over the national production. No difficulty is expected in absorbing
this quantity. Coconut marketing would be handled by existing channels, which
are satisfactory.
5.        The price of nuts is generally determined by the wholesale price
of coconut oil. Coconut oil prices are subject to fluctuations caused by
the overall availability of fats and oils in the country. Domestic prices
for copra and coconut oil have always been higher than those prevailing on
international markets, because of import restrictions. A comparison of world
prices for coconut oil with Kerala prices is given below for the five last
years:
Coconut Oil
Years                 1970/73       1974        1975

ANNEX 13

Past prices of coconut and its products are given in Table 4. Numerous
studies of coconut prices carried out by GOK and the Directorate of Coconut
Development have concluded that, based on the projected fats and oil situation
in the country, the price of coconut oil would not drop below Rs 800 per
quintal. On this basis the projected farmgate price of coconut has been
taken at Rs 700 per 1,000 nuts. Future world prices of nuts are given
in para 1 of Appendix.
6.        Pepper.  Three-fourth of world pepper supplies originate in three
Asian countries - India, Malaysia and Indonesia. India is the largest pro-
ducer with 25 to 30% of the world market. Annual production in India averaged
37,000 tons over the period 1972-74, some 70% of which is exported. The USSR
and several other centrally planned countries, the US and Canada are the
largest customers. The market outlook for Indian pepper is considered favor-
able because of increased exports to the East European countries and prospects
of larger purchases by US and also because of a potentially growing domestic
demand. Internal demand, estimated to be 10,000 to 12,000 tons annually, is
restricted by the relatively high prices. Per capita consumption is low at
20 grams per year as compared to about 100 grams in the US and 50 grams in
the USSR.
7.        No study is available that would give the longer term outlook for
the pepper economy. It is known to depend largely on production developments
in Indonesia where disease of the pepper vine has seriously affected the crop.
Some rehabilitation work has taken place but there is no sign of a major
expansion program. Malaysia has centralized its pepper trade but has no
plans to increase production. Brazil has become the fourth largest producer
exporting some 15,000 tons but it is not expected to make a major expan-
sion in exports. Despite some area increase in India, pepper production has
remained stable as a result of decreasing yields. This project in Kerala,
which accounts for 95% of Indian export, would be India's only large expan-
sion program. By 1990 the world pepper demand will be around 160,000 tons if
the average growth rate of 3% over the period 1965-1975 is maintained, a total
increase of around 55,000 tons over the 1975 level. The pepper output due to
the rehabilitation work undertaken in this project would yield a maximum of
6,000 tons at full development by 1990, an estimated 3% of world production
at that time. Domestic demand increase would be 4,000 tons in 1990 under the
assumption that the already low per capita consumption is just maintained.
8.        The pepper market in the sixties was characterized by frequent price
fluctuations. After initial drops, prices began to recover from 1964-65 on-
wards. Since then Cochin prices for garbled pepper have averaged Rs 10,000/
ton in 1976 constant terms although prices have been above Rs 12,000 since
1974. In 1976, New York CIF price for Indian pepper was US$0.81/lb in January
and US$1.00 in August, equivalent to Cochin prices of about Rs 16,000 and
20,000/ton. Recent prices have been abnormally high. For the purpose of
financial calculations a farmgate price of Rs 10,000/ton was used (equivalent
to a Cochin quotation of Rs 12,000).



ANNEX 13
Page 4
9.        Cashew.  India is recognized as the world's leading producer of high
quality whole cashew kernels for use as dessert nuts. Exports, which reached
65,000 tons in 1972-73 have been decreasing due to a sharp decline in the avail-
ability of imported raw nuts. India's cashew processing industry, heavily
labor intensive, is now running much below capacity. Until 1974/75 India
received excellent prices in the US for high quality kernels, while selling
the bulk of standard grade nuts to USSR and Eastern Europe. World production
is likely to lag behind demand and prices can therefore be expected to increase.
Prices of kernels CIF New York over the past years have been increasing from
US 60 cents/lb in 1967 to US$1.30 in January-October 1976, though with wide
fluctuations. In view of a sustained demand in the overseas market, and of
the general shortage of raw nuts, prices of indigenous raw nuts can be expect-
ed to keep rising. Current farmgate price Rs 3/kg of raw nut was used in the
farm models ignoring possible increases. Incremental project output is
marginal with 6,000 tons or 4% of the country's production.
10.       Cocoa.  When in full bearing, the annual crop from the present area
is expected to be 4,500 tons of dry beans. Production from the project
plantations would add another 2,000 tons at full development. A study con-
ducted by the National Commission on Agriculture estimated the country's
requirement at 46,000 tons. Incremental production would therefore help
toward meeting a fraction of the constrained domestic demand.
11.       While a certain amount of fermentation is carried out by producers
in their own plantings, in the interest of quality the larger manufacturers
offer to purchase wet beans at 40% of the dry price for central processing.
This arrangement is considered satisfactory under present conditions but
there is no reason why smallholders would not carry out this simple process
themselves provided, however, sufficient wet beans were available to ensure
an adequate fermentation; 1 ha of cocoa would be the minimum area required.
According to Bank forecasts, cocoa prices (CIF New York) are expected to
decline from the present US$0.80/lb to US$0.61 in 1980 and US$0.51 in 1985.
After making necessary adjustments for shipping and handling costs, cocoa
prices CIF Bombay would be Rs 12,000/ton in 1980, corresponding to farmgate
price of about Rs 10,000/ton. 1980 price forecast was used because local
prices are bound to be higher than international prices on account of import
restrictions.
12.       Intercrops.  All intercrops except perhaps ginger would be consumed
in the State itself (Annex 1, paras 18 through 21). Ginger, however, would
be partly exported. The incremental production of dry ginger from the project
is expected to be a maximum of 3,300 tons by 1986. This output would repre-
sent an increase of 6% on India's 1974-75 production. However, the ginger
crop is an alternative to tapioca and vegetables and the intercrop mix would
be altered by the farmer depending on product demand and relative profitabil-
ity of the different crops. Calicut and Cochin varieties which have a pre-
ference in the world market, would be grown in the project area. In addition
to ginger minor quantities of oleoresin of ginger oil are exported from India.
Importing countries are Saudi Arabia, USA and Morocco for dry ginger, Japan
for oleoresin, and France, Japan and Sri Lanka for ginger oil.



ANNEX 13
Page 5
13.       Milk.  Annual incremental production anticipated from the project
areas is 15,000 tons compared with the Kerala's current production estimated
at 440,000 tons. The per capita daily milk availability in the State is about
52 grams compared with 210 grams recommended by WHO as minimum requirement.
At present there are six pasteurization plants and twelve milk chilling centers
operating in Kerala. In each of the project area Districts there are more
than fifty organized Primary Milk Supply Unions. In 1975, the existing dairy
plants handled about 11,000 tons, utilizing only 50% of the installed capa-
city. The major constraint is supply of milk to the plants.
14.       Fifth Five Year Plan provision for dairy development in the State
is Rs 30 M over the next five years. In addition, other special programs are
being planned by GOK. Another special program is "Operation Flood". Operation
flood aims at the improvement of milk marketing in the country through speeding
up dairy development by increasing milk procurement and production in rural
areas through farmers' organizations. Operation Flood Phase II commenced
in 1976/77 and about Rs 46 M has been alloted to Kerala for utilization in
the Quilon and Cannanore SFDA districts.
15.       A detailed survey of Trivandrum, and two other Districts outside the
project area is being carried out by the National Dairy Development Board
to assess organization structure for milk marketing and dairy development.
The Board also has started, in the three project area Districts in the north,
a survey of the availability of milk and development of milk production. On
completion of these surveys by end of 1976, a detailed scheme for the creation
of necessary infrastructure and processing facilities for marketing of milk
produced in all four Districts would be finalized by the newly created Live-
stock Development and Milk Marketing Board. The resultant investment program
is expected to be completed before any milk production from proposed project
becomes available from the project for marketing.
16.       The Livestock Development and Milk Marketing Board started
functioning in 1975. It took over all dairy plants and chilling centers
which belonged to the Government. Among other functions (see Annex 1,
para 30), it assists Primary Cooperative Societies in milk marketing.
These Societies bring the milk to a collection center from where milk is
transported in Board's vehicles to the pasteurization plants or chilling
centers. The Board fixes the price it pays for milk, taking into account
farmers' production costs. In natural grassland areas like Iddiki Districts
the price paid to the farmer is as low as Rs 1.40/liter which help cover
higher transport costs. In the other areas closer to the plants, the far-
mer receives Rs 1.60 and the Primary Society Rs 1.75. Farm gate prices
used in financial projection is Rs 1.60/liter. In order to encourage
production of quality milk, the Board proposes to introduce a change in
price policy for which a pre-requisite is the supply of milk testing equip-
ment at the collection centers. Initially, three to four hundred Primary
Societies in Trivandrum District will be equipped for such testing. For
these quality control activities, the Board has received Rs 1 M from the
Swiss Government. Swiss Government funds are also provided for milk procure-
ment, processing and marketing (Rs 3.7 M) and for meat marketing (Rs 2 M).



ANNEX 13
Page 6
17.       Rubber.  Although India's natural rubber production has increased
more than three-fold during the last ten years, its current production of
130,000 tons is yet less than 5% of total world production. India is a
net importer of rubber. It has, however, not imported natural rubber since
1970. 1/ Small quantities were exported in 1973/74 (1,450 tons) and in
1975/76 (3,500 tons) to ease local price fluctuations caused by temporary
surplus. The long-term picture is that the country's demand for natural
rubber would be far in excess of domestic supply as would be evident from
the Indian Rubber Board projections given below:
Projected Demand
1975    1980    1985    1990
('000 tons)
High                   161      260     415      670
Most likely            161      240     360      545
Low                    161      225     315      440
Projected Supply
1980     1985    1990
('000 tons)
Natural Rubber             174      205      250
Synthetic Rubber           50        80      100
Total              224       285     350
18.       Export of manufactured rubber goods (mostly tires and tubes), cur-
rently Rs 60 M, is targetted to double during the Fifth Five Year Plan period.
Local demand for natural rubber is concentrated in thirteen large companies
which consume 70% of the country's production. The supply, however, is widely
diffused because of the predominance of smallholders in the subsector. The
investments proposed under this project would not directly contribute to
any quantity increase. It would, at full capacity of the ten factories,
help to upgrade annually some 25,000 tons of rubber that would otherwise be
sold by the smallholders as lower priced grades. The crumb rubber produced by
the Cooperatives would be marketed as technically specified rubber and, as far
as practical, channelled through the Kerala State Rubber Marketing Federation.
Warehousing and transport facilities provided in the project for the Federation
would also strengthen its ability to counter the monopolistic position now
occupied by the industrial consumers and obtain better prices for the small-
holders' rubber.
19.       Crumb rubber is in high demand in the tire industry for its
cheaper price (in relation to synthetic rubber) and its standard quality.
Prices used in financial projections are those forecast by the Indian
Rubber Board which are realistic:
I/   Synthetic rubber is imported.

ANNEX 13
Appendix
Page 1
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Commodity Prices Used in Economic Analysis
1.        Coconut.  Consumption of coconut oil in India is artificially con-
strained by import restrictions and limitations on its use in industry. The
incremental production from the project has therefore been assumed to be for
import substitution, and oil valued in accordance with Bank price projections
(made for Indonesian coconut oil in bulk CIF Europe). CIF Bombay prices were
arrived at by making adjustments for shipping costs. Economic prices of
coconuts were derived from CIF Bombay price after necessary adjustments
for transport and processing costs made on the basis of 142 gm of copra per
nut and a milling rate of 63%.
Economic Price of Coconut
(Rs per 1,000 nuts)
1976     1977      1978      1979     1980      1985
250      280       310       350      390       450
Additional returns from the husk (Rs 35 per 1,000) and from the shell (Rs 17
per 1,000) were also assumed. Full value of husking was taken as a benefit
since labor generated is shadow priced 0.
2.        Cocoa production would also be for import substitution.  Bank's
forecast for cocoa beans (Accra, CIF New York) is US$0.61 in 1980 and US$0.51
in 1985. CIF Bombay equivalent would be Rs 12/kg in 1980 and Rs 10/ kg in
1985. After allowing for local transport and handling costs at Rs 0.50/kg,
the economic price for cocoa beans (equivalent dry) was taken at Rs 11.50/kg
and Rs 9.50/kg.
3.        Pepper.  No price forecast has been made by Bank for pepper.
The basic price used for economic and financial calculations is discussed
in para 7 of this Annex. It is based on export price of Indian pepper - FOB
Cochin of Rs 12.0/kg. After allowing for internal transport and handling
costs (Rs 0.50/kg), processing costs (Rs 0.10/kg) and using a conversion
factor garbled/ungarbled pepper of 95%, the economic price was taken at
Rs 10.90/kg.



ANNEX 13
Appendix
Page 2
4.        Cashew.  An export price from India of at least Rs 11 per lb has
been projected for cashew kernels in 1978/79 1/. Prices are expected to
remain firm in the longer term. After allowing for internal transport and
handling costs (Rs 0.50/kg), processing costs of (Rs 1.50/kg of raw nuts),
and using a conversion factor kernel/raw nuts of 25%, price of cashew raw nut
was projected at Rs 4.40/kg.
5.        Milk.  The economic price used for milk is Rs 1.60 per liter - the
price fixed by the Kerala Livestock Development and Milk Marketing Board for
milk collected in the project areas. The retail price suggested by the
Board varies between Rs 1.90 and Rs 2.00 depending on location of outlets. It
is known, however, that consumers in most places often pay Rs 2.50 per liter.
The price fixed by the Board should therefore be considered below free market
price. By using Rs 1.60 as a price for milk in the calculations an apparent
consumer surplus has been ignored.
6.        Intercrops.  Incremental production of other crops, namely tapioca,
pulses, vegetables and ginger were valued at current farmgate price since
most of these are used for local consumption.
1/   Report on Export Prospects, April 1976, India Division.

KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Financial Projections
Costs and Benefits
1.        Investment costs and operating results relating to smallholder
farm models are shown in Annex 3, Tables. Costs and results of the cashew
development by KSPC and seed garden development by SADU are shown in the
Tables in Annex 4 and 5 respectively. Crumb rubber factory costs and
results are in Tables 1 and 2 of Annex 7.
Farm Models
2.        Models for coconut development without irrigation are based on
0.5 ha holdings. The pepper model is based on 1 ha holding, and so are models
for coconut development under irrigation with fodder and cocoa intercropping
(Annex 3, Tables). Tables for irrigated development are prepared to demon-
strate that it would normally not be financially viable to invest in a new
dugwell and a 3.0 hp pumpset for holdings less than 1 ha. A farmer's invest-
ment plan including a new well would generally not be approved nor his loan
granted for holdings of less than 1 ha. Farmer investment plan including
installation of filter points or improvement of existing wells with 1.5 hp
pumpsets would be for holding not less than 0.5 ha.
Rates of Return
3.        The financial rates of return for the various types of project
development are as follows:
ROR
Coconut (Smallholders)
New Planting:
- without irrigation             49
- with irrigation                27
Rehabilitation:
- without irrigation             40
- with irrigation                 18



ANNEX 14
Page 2
ROR
Pepper (Smallholders)
Rehabilitation                     23
Cashew (KSPC)                           22
Seed Gardens (SADU)                     13
Rubber Processing (CRMS/IKRMF)          34
Cash Flow Statements
4.        The cash flow statements for each type of development based on the
models in Annexes 3, 4, 5 and 7 are given in the accompanying Tables. Major
assumptions used in the cash flow calculations are given in the Appendix. It
will be seen from these cash flow statements that the different types of
development planned under this project would be financially viable.
5.        Maximum loans required from participating farmers as well as income
after debt service would vary widely depending on the type of development
chosen:
Income After
Maximum Loan   Loan Repayment
Rs/ha            Rs/ha
Coconut New Planting:
- without irrigation         7,600             7,570
- with irrigation           14,500            10,300
Coconut Rehabilitation:
- without irrigation         6,400             5,400
- with irrigation           19,800            10,300
(cocoa as intercrop)
- with irrigation           22,500             9,300
(fodder and dairy)
Pepper Rehabilitation        9,300             5,800
The loans taken by project participants to finance these investments
could be repaid in full between eleven and fifteen years from the time of
commencing the development. Farmers would be able to improve their con-
sumption levels during the debt service period (see cash flow statements
Table 1 through 6). The financial situation of the farmer would, in
most cases, be more favorable than that reflected in the Cash Flow State-
ments. In these projections it is assumed that the farmer finances 100%



ANNEX 14
Page 3
of the investment with interest bearing loans. In practice, however, he
would borrow 95 to 85%. Thus the interest payments need not be so high;
loan repayments could be completed earlier or the improvement in consump-
tion level could be better than that apparent from the cash flow state-
ments.
6.        In the case of irrigated development, particularly coconut re-
habilitation with cocoa and fodder intercropping (see Tables 4 and 5 which
picture the least favorable situation 1/), only 10 to 25% increases in
consumption levels are allowed during the debt service period of 13 to 15
years. Even then investments in irrigated development would have special
attractions to the farmer on more than one count:
-    farmer's equity built during the 13 to 15 years period
is high between Rs 18,000 and Rs 25,000;
-    the debt service periods would be less than those reflected
in Tables 2, 4 and 5 in all cases where the farmers' hold-
ings are more than the minimum 1 ha;
-    the cost of well (Rs 8,000) and pumpset (Rs 3,550),
included in the investment would be considered by
most Kerala farmers to be well spent even without
such agricultural development because of the need
to have water for drinking, bathing and washing
purposes; and
-    many farmers would find it possible to sell water to
their neighbors for agricultural as well as domestic
uses and no account has been taken of this potential
source of income.
1/   High irrigation investment cost of Rs 11,550 per ha compared
with an overall project average of Rs 7,000 per ha. See Annex 3,
Appendix 2.

Appendix
INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Notes on Financial Statements
1.        Financial year for all participating agencies would be April/March.
2.        Project years are assumed to correspond to Financial Years and year
dne taken as April 1, 1977 to March 31, 1978.
3.        Physical contingencies are included at 10% flat on base capital
costs of Rubber Processing, Cashew and Seed Garden components.
4.        Price contingencies included in computing project costs have been
excluded in rate of return calculations and in cash flow statements since
operating income has been taken at 1976 values.
5.        Prices used to evaluate incremental production are given in Annex 13,
Table 1.
6.        Daily labor rates are taken at Rs 8.00 for men and Rs 6.50 for
women - those specified in the State minimum wage legislation.
7.        Foreign exchange conversions are made at Rs 9.00 to US$1.00.
8.        Interest paid by ultimate borrowers is taken at 12% for short
term and 11% for long-term loans.
9.        Interest unpaid in any year is capitalized and the interest cal-
culations are at compound rates.
10.       Income tax has been assumed to be not applicable to any of the
participants except the Kerala State Plantation Corporation. The model farmer
would not, by and large, reach taxable income levels. Income of cooperative
institutions which distribute dividends based on purchases or sales turnover
are not taxable. KSPC's tax is estimated at 50% of taxable income.
II.       Rate of return calculations are based on 25 year stream of costs and
benefits.
12.       Base costs are taken at prices ruling at September 1976.
13.       In all cases, the full amounts of the investments costs are assumed
to be borrowed by the participants from banks as long-term loans.

ANNEX 15

INDIA
KERALA AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Analysis
Malor Benefits
1.        Some 65,000 smallholders in coconut areas and 10,000 in pepper
areas would directly participate and benefit from the project. More than
50,000 small rubber growers who would sell at least a proportion of their
rubber production to the crumb rubber factories would also benefit. A com-
parison of farmers' income level in pre- and post project situations is given
in Table 3. Coconut Rehabilitation without irrigation would generate a 100%
increase in the income of about 45,000 families of the lowest income group.
This is the group whose annual farm income presently is about Rs 1,500 (US$160)
derived from 0.5 ha and often supplemented by activities like fishing and coir
making or with money sent from relatives who have migrated out of the State.
Coconut rehabilitation under irrigation with intensive intercropping would
yield more spectacular results. Largest increase would be obtained from 1.0
ha coconut holdings with cocoa as major intercrop, with farmers' income rising
from about Rs 2,900 (US$320) to Rs 10,300 (US$1,150). Dairy in 1.0 ha of
irrigated coconut holding would give Rs 9,300 (US$1,050), an incremental in-
come of Rs 6,400. Newly settled families estimated to be 9,000 would benefit
from the coconut new planting development on the 5,000 ha. Their income
would increase to Rs 3,800 (US$420) from 0.5 ha unirrigated plots, and to
Rs 10,300 (US$1,150) from 1.0 ha irrigated plots. Income of 10,000 pepper
farmers would increase threefold to Rs 5,800 (US$650). Debt service continues
until year 11 for pepper growers, and 13 to 15 years for coconut growers.
Incentive has therefore been built in farm models' by allowing improvement
of participants' consumption levels for all farmers. The 50,000 rubber
smallholders would benefit from higher prices realized for their scrap rubber
and, to a lesser extent, on their premium grades.
2.        Project production figures are summarized in Annex 13, Table 2
Increased output would be the result mainly of intensified and improved agri-
cultural practices since only 5,000 ha of additional land would be brought
under cultivation (coconut new plantings). Better yields would be obtained
not only from commercial crops and but also from garden crops. This increase
in productivity is of great significance to Kerala where land, relative to
other Indian States, is scarce. The per capita availability of cultivated
land is 0.11 ha and there is very little new land which could be brought under
cultivation. The many intercrops and the cattle raised under coconut, would
help improve nutrition standards of the participants' households as well as of
rural families in the neighborhood. Increments of coconut and cocoa produc-
tion would allow for foreign exchange savings, while pepper and cashew



ANNEX 15
Page 2
could bring significant foreign earnings. At full development (by 1990)
annual foreign exchange earnings from pepper and cashew would be Rs 110 H1,
and foreign exchange savings from coconut oil and cocoa Rs 125 M. Although
cashew would contribute to additional foreign exchange earnings, the prin-
cipal aim of the cashew development component is to demonstrate the feasi-
bility of applying intensive and improved practices in cashew cultivation.
This would contribute to GOK's long-term objective of increasing cashew pro-
duction to secure employment for the 125,000 people now working on the nume-
rous cashew processing units in the State. In addition, the economics of
introducing cashew apple processing would be studied. If the feasibility were
proved, it would be a breakthrough to utilize almost a million tons of cashew
apple which is now largely wasted.
3.        The project would also create additional employment of about 50,000
man-years during the development period, and 13,000 man-years thereafter annu-
ally for better management of the plantations, intensified intercropping, and
for harvesting. The main effect would not be creation of new jobs but rather
better utilization of the agricultural work force, since underemployment on
the farm is high. Employment would also be generated in the labor-intensive
industries which consume the raw materials provided by these crops, namely oil
milling, coir and cashew processing.
Additional Benefits
4.        Other benefits would arise from:
(a) institutional improvements through strengthened extension
services and by building project implementation capability
within the many institutions that would participate in the
project: GOK agencies, KSPC, IRB, CRMS, KRMF and the agri-
cultural credit institutions;
(b) provision of high yielding and disease resistant planting
materials for coconut, cashew, cocoa, pepper, and other
spices from the seed gardens to be established under the
project;
(c) strengthening research activities of CPCRI and AUK in the
field of wilt diseases of coconut and pepper which are of
major concern to GOK since a third of the coconut plantations
and pepper areas are affected;
(d) the demonstration effect that improved cultivation practices,
followed by project participants, would have on the surround-
ing smallholder farmers;
(e) probable project replication because the proportion of
rehabilitated coconut, cashew and pepper area is small



ANNEX 15
Page 3
(less than 5%) in relation to the total area under these
crops to be rehabilitated in the State as well as in the
two neighboring States of Karnataka and Tamil Nadu where
tree crops are also of major importance;
(f) provision of electric and water facilities to about
9,000 households; and
(g) increased milk production adequate to provide 75,000
people with the minimum recommended of 210 gm per day.
Methodologs
6.        Cost and benefit streams are considered for a period of 25 years;
no attempt was made to estimate the residual value of capital assets. Most
tree crops would still be producing after year 25 but with decreasing yields.
The crumb rubber factories with proper maintenance costs of machinery should
continue to be productive during the entire period. All replacement of equip-
ment and livestock considered necessary has been included.
7.        Costs.  1976 prices have been used for all items of costs.
Physical contingencies of 5% and 10% have been added to all project costs ex-
cept those relating to smallholders' on-farm development. Exchange rate used
in the computations is Rs 10.50 to 1 US dollar (rather than the ruling rate of
Rs 9.00) to reflect the relative scarcity of foreign exchange. Input and
equipment prices used reflect the elimination of all known tariffs and sub-
sidies. (The only subsidy involved is that on phosphates - at the rate of
Rs 1,250 per ton of P205). Hired labor has been costed at Rs 8.00 per day for
men and Rs 6.50 per day for women which rates are payable under the State's
minimum wages legislation. Family labor, however, is shadow priced zero;
there is heavy underemployment on the farm and it is inconceivable that
employment would be available to family labor outside the farmer's own land-
holdings. 1/
8.        Benefits.  Project benefits from agricultural development are taken
on the value of incremental production of coconut, cocoa, pepper, cashew, milk
and various intercrops. Commodity prices used are discussed in the Appendix
of Annex 13. No attempt was made to value the economic benefits arising from
seed gardens or research components, as these would be difficult to quantify.2/.
As a result of the rubber processing component, a readily useable product
1/   Incremental consumption by project participants who would be enjoying
better incomes has not been taken as cost; however this would be off-
set by the overestimated economic cost of hired labor since minimum
wages are not always applied.
2/   Costs of these components also have been excluded.



ANNEX 15
Page 4
(crumb rubber) would be made available to the tire industry, which would lower
their processing costs. This reflects into the price offered for crumb rubber
and consequently into a higher value obtained per unit of scrap incorporated
into crumb rubber, of about 20% over unprocessed scrap. This increased value
was included in the benefits.
Economic Rate of Return (ROR)
9.        Based on these assumptions ROR for the project would be 30% (Table
2). Calculations were also made of ROR for the various subprojects and are
tabulated below:
ROR
Coconut New Plantin                              %
-    without irrigation                         47
-    with irrigation                            25
Coconut Rehabilitation
-    without irrigation                         34
-    with irrigation                            15
Pepper Rehabilitation                           27
Cashew Development                              39
Rubber Processig                                68
10.       The sensitivity oL the above rates to variations in costs and
benefits has been tested and the results are given in Table 1. Overall ROR
drops to 26% if current exchange rate is used. Sensitivity of ROR for small-
holder farm development was also tested: if current exchange rate is used
ROR drops from 24% to 17%; if family labor is valued at half the minimum wage
and hired labor costed full, ROR would drop to 19%. Project ROR was also
tested for slower rate of implementation; one year delay in benefits would
reduce ROR from 30% to 23%.
II.       Since very little is known about the long term price trend for pepper,
a special analysis was made to ensure that the pepper component remains eco-
nomically justified even if project estimate of market trends turned out to
be too optimistic. The concern centered around the low price elasticity of
demand for pepper in the world market (estimated at 0.5), India's large mar-
ket share (about 25% of world production), the relatively large addition to
world exports this project would bring about (an estimated 3% of total exports
by 1990 1/) and, consequently, the possibility of a price fall that would
affect not only the additional production but India's current sales as well.
1/   Under the assumption that 3,000 tons would be absorbed by the domestic
market. This is a low estimate (para 7, Annex 13).



ANNEX 15
Page 5
A sensitivity test was therefore carried out with possible lower prices. It
was found that a ROR of 12% could be achieved even if pepper prices fell to
Rs 8.75/kg (such drop would result i- a marginal revenue of Rs 7.00/kg);
a price of Rs 8. 75/kg is less than half cif today's prices and at least 10%
lower than those during the last ter years (in 1976 constant terms - see
Table below). It is therefore reasonable to expect world prices to remain
above this level. It is worth noting that a price fall of this magnitude
would increase sales within India, where the price elasticity is higher than
in developed countries, to the extent that very little if any of the addi-
tional production would be exported.
